The Notch signaling pathway, previously known for maintaining undifferentiated germline stem cells/progenitors in Caenorhabditis elegans, has been found to promote reprogramming of germ cells into somatic cells. This unexpected role of Notch signaling was discovered through a combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcriptome analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes. The study reveals that Notch signaling activates specific genes silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 ( PRC2 ), and identifies the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial Notch target facilitating reprogramming. The findings suggest that Notch signaling antagonizes PRC2-mediated silencing to promote reprogramming of germ cells into somatic cells. This novel role of Notch signaling has implications for our understanding of development and diseases associated with abnormal Notch signaling. In the C. elegans germline, GLP-1/Notch signaling is essential for maintaining a pool of undifferentiated stem cells/progenitors. However, this study shows that increased GLP-1/Notch signaling promotes cell fate reprogramming by activating genes that are normally silenced by PRC2. The X chromosome bias among Notch-activated genes suggests an antagonistic relationship between GLP-1/Notch and PRC2. The C. elegans PRC2 consists of MES-2, -3, and -6, and levels of the corresponding transcripts were not altered by GLP-1/Notch signaling. The study identifies genes regulated by GLP-1/Notch, including genes that maintain germline stem/progenitor cells, and shows that many genes activated by GLP-1/Notch signaling are also repressed by PRC2. The findings demonstrate an antagonistic effect of GLP-1/Notch and PRC2 on germ cell-fate decisions and co-regulation of their common target, UTX-1. This study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms promoting or inhibiting reprogramming in intact organisms and has implications for our understanding of development and diseases associated with abnormal Notch signaling.
The study investigated the role of the adaptor protein SKAP2 in host defense against Klebsiella pneumoniae (K. pneumoniae) infection. K. pneumoniae is a pathogen that causes respiratory, blood, liver, and bladder infections. The research found that SKAP2 is essential for protection against K. pneumoniae pulmonary infections in mice. Mice lacking SKAP2 (Skap2-/-) had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice. The bacterial burden was controlled by SKAP2 expression in innate immune cells, such as neutrophils and monocytes. Neutrophils from Skap2-/- mice were present in infected lungs and degranulated normally in response to K. pneumoniae infection. However, K. pneumoniae-stimulated reactive oxygen species (ROS) production in vitro was abolished in Skap2-/- neutrophils. The study also found that K. pneumoniae-induced ROS response required the activity of several proteins, including Src family kinases (SFKs), Syk, Btk, PLCγ2, and PKC. The loss of SKAP2 significantly hindered the K. pneumoniae-induced phosphorylation of SFKs, Syk, and Pyk2, suggesting that SKAP2 is proximal to their activation in pathogen-signaling pathways. The study concluded that SKAP2-dependent signaling in neutrophils is essential for K. pneumoniae-activated ROS production and for promoting bacterial clearance during infection. The results suggest that SKAP2 plays a critical role in host defense against K. pneumoniae infection and that ROS production is an important mechanism for protection against this pathogen. The study also found that K. pneumoniae is resistant to neutrophil phagocytosis regardless of SKAP2 expression, but can elicit SKAP2-independent degranulation during infection. Mice lacking the essential gp91phox component of NADPH oxidase (Cybb-/-) were significantly more susceptible to K. pneumoniae infection, further supporting the importance of ROS in host defense against this pathogen.
A mathematical model of Plasmodium vivax transmission has been developed to investigate the contribution of the hypnozoite reservoir to the parasite's life cycle. The hypnozoite reservoir refers to the latent liver-stage parasites that can reactivate weeks to months after the initial infection, causing new blood-stage infections. A within-host model of liver-stage hypnozoites was created and validated against data from tropical strains of P. vivax. The model demonstrated that the hypnozoite reservoir is over-dispersed, with many individuals having few or no hypnozoites and some having intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypnozoites were predicted to experience more relapses and contribute more to onwards P. vivax transmission.

The model was then embedded in a P. vivax transmission model to demonstrate the build-up of the hypnozoite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypnozoite activation and death. The results showed that incorporating hypnozoite killing drugs, such as primaquine, into first-line treatment regimens could cause substantial reductions in P. vivax transmission. This is because individuals with the most hypnozoites are more likely to relapse and be targeted for treatment.

Relapse infections arising from the activation of hypnozoites in the human liver have important consequences for the transmission dynamics of P. vivax. The hypnozoite reservoir constitutes a third malaria parasite reservoir, in addition to the reservoirs in the blood circulation and mosquito present for P. falciparum. Most existing models of malaria transmission do not account for the additional reservoir of parasites in the liver, but the hypnozoite reservoir has been incorporated into some P. vivax transmission models as a state to denote hypnozoite infection or as up to two broods of hypnozoites.

Mathematical models of malaria transmission that account for P. vivax relapses can provide valuable insights into the impact of malaria control interventions on the parasite's reservoirs in the vector, the blood, and the liver. The within-host model of hypnozoites in liver hepatocytes demonstrated that many of the epidemiological patterns of relapse infections can be explained by making the assumption that hypnozoites activate and die at a constant rate. This model was integrated into the existing theory of Ross-Macdonald models to account for the relapse infections characteristic of P. vivax malaria.
This study investigates the dynamics of carbon (C) recovery in Amazonian forests following selective logging, a common human disturbance in tropical forests. Selective logging results in the emission of over 90 Tg of C to the atmosphere each year, which is then counterbalanced by forest regrowth. To understand the regional differences in C recovery, the authors developed an original modeling approach using data from 133 permanent forest plots (175 ha total) across Amazonia. The results show that net aboveground C recovery over 10 years is higher in the Guiana Shield and the west (21 ± 3 Mg C ha-1) than in the south (12 ± 3 Mg C ha-1), where environmental stress is high due to low rainfall and high seasonality. The study highlights the key role of survivors in forest regrowth and provides a comprehensive map of post-disturbance C recovery potential in Amazonia.

The authors partitioned the contributions to post-disturbance aboveground carbon stock (ACS) gain and loss from growth and recruitment of trees ≥20 cm DBH and mortality of survivors and recruited trees to identify the main drivers and patterns of ACS recovery in forests disturbed by selective logging across Amazonia. The study used long-term (8-30 year) inventory data from 13 experimentally-disturbed sites in the Amazon Basin and the Guiana Shield. The sites met specific criteria, including location in tropical forests with mean annual precipitation above 1000 mm, a total censused area above 1 ha, at least one pre-logging census, and at least two post-logging censuses.

The study focused on selective logging, a type of disturbance with significant economic value and implications for forest management. The results provide clues on the regional differences in Amazonian forests' response to large ACS losses induced by other disturbances. The authors up-scaled their results to Amazonia (sensu lato) using global maps of ACS, climatic conditions, and soil bulk density to elaborate predictive maps of potential ACS changes over 10 years under the hypothesis of a 40% ACS loss, a common disturbance intensity after conventional logging in Amazonia.

The study highlights the importance of understanding the impacts of human activities on carbon cycles, particularly the 2.1 ± 1.1 Pg C yr-1 of C loss caused by various forms and intensities of anthropogenic disturbances in tropical forests. The results of this study provide valuable insights into the dynamics of C recovery in Amazonian forests following selective logging and contribute to the development of predictive maps of post-disturbance C recovery potential in Amazonia.
The study investigated the relationship between neurosphere-initiating cells (NICs) and neural stem cells (NSCs) in the adult mouse forebrain, specifically in the subventricular zone (SVZ) of the lateral ventricle. The researchers used flow cytometry to prospectively identify and isolate NICs as GlastmidEGFRhighPlexinB2highCD24−/lowO4/PSA-NCAM−/lowTer119/CD45− cells, referred to as GEPCOT cells. These cells were found to be highly mitotic and short-lived in vivo, based on fate-mapping with Ascl1CreERT2 and Dlx1CreERT2. In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells were quiescent, expressed higher levels of Glast, and lower levels of EGFR and PlexinB2. Pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres but expressed stem cell markers such as Slc1a3-CreERT, GFAP-CreERT2, Sox2CreERT2, and Gli1CreERT2, and were long-lived in the SVZ. Conditional deletion of the Bmi-1 polycomb protein depleted both pre-GEPCOT and GEPCOT cells, with pre-GEPCOT cells being more dependent on Bmi-1 for Cdkn2a ( p16Ink4a ) repression. The study distinguishes between quiescent NSCs and NICs, making it possible to study their properties in vivo. GEPCOT cells accounted for 3.2 ± 0.7% of all SVZ cells and were highly enriched for NICs, with 36 ± 6% forming neurospheres and 74% of those undergoing multilineage differentiation. In contrast, pre-GEPCOT cells were unable to form neurospheres but expressed stem cell markers and were long-lived in the SVZ. Fate-mapping with Ascl1CreERT2 or Dlx1CreERT2 showed that GEPCOT cells persisted only transiently in the SVZ, while pre-GEPCOT cells were long-lived. Conditional deletion of Bmi-1 depleted both cell populations, with pre-GEPCOT cells being more dependent on Bmi-1 for Cdkn2a repression.
The study investigated the function of Piezo1 ion channels as mechanotransducers in various cell types and tissues. Cryo-electron microscopy revealed that the Piezo1 structure is bowl-shaped and capable of inducing membrane curvature, suggesting that Piezo1 ion channels may interact functionally. The researchers used cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp stimulation to examine large numbers of membrane patches from cells expressing Piezo1 endogenously at low levels and cells overexpressing Piezo1 at high levels. The results showed that Piezo1 density has no effect on its pressure sensitivity or open probability in the nominal absence of membrane tension, both at endogenous densities (1–2 channels/μm2) and at non-physiological densities (10–100 channels/μm2) predicted to cause substantial footprint overlap. This suggests that Piezo channels, at densities likely to be physiologically relevant, inherently behave as independent mechanotransducers. The study proposes that this property is essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire cell membrane. Stochastic simulations of Piezo1 spatial distributions supported the electrophysiological data, showing that Piezo1 ion channels, at densities likely to be physiologically relevant, inherently function as independent mechanotransducers. This result is important because it shows that Piezo1 channels have no strong intrinsic tendency to spatially concentrate and functionally modulate each other’s mechanical sensitivity, which is ideal for a cell to transduce mechanical forces homogeneously. The study contributes to our understanding of the molecular mechanisms of force detection in cells and highlights the importance of Piezo1 channels in sensing mechanical forces.
The study investigated the influence of evidence volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence in a perceptual decision. A novel sensory manipulation was introduced to mimic the addition of unbiased noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex, which was verified with neuronal recordings from macaque areas MT/MST. The results showed that increasing the noise induced faster decisions and greater confidence in both humans and monkeys over a range of stimuli for which accuracy was minimally impaired. The magnitude of the effects was in agreement with predictions of a bounded evidence accumulation model.

The study aimed to test the predictions of the bounded evidence accumulation model, which suggests that an increase in noise should lead to faster decisions, reduced accuracy, and paradoxically, higher confidence. To achieve this, the researchers applied the manipulation to a well-studied motion task, verified the effect of the manipulation by recording from neurons in the visual cortex of the macaque, and showed how the framework based on the bounded accumulation of evidence can account for the joint effect of volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence.

The study demonstrated that a stimulus manipulation that increases the variance of the momentary evidence bearing on a decision, termed volatility, increases both the speed of the decision and the confidence associated with it. The researchers tested the influence of volatility on the decision process by mimicking a manipulation of noise by changing the statistical properties of a dynamic stimulus. They verified that the manipulation has the desired properties by recording from direction-selective neurons in the middle temporal (MT) and medial superior temporal (MST) areas of the macaque visual cortex, which are known to represent the momentary evidence in tasks identical to those in the study.

The study used a reaction-time version of the random-dot motion discrimination task to investigate the relationship between volatility and decision speed. Three human participants completed 6631 trials, and one macaque completed 14,137 trials. The results showed that increasing the noise induced faster decisions and greater confidence in both humans and monkeys over a range of stimuli for which accuracy was minimally impaired. The magnitude of the effects was in agreement with predictions of a bounded evidence accumulation model.
The insulin-like signaling pathway in Caenorhabditis elegans maintains a relatively short wild-type lifespan by phosphorylating and inactivating DAF-16, the ortholog of the FOXO transcription factors in mammalian cells. DAF-16 is phosphorylated by AKT kinases, preventing its nuclear translocation. Calcineurin (PP2B phosphatase) also limits the lifespan of C. elegans, but its mechanism of action was previously unknown. This study reveals that TAX-6•CNB-1, the C. elegans Calcineurin ortholog, and UNC-43, the C. elegans Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent kinase type II (CAMKII) ortholog, regulate lifespan through DAF-16. UNC-43 phosphorylates and activates DAF-16, promoting its nuclear localization, whereas TAX-6•CNB-1 dephosphorylates and inactivates DAF-16. The phosphorylation of DAF-16 at S286 by UNC-43 is removed by TAX-6•CNB-1, leading to DAF-16 inactivation. In contrast to insulin signaling, which phosphorylates and inactivates DAF-16, UNC-43 phosphorylates and activates DAF-16. Mammalian FOXO3 is also regulated by CAMKIIA and Calcineurin. The study demonstrates that CAMKII (UNC-43) and Calcineurin (TAX-6•CNB-1) regulate C. elegans lifespan through the reversible phosphorylation of DAF-16. The direct, antagonistic nature of these two enzymes raises the possibility that they would constitutively negate each other and have no control over DAF-16. However, the insulin signaling pathway appears to dominate in conditions where there is a conflict between IIS and CAMKII/Calcineurin signaling toward DAF-16. The study provides new insights into the regulation of lifespan in C. elegans and highlights the complex interplay between multiple signaling pathways, including insulin/IGF-1 signaling, germline signaling, mitochondrial signaling, and the signaling pathways induced by dietary restriction.
A recent study in Drosophila (fruit flies) has shed light on the organizational principles governing the sequential execution of motor programs, specifically in the context of grooming behavior. The researchers used genetically targeted activation of neural subsets to drive distinct motor programs that clean individual body parts, demonstrating that these programs are organized into a suppression hierarchy. This hierarchy dictates that motor programs that occur first suppress those that occur later, allowing the grooming sequence to progress down the hierarchy.

    The study found that dust on the body elicits different independently evoked cleaning motor programs for five body parts that progress in series. By controlling each cleaning motor program through genetic targeting, the researchers were able to demonstrate that they are organized into a suppression hierarchy. An empirically derived computational model was also developed to show how a suppression hierarchy can mediate the sequential selection of the cleaning motor programs.

    The findings suggest that the evolutionarily ancient ability to select among competing behaviors through hierarchical suppression may have provided the necessary infrastructure to perform motor actions in sequence. This is in contrast to activation chain and parallel models, which describe different mechanisms for generating sequential behavior. The study highlights the importance of understanding the neural circuitry for each cleaning module and how these circuitries interact to reveal how the serial organization of grooming behavior is encoded in the nervous system through a hierarchical suppression mechanism.

    The research provides the first example of an innate motor sequence implemented by the prevailing model for generating human action sequences. The study's findings have implications for understanding the neural control of serial action selection in other animals, including humans. By identifying the neural circuitry for each cleaning module and determining how these circuitries interact, researchers can gain a deeper understanding of how the serial organization of behavior is encoded in the nervous system.

    The study's use of genetically targeted activation of neural subsets allowed for experimental control over each cleaning motor program, enabling the demonstration of the suppression hierarchy. This approach has invigorated interest in identifying the neurons that drive behavior and has enabled experiments to probe the organization of serial behavior through specific control of individual motor actions.
The protein Shugoshin-like 2 (Sgol2) plays a crucial role in coordinating essential cell cycle processes during meiosis I in mammalian oocytes. Sgol2 is involved in protecting cohesin, silencing the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC), promoting the congression and bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis I spindles, facilitating the formation of kinetochore-microtubule attachments (K-fibers), and limiting bivalent stretching. Sgol2's ability to protect cohesin depends on its interaction with protein phosphatase 2A (PP2A), while its ability to silence the SAC is mediated by direct binding to the MCC protein Mad2. In contrast, Sgol2's effects on bivalent stretching and K-fiber formation are independent of PP2A and are mediated by the recruitment of the kinesin MCAK and the inhibition of Aurora C kinase activity, respectively. By virtue of its multiple interactions, Sgol2 links many of the processes essential for faithful chromosome segregation.

Depletion of Sgol2 in oocytes has pleiotropic consequences during meiosis I, including inefficient SAC silencing, increased bivalent stretching, and alterations in the dynamics of kinetochore-microtubule attachments. Sgol2 silences the SAC by binding to Mad2 using a similar MIM motif common to Mad2's other two partners, Cdc20 and Mad1. Sgol2 also delays bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis I spindles, causes increased bivalent stretching, and increases Aurora B/C kinase activity at kinetochores, which is accompanied by an increase in lateral and a decrease in end-on kinetochore-microtubule attachments. Furthermore, Sgol2 deficiency delays APC/CCdc20 activation, suggesting that it is required to shut off the SAC.

Interestingly, a single protein, Sgol2, regulates sister chromatid cohesion, chromosome traction, the spindle assembly checkpoint, and Aurora B/C kinases. Different point mutations in Sgol2 alter the regulation of some but not other processes, indicating that the pleiotropy observed in Sgol2-deficient oocytes is not due to knock-on effects of eliminating a single function. Sgol2's ability to protect centromeric cohesin depends on its interaction with PP2A, but its deficiency causes striking changes in chromosome and microtubule dynamics, suggesting that Sgol2 has additional functions beyond cohesin protection.
Neuron-wide RNA transport combines with netrin-mediated local translation to spatially regulate the synaptic proteome in Aplysia sensory-motor neurons. Previous studies have shown that local translation plays a crucial role in altering the structure and function of synapses during synapse formation and experience-dependent synaptic plasticity. This study investigated whether local stimulation also triggers the directed trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to stimulated synapses. Imaging of RNA localization and translation in cultured Aplysia sensory-motor neurons revealed that RNAs were delivered throughout the arbor of the sensory neuron, but translation was enriched only at sites of synaptic contact and/or synaptic stimulation. The mechanisms that trigger local translation were found to involve calcium-dependent retrograde netrin-1/DCC receptor signaling. Spatially restricting gene expression by regulating local translation rather than directing the delivery of mRNAs from the nucleus to stimulated synapses maximizes the readiness of the entire neuronal arbor to respond to local cues. The study suggests that the spatial regulation of gene expression in Aplysia sensory-motor neurons is primarily mediated at the level of translation rather than at the level of RNA targeting from the nucleus. Netrin-1/DCC signaling was found to play a key role in regulating local translation at synapses and/or stimulated synapses. The release of netrin-1 from either the sensory neuron (SN) or the motor neuron (MN) during synapse formation and synaptic plasticity triggers translation at sites of synaptic but not non-synaptic contacts. Recombinant Fc-netrin-1 was used to test the possibility that netrin-1 regulates the synthesis of localized transcripts in Aplysia sensory-motor neuronal cultures. The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms underlying the spatial regulation of gene expression in neurons and the role of netrin-1/DCC signaling in local translation.
Title: Spatiotemporal mosaic self-patterning of pluripotent stem cells using CRISPR interference

    A novel in vitro system has been developed to investigate cell-cell interactions and multicellular organization within human induced pluripotent stem cell (hiPSC) colonies. The system utilizes CRISPR interference (CRISPRi) to silence key proteins that regulate cell adhesion and cortical tension, allowing for controlled symmetry-breaking events within hiPSC populations. By creating mixed populations of hiPSCs with and without the CRISPRi system and inducing mosaic knockdown (KD) of specific genes, researchers were able to observe differential patterning within the colonies due to cellular self-organization.

    The study focused on the effects of induced mosaic KD of molecular regulators of cortical tension (ROCK1) and cell-cell adhesion (CDH1) on hiPSC colonies. Mosaic KD of ROCK1 or CDH1 resulted in differential patterning within the colonies, while retaining an epithelial pluripotent phenotype. This patterning was accompanied by a transient wave of differential gene expression within the mixed populations, which stabilized in coordination with the observed self-organization.

    The CRISPRi system enabled the genetic interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, facilitating a better understanding of the molecular pathways that regulate symmetry-breaking during morphogenesis. The study demonstrates a novel strategy to direct the emergence of heterotypic cell populations and control multicellular organization in pluripotent stem cells. This approach has the potential to create robust models of morphogenesis necessary for the mechanistic study of human developmental tissue patterning and formation.

    The results of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of morphogenesis and the role of cell-cell interactions in the formation of complex multicellular patterns and structures. The use of CRISPRi to silence specific genes within subpopulations of cells allows for a high degree of control over the emergence of asymmetric cell phenotypes and the development of multicellular patterns. This approach has significant implications for the study of human developmental biology and the development of novel therapeutic strategies for tissue engineering and regenerative medicine.
A resource-rational theory of set size effects in human visual working memory proposes that the brain minimizes a weighted sum of an error-based behavioral cost and a neural encoding cost to explain why encoding precision in visual working memory decreases with the number of encoded items. The theory suggests that the brain trades off expected task performance against the cost of spending neural resources on encoding. This normative theory was tested on data from nine delayed-estimation experiments and found to account well for effects of both set size and probing probability on encoding precision. The model also predicts that the total amount of invested resource can vary non-monotonically with set size, which is supported by the data. Furthermore, the theory suggests that it is sometimes optimal to encode only a subset or even none of the relevant items in a task. This raises the possibility that cognitive "limitations" arise from rational cost minimization rather than from constraints. The theory may apply beyond working memory tasks, potentially explaining set size effects in attention tasks as well. It provides a normative formalism to test the speculation that the selectivity of attention arises from a need to balance performance against the costs associated with spiking. The model has relatively few parameters and accounts for a non-monotonicity between set size and the total amount of resource that subjects invest in item encoding. At the neural level, this translates to a prediction of a non-monotonic relation between population-level spiking activity and set size, which can be tested using neuroimaging experiments. The theory also makes predictions about effects of incentive manipulations on encoding precision, as such manipulations affect the expected behavioral cost function.
The Irish potato famine pathogen, Phytophthora infestans, has evolved an effector protein called PexRD54 that antagonizes a host autophagy cargo receptor to counteract host defenses. Autophagy is a cellular process used by plants to safeguard against infectious diseases, but how plant pathogens interfere with autophagy-related processes was previously unknown. PexRD54 binds to the host autophagy protein ATG8CL, stimulating the formation of autophagosomes and depleting the autophagy cargo receptor Joka2 out of ATG8CL complexes. This interaction allows PexRD54 to interfere with Joka2's positive effect on pathogen defense, promoting disease susceptibility.

    PexRD54 has an ATG8 interacting motif that enables it to bind to ATG8CL with high affinity, unlike the Legionella effector RavZ, which activates autophagy to eliminate defense-related compounds or reattribute cellular resources. The RXLR effector family, to which PexRD54 belongs, is a diverse group of modular proteins that alter various host processes and serve as useful probes to dissect key pathways for pathogen invasion.

    The study reveals that, in addition to disrupting autophagy, pathogens can also activate it for their own benefit. PexRD54 competes with the host cargo receptor Joka2 and depletes it out of ATG8CL autophagosomes to promote disease susceptibility. The effector's ability to outcompete Joka2 and deplete it from ATG8CL autophagosomes suggests that PexRD54 has evolved to antagonize a host autophagy cargo receptor to counteract host defenses.

    The findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms that mediate defense-related autophagy and the selective nature of this process. The study highlights the importance of understanding how adapted plant pathogens manipulate defense-related autophagy and/or subvert autophagy for nutrient uptake. Future studies will need to consider pathogen-directed modulation of the spatio-temporal dynamics of autophagy and subcellular trafficking during host infection.

    The discovery of PexRD54's ability to bind to ATG8CL and deplete Joka2 from ATG8CL complexes suggests that other plant pathogens may also secrete effectors that evolved an ATG8 interacting motif or target autophagy in other ways. These effectors could serve as valuable tools to dissect the mechanisms of defense-related selective autophagy.
The study investigated the adaptive responses of human cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) to controlled mechanical strain and oxygen changes in vitro. Cardiac fibroblasts play a crucial role in tissue adaptation and inflammation during pathological conditions such as ischemia. However, a comprehensive understanding of how early environmental changes induce CF pathological responses is lacking due to the lack of approaches involving complex and simultaneous environmental stimulation. To address this knowledge gap, the authors developed a multi-stimulus microdevice that applies cyclic mechanical strain and controlled oxygen tension to human primary CFs. The results show that individual stimuli cause distinct responses in CFs, including proliferative effects such as increased mitotic cells, YAP translocation, and PDGF secretion, as well as increased collagen presence. Notably, the combination of hypoxia and simulated loss of contractility (2% strain) triggers the release of inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases. This synergistic effect was not observed with individual stimuli. The study highlights the importance of considering the combination of environmental stimuli in understanding CF pathological responses and provides insights into emerging adaptive cellular mechanisms. The findings have implications for future studies of pathological myocardial remodeling and the development of in vitro models for cardiac disease. The authors propose an innovative model that recreates a pathological environment for understanding CF responses during cardiac injury, providing a tool for better understanding pathological mechanisms and tailoring reparative strategies. The study contributes to the development of in vitro models of cardiac disease, which have been widely studied under relevant physico-chemical stimulation, including mechanical stress, oxygen deprivation, and biochemical stimulation with pro-fibrotic cytokines. By finely controlling the applied mechanical strain and oxygen regimes, the authors provide insights into the individual or synergistic contributions of environmental signals in the activation of early CF responses relevant to cardiac fibrotic disease. The results suggest that mimicking the combination of hypoxia and reduced contractility is crucial in eliciting inflammatory and fibrotic remodeling responses of CFs, while individual environmental stimuli only regulate proliferative and collagen-related responses.
Drosophila SWR1 and NuA4 complexes are defined by DOMINO isoforms. The DOMINO complex is a chromatin regulator that combines histone acetylation and deposition of H2A. Z variant in Drosophila. Two alternative isoforms of the DOMINO nucleosome remodeling ATPase, DOM-A and DOM-B, specify two distinct multi-subunit complexes. Both complexes are necessary for transcriptional regulation, but through different mechanisms. The DOM-B complex incorporates H2A. V genome-wide in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex. In contrast, the DOM-A complex functions as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4, similar to the yeast NuA4 complex. This work provides an example of how different evolutionary strategies can lead to similar functional separation. In yeast and humans, nucleosome remodeling and histone acetyltransferase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralog specification. Drosophila generates the same diversity by alternative splicing of a single gene. The two DOM isoforms, DOM-A and DOM-B, have distinct structures and functions. DOM-A has a long ATPase domain and is thought to be the central subunit of a NuA4-type complex in Drosophila. The existence of such a complex with functional and structural similarity to the yeast NuA4 complex has not been reported before. The ATPase activity of DOM-A is dispensable for H4K12 acetylation by the DOM-A complex. This work illustrates how alternative splicing generates functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators. H2A. Z is primarily found at active promoters and enhancers, where it is thought to be important for regulating transcription initiation and early elongation. In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, H2A. Z is introduced into chromatin by the SWR1 complex, a multi-subunit ATP-dependent chromatin remodeler with the INO80-type ATPase SWR1 at its core. The DOM-B complex incorporates H2A. V genome-wide in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex. The DOM-A complex functions as an ATP-independent histone acetyltransferase complex, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4, similar to the yeast NuA4 complex. The functional differences of the two DOM isoforms on transcription were explored by RNAseq. Knock-downs of DOM-A, DOM-B, TIP60, and H2A. V were included in the analysis. The results suggest that the two DOM isoforms have distinct roles in transcriptional regulation.
The formation of neuromuscular junctions (NMJs) between motoneurons and muscle cells is a complex process that requires precise interactions between the two cell types. A key player in this process is β-catenin (Ctnnb1) in muscle cells, which has been shown to be critical for motoneuron differentiation. However, the nature of the retrograde signal from muscle cells to motoneurons that directs presynaptic differentiation remains poorly understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers used an in vivo transgenic approach to investigate the functions of muscle Ctnnb1 in NMJ formation. They found that the transcriptional activity of muscle Ctnnb1 is essential for presynaptic differentiation, as a Ctnnb1 mutant lacking the transactivation domain (TAD) was unable to rescue presynaptic deficits in mice with a Ctnnb1 mutation. In contrast, the cell-adhesion function of Ctnnb1 was found to be dispensable. This suggests that muscle Ctnnb1 regulates presynaptic differentiation by controlling the expression of muscle-derived proteins.

    The researchers then screened for proteins that may serve as Ctnnb1-directed retrograde factors and identified Slit2 as a potential candidate. They found that transgenic expression of Slit2 in muscle cells was able to diminish presynaptic deficits in mice with a Ctnnb1 mutation, including poor innervation, reduced vesicle density and active zone, and depressed miniature endplate potentials (mEPPs). Furthermore, Slit2 immobilized on beads was able to induce synaptophysin puncta in axons of spinal cord explants. These observations suggest that Slit2 serves as a factor utilized by muscle Ctnnb1 to direct presynaptic differentiation. The researchers also showed that Ctnnb1 associates with Tcf/Lef cis-elements in the promoter region of the Slit2 gene, and that Slit2 expression in muscle cells is promoted by elevating Ctnnb1. Slit2 was found to be concentrated at the NMJ, further supporting its role in presynaptic differentiation. Overall, these findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying NMJ formation and highlight the importance of Slit2 as a retrograde signal from muscle cells to motoneurons.
Title: Mapping the Dynamics of Force Transduction at Cell–Cell Junctions of Epithelial Clusters

    A recent study has elucidated the dynamics of force transduction at cell–cell junctions in epithelial clusters using a combination of computational and experimental approaches. The research aimed to quantify the transmission of mechanical forces through cell–cell junctions with both sub-cellular and multi-cellular resolution. The study focused on spontaneously-forming adherent epithelial cell clusters and found that basal force fluctuations were coupled to E-cadherin localization at the level of individual cell–cell junctions.

    Using a generalized cell–cell force measurement and analysis method, the researchers quantified the transmission of mechanical forces through cell–cell junctions of dynamic adherent epithelial clusters. Time-resolved force measurements demonstrated that cell–cell forces at individual junctions fluctuate over time, both during interphase and cell divisions. The study investigated whether and how basal force variations were coupled to the localization and recruitment of molecular components of cell–cell junctions, focusing on E-cadherin, the backbone of adherens junctions for epithelial cells.

    The results showed that local mechanical stresses along the cell junction correlated with the localization of E-cadherin molecules. Expanding the approach to temporal force fluctuation analysis at the length scale of a cell, the researchers found that force propagation through cells can be intercepted by cell anchorage to the extracellular matrix and by actomyosin contraction. The method also provided insights into tissue mechanics by revealing that force transmission across a cell cluster is short-ranged, typically on the length scale of one cell diameter, due to the anchoring and scrambling of forces by cell–matrix adhesions and basal actomyosin contractility.

    The study demonstrated that force transmission through a cell required coordinated modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility in the cell and its neighbors. The findings provide insights into mechanisms that could control mechanical stress homeostasis in dynamic epithelial tissues and highlight the developed methods as a resource for the study of mechanotransduction in cell–cell adhesions.
Huntington’s disease is a neurodegenerative disorder caused by an expansion of the polyglutamine tract in the huntingtin protein. To understand the structural and biochemical properties of huntingtin and the effect of the polyglutamine expansion, researchers conducted various biophysical experiments, including circular dichroism, single-particle electron microscopy, and cross-linking mass spectrometry. The results indicate that huntingtin is composed of five distinct domains and adopts a spherical α-helical solenoid structure, with the amino-terminal and carboxyl-terminal regions forming a circumscribed central cavity. The polyglutamine expansion was found to increase the α-helical properties of huntingtin and affect intramolecular interactions among the domains. These findings provide insight into the structural characteristics of full-length huntingtin and how the polyglutamine expansion affects its structure and function. The researchers propose that the spherical solenoid structure of huntingtin enables the modulation of its structure and function by the polyglutamine tract, which may explain how the disease-causing expansion of the polyglutamine tract confers a novel property on huntingtin. The study used a panel of highly purified human recombinant huntingtins with varied lengths of polyglutamine tracts to investigate the impact of the polyglutamine tract on huntingtin’s basic structural features. The results suggest that huntingtin may undergo dramatic conformational changes upon interaction with its binding partners. Single-particle electron microscopy of recombinant human huntingtins with polyglutamine tract lengths of 23- and 78-residues revealed a spherical α-helical solenoid structure. The study provides a satisfying solution to the mystery of how the amino-terminal polyglutamine tract may modulate huntingtin structure and function. The findings have implications for understanding the molecular mechanisms underlying Huntington’s disease and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies.
Ferritinophagy, the autophagic turnover of ferritin, is a critical process for regulating intracellular iron bioavailability. However, the mechanisms controlling ferritinophagy flux and the role of NCOA4, a selective cargo receptor for ferritin, in iron-dependent processes are poorly understood. This study reveals the molecular basis of the NCOA4-ferritin complex and elucidates how intracellular iron levels modulate NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy in cells and in an iron-dependent physiological setting. NCOA4 interacts directly with ferritin heavy chain 1 ( FTH1 ) via a conserved NCOA4 C-terminal domain and a key conserved residue on FTH1. This interaction is essential for the delivery of ferritin to the lysosome via autophagosomes. NCOA4 abundance is under dual control via autophagy and the ubiquitin proteasome system, with ubiquitin-dependent NCOA4 turnover promoted by excess iron and involving an iron-dependent interaction between NCOA4 and the HERC2 ubiquitin ligase. The HERC2 ligase recognizes NCOA4 under high iron conditions to mediate NCOA4 turnover via the ubiquitin-proteasome system, thereby reducing steady-state NCOA4 levels and increasing ferritin for iron capture. In zebrafish and cultured cells, NCOA4 plays an essential role in erythroid differentiation, highlighting the importance of NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy in maintaining cellular iron homeostasis. These findings provide new insights into the regulation of ferritinophagy and the role of NCOA4 in iron-dependent processes, shedding light on the molecular mechanisms underlying iron homeostasis in cells.
The lateral prefrontal cortex (LPFC) plays a crucial role in higher-level cognition, but its functional organization has remained poorly understood. A long-standing proposal suggests that the LPFC is organized hierarchically, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract information to facilitate efficient and flexible cognition. However, empirical support for this theory has been limited. Recent human functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data revealed a rostral-caudal gradient of abstraction in the LPFC, indicating that progressively rostral areas of the LPFC process more abstract information. Dynamic causal modeling further revealed asymmetrical interactions among LPFC sub-regions, indicative of hierarchical processing. Contrary to prevailing assumptions, the relative strength of efferent versus afferent connections positioned the mid-LPFC as the apex of the hierarchy. Cognitive demands induced connectivity modulations towards the mid-LPFC, consistent with a role in integrating information for control operations. Furthermore, the strengths of these dynamics were related to trait-measured higher-level cognitive ability. These findings collectively suggest that the LPFC is hierarchically organized, with the mid-LPFC positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information to control behavior. The hierarchical organization of the LPFC was confirmed by examining gradients of stimulus domain-generality/specificity and temporal control over action. Modeling interactions among LPFC sub-regions revealed that cognitive control demands elicit asymmetrical influences indicative of hierarchical processing. Both the hierarchical orientation of the mid-LPFC in fixed connectivity and the top-down influences from rostral LPFC to mid-LPFC during the engagement of cognitive control were positively related to higher-level cognitive ability. The results suggest that top-down control within the LPFC is an important determinant of higher-level cognitive function. Furthermore, more strongly asymmetrical hierarchical projections from mid-LPFC to other LPFC regions were related to better higher-level cognitive ability, indicating a beneficial cognitive effect of a hierarchical organization with mid-LPFC at the apex. Dynamic causal modeling revealed that hierarchical asymmetries in connectivity were strongest in mid-LPFC, and cognitive demands elicited both top-down influences from rostral to mid-LPFC and bottom-up influences from caudal to mid-LPFC. Analyses of effective connectivity and its modulations suggest that mid-LPFC forms a nexus where information converges to influence action.
The hominoid-specific gene TBC1D3 has been found to play a crucial role in the generation of basal neural progenitors and the induction of cortical folding in mice. Cortical expansion and folding are thought to be linked to the evolution of higher intelligence, but the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying cortical folding remain poorly understood. TBC1D3 undergoes segmental duplications during hominoid evolution, but its role in brain development has not been explored until now. The study found that expression of TBC1D3 in ventricular cortical progenitors of mice via in utero electroporation caused delamination of ventricular radial glia cells and promoted the generation of self-renewing basal progenitors with typical morphology of outer radial glia, which are most abundant in primates. Furthermore, down-regulation of TBC1D3 in cultured human brain slices decreased the generation of outer radial glia. Localized outer radial glia proliferation resulting from either in utero electroporation or transgenic expression of TBC1D3 was often found to underlie cortical regions exhibiting folding. This suggests that TBC1D3 is required for the generation of outer radial glia in regulating the cortical expansion and folding. The study also found that TBC1D3 expression in neuroprogenitors by in utero electroporation at E13.5 caused a reduced expression of Trnp1 in flow cytometry-sorted cells at E15, indicating that TBC1D3 affects intrinsic stemness signaling pathways in basal progenitors. The study provides proof-of-concept that brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during hominoid evolution, and that TBC1D3 acts as a candidate gene that controls many other genes involved in cortex expansion and folding. The results suggest that TBC1D3-induced outer radial glia generation and cortical folding may be a key mechanism underlying the evolution of higher intelligence in primates.
The study investigated the role of ubiquitination in the transcriptional activity of the plant immune coactivator NPR1. NPR1 is a key regulator of systemic acquired resistance in plants, and its activation is associated with transcriptome reprogramming. Previous research suggested that immune-induced ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation of NPR1 facilitate continuous delivery of active NPR1 to target promoters, thereby maximizing gene expression. However, the study aimed to explore whether ubiquitination of NPR1 plays a transcriptional role prior to its proteasomal turnover.

The results showed that ubiquitination of NPR1 is a progressive event, initiated by a Cullin-RING E3 ligase (CRL3), which promotes chromatin association and expression of target genes. Subsequent polyubiquitination of NPR1 by the E4 ligase UBE4 targets it for proteasomal degradation. Conversely, ubiquitin ligase activities are opposed by UBP6/7, two proteasome-associated deubiquitinases that enhance NPR1 longevity.

The study found that initial CRL3-mediated ubiquitination is required for NPR1 to attain its full transcriptional activity, while the stepwise formation of long ubiquitin chains mediated by UBE4 inactivates NPR1 and promotes its degradation by the proteasome. Instead, initial ubiquitination boosts NPR1 transcriptional coactivator activity, enhancing target promoter occupancy in the short term and potentially promoting genomic mobility of NPR1 in the longer term. The study suggests that ubiquitin chain extension and trimming activities can fine-tune transcriptional outputs of unstable eukaryotic activators without strict requirement for sacrificial turnover.

Furthermore, the study demonstrated that loss of UBE4 largely uncouples NPR1 target gene expression from proteasome activity, highlighting the importance of progressive ubiquitination for NPR1 activity. The findings indicate that the sequential actions of E3 and E4 ligases, balanced by opposing deubiquitinases, fine-tune the activity of NPR1 without a strict requirement for its sacrificial turnover. This study provides new insights into the complex regulation of NPR1 and its role in plant immune responses.
In Arabidopsis thaliana, 24 nucleotide small interfering RNAs ( 24 nt siRNAs ) play a crucial role in guiding cytosine methylation and silencing of transposons and a subset of genes. The biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs requires nuclear RNA polymerase IV ( Pol IV ), RNA-dependent RNA polymerase 2 ( RDR2 ), and DICER-like 3 ( DCL3 ). However, the precursors of siRNAs have remained largely undefined. This study aimed to identify the precursors of 24 nt siRNAs in Arabidopsis. The researchers found that Pol IV and RDR2-dependent RNAs ( P4R2 RNAs ) accumulate in dcl3 mutants and are diced into 24 nt RNAs by DCL3 in vitro. P4R2 RNAs are mostly 26-45 nt and initiate with a purine adjacent to a pyrimidine, characteristics shared by Pol IV transcripts generated in vitro. RDR2 terminal transferase activity may account for occasional non-templated nucleotides at P4R2 RNA 3’ termini. The 24 nt siRNAs primarily correspond to the 5’ or 3’ ends of P4R2 RNAs, suggesting a model whereby siRNAs are generated from either end of P4R2 duplexes by single dicing events. The study also found that dozens of P4R2 RNAs can overlap at a given locus, but individual P4R2 RNAs represent individual Pol IV-RDR2 transcription units. Furthermore, the short double-stranded P4R2 RNAs that accumulate in dcl3 mutants are the immediate precursors of siRNAs, based on the ability of DCL3 to cut these precursors into 24 nt RNAs in vitro. These findings provide new insights into the biogenesis of 24 nt siRNAs and the role of Pol IV and RDR2 in this process.
The development of mature neural networks in the brain is a complex process that enables the precise spatiotemporal coordination of synaptic and cellular activity necessary for cognition. However, the postnatal time course for neural networks to perform complex computations remains poorly understood. A recent study investigated the progression of large-scale synaptic and cellular activity patterns across development in immature mice using high spatiotemporal resolution in vivo electrophysiology. The results reveal that mature cortical processes emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile transition period at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development. This transition is characterized by relative neural quiescence, after which spatially distributed, temporally precise, and internally organized activity occurs. The study also demonstrates a similar developmental trajectory in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism that could facilitate a transition in network operation. The authors hypothesize that this transient quiescent period is a requisite for the subsequent emergence of coordinated cortical networks. The study found that the transition between immature and mature network dynamics was characterized by unexpectedly decreased coordinated cellular and synaptic activity at the beginning of the second postnatal week. After this timepoint, precise neuronal synchronization and oscillatory coupling in space and time robustly emerged. The results suggest that a transient period of developmental perturbation may be an evolutionarily conserved indicator of a substantial shift in cortical network operations. This shift is marked by the disappearance of immature activity patterns and the emergence of internally organized, spatially distributed, and temporally precise activity. The study provides new insights into the development of mature neural networks and highlights the importance of a transient quiescent period in the emergence of coordinated cortical networks.
An agent-based model has been developed to investigate the population structuring of multi-copy, antigen-encoding genes in Plasmodium falciparum, a parasite responsible for malaria. The model extends earlier ‘strain theory’ by considering the extensive diversity of var genes, which undergo antigenic variation via sequential, mutually exclusive expression. The model tracks the dynamics of all unique var repertoires in a population of hosts and demonstrates that even under high levels of sexual recombination, strain competition mediated through cross-immunity structures the parasite population into a subset of coexisting dominant repertoires of var genes. The degree of antigenic overlap between these repertoires depends on transmission intensity. Empirical comparison of patterns of genetic variation at antigenic and neutral sites supports the role of immune selection in structuring parasite diversity.

The var gene family of P. falciparum encodes P. falciparum erythrocyte membrane protein 1 (PfEMP1), the major surface antigen of the parasite's blood stages that binds to host endothelial cell receptors to allow the parasite to sequester within various host tissues. The model considers the effects of immune selection in populations of pathogens whose antigen-encoding loci are multi-copy and recombining. By tracking all possible var repertoires, given a diverse pool of genetic variants, the model explores whether structured parasite populations resulting from the selection pressures exerted by competition among distinct var repertoires for hosts, mediated by cross-immunity, can emerge.

Mathematical models of competition between pathogen strains have demonstrated that immune selection can lead to the maintenance of discrete, non-overlapping, antigenic repertoires, even when antigenic variability is driven by several unlinked genes that undergo recombination. The ‘var strain’ model suggests that vector control strategies that reduce transmission will not always drastically reduce var gene diversity, because a reservoir of parasite genomes with diverse (highly non-overlapping) var repertoires will remain. The agent-based model developed in this study extends strain theory to address the effect of immune selection acting on multi-copy antigen genes and provides insights into the population structuring of P. falciparum.
The deamidase OspI from the enteric bacterium Shigella flexneri disrupts the interaction between the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC13 and the RING-finger ubiquitin ligase TRAF6 by deamidating a glutamine residue in UBC13 to glutamate (Q100E). This post-translational modification silences the polyubiquitination activity of the UBC13/TRAF6 complex, which is essential for the downstream activation of the NF-κB inflammatory response. The precise mechanism by which deamidation inhibits the UBC13/TRAF6 interaction was investigated, revealing that it perturbs both native and transient interactions between the two proteins. Deamidation creates a new intramolecular salt-bridge in UBC13 that competes with a critical intermolecular salt-bridge at the native UBC13/TRAF6 interface, preventing the formation of a typical UBC13/RING complex. Additionally, repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged surface of TRAF6 prevents transient interactions necessary for complex formation. These findings highlight a novel mechanism by which a post-translational modification can inhibit protein-protein association by disrupting the conformation and stability of transient complexes. Further studies demonstrated that deamidation disrupts the interaction between UBC13 and TRAF6RING by three mechanisms: the appearance of a new intramolecular salt-bridge in UBC13, salt-bridge competition that perturbs transient complexes, and transient repulsion between E100 and the negatively charged interface of TRAF6RING. The Shigella flexneri effector OspI suppresses the inflammatory response by deamidating UBC13 at Q100, which inhibits the synthesis of K63-linked polyubiquitin chains by the UBC13/TRAF6 complex, preventing the downstream activation of the NF-κB pathway.
Title: Asymmetric Effects of Activating and Inactivating Cortical Interneurons: A Study in the Auditory Cortex of Awake Mice

    Summary:
    A recent study investigated the effects of activating and inactivating two types of cortical interneurons, somatostatin-positive (Sst+) and parvalbumin-positive (Pvalb+) interneurons, in the auditory cortex of awake mice. Using single-unit recordings and optogenetic manipulations, the researchers found that inactivating Sst+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivating Pvalb+ interneurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer. In contrast, activating Sst+ and Pvalb+ interneurons did not produce such differences. These findings suggest that Sst+ and Pvalb+ interneurons support specific, separable functions in the auditory cortex.

    The study used a linear framework to evaluate how interneuron activation suppresses auditory cortical responses. The researchers applied this framework to 59 units from ChR2/Sst mice and 57 units from ChR2/Pvalb mice whose responses were both tuned for frequency and significantly suppressed by activation of interneurons. The results showed that inactivating Sst+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivating Pvalb+ interneurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer.

    The study also used a simple network model to understand the asymmetric effects of activating and inactivating interneurons. The model showed that relatively small changes in key parameters, such as spontaneous activity or strength of the light manipulation, determined whether activation and inactivation would produce consistent or paradoxical conclusions regarding interneurons' computational functions.

    The findings of this study challenge the assumption that tonic activation and inactivation will both produce valid, internally consistent insights into interneurons' computational roles. Instead, the results suggest that the effects of activating and inactivating interneurons can be asymmetric, and that the choice of manipulation can influence the conclusions drawn about interneurons' functions. The study highlights the importance of considering the specific properties of interneurons and the experimental design when using causal strategies to gain insight into interneuron function.
Title: Externally induced frontoparietal synchronization modulates network dynamics and enhances working memory performance

Cognitive functions such as working memory (WM) are emergent properties of large-scale network interactions, and synchronization of oscillatory activity is thought to contribute to WM by enabling the coordination of long-range processes. However, causal evidence for the way oscillatory activity shapes network dynamics and behavior in humans is limited. To investigate the role of oscillatory synchronization in WM, researchers used transcranial alternating current stimulation (tACS) to exogenously modulate oscillatory activity in a right frontoparietal network that supports WM.

The study aimed to provide causal evidence for the role of oscillatory synchronization during demanding WM performance and to investigate the underlying neural mechanisms by which frontoparietal phase synchronization influences verbal WM. Two experiments were conducted using tACS to selectively entrain endogenous brain rhythms. The researchers tested four hypotheses: (1) behavioral WM performance is enhanced by entraining synchronous activity within a right frontoparietal network activated by WM demands; (2) regional brain activity in this frontoparietal network is modulated in a phase-dependent manner; (3) changes in BOLD induced by phase-dependent network stimulation correlate with behavioral performance; and (4) functional connectivity across distant brain regions is modulated in a phase-dependent manner.

The results showed that externally induced synchronization improved performance when cognitive demands were high. Simultaneously collected fMRI data revealed tACS effects dependent on the relative phase of the stimulation and the internal cognitive processing state. Synchronous tACS during the verbal WM task increased parietal activity, which correlated with behavioral performance. Functional connectivity results indicated that the relative phase of frontoparietal stimulation influences information flow within the WM network. Specifically, frontal interactions increased with synchronous theta stimulation, whereas posterior interactions increased with desynchronous stimulation, suggesting that information flow through brain regions involved in WM can be differentially affected by varying the phase of external stimulation.

Overall, the findings demonstrate a link between behavioral performance in a demanding WM task and large-scale brain synchronization across a right frontoparietal network activated by the task. The study provides direct evidence for the role of phase synchronization in cognitive demanding WM processes and shows the neural correlates associated with this process. The results suggest that external manipulations of large-scale brain synchronization can be used to shape activity and connectivity within brain networks, and those modulations are detectable using fMRI.
The transcription factor Pitx2 has been identified as a key regulator of embryonic axis formation and twinning in amniote embryos. In contrast to invertebrates and anamniote vertebrates, where embryonic polarity is specified by maternal determinants early in development, amniote embryos remain plastic and can form multiple individuals until gastrulation. A molecular screen was conducted to identify upstream regulators of cVg1, a transforming growth factor beta (TGFβ) signal expressed posteriorly before gastrulation in the chick embryo. The screen revealed Pitx2 as a candidate transcription factor that acts as a direct regulator of cVg1 expression by binding to enhancers within neighboring genes. Pitx2 is essential for axis formation and is also involved in left-right asymmetry, suggesting that the same ancient polarity determination mechanism has been co-opted to different functions during evolution.

Pitx2 is a transcription factor that is expressed in the same domain as cVg1 in whole embryos and in bisected embryo marginal zone, and is expressed before cVg1. The results implicate Pitx2 as an upstream regulator of cVg1 expression, and suggest that it plays a crucial role in the earliest stages of specifying embryo polarity. The ability of higher vertebrate embryos to retain a regulative model of development until a late stage suggests that localisation of maternally inherited determinants is not an essential component of the mechanisms specifying embryo polarity.

The expression of Pitx2 and cVg1 was examined in whole embryos and in isolated anterior halves, and the results show that Pitx2 is expressed before cVg1 and in the same domain. This suggests that Pitx2 acts as a direct regulator of cVg1 expression, and that it is essential for axis formation. Pitx2, Vg1/GDF1, and Nodal are also key actors in left-right asymmetry, suggesting that the same ancient polarity determination mechanism has been co-opted to different functions during evolution. These findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of embryonic axis formation and twinning in amniote embryos, and highlight the importance of Pitx2 as a key regulator of these processes.
In the fruit fly Drosophila melanogaster, projection neurons ( PNs ) in the antennal lobes play a crucial role in signaling the acceleration of odor concentrations to higher brain centers. The study investigated how early olfactory circuits process temporal variations of olfactory stimuli by subjecting flies to precisely defined odor concentration waveforms and examining the spike patterns of olfactory sensory neurons ( OSNs ) and PNs. The results showed a significant temporal transformation between OSN and PN spike patterns, with the PN output strongly signaling the OSN spike rate and its rate of change. A simple two-dimensional model admitting the OSN spike rate and its rate of change as inputs closely predicted the PN output, indicating that PNs primarily signal the acceleration and rate of change of dynamic odor stimuli to higher brain centers. This encoding mechanism enables animals to reliably respond to the onsets of odor concentrations. PNs subsequently relay olfactory information to higher brain centers such as the mushroom body and the lateral horn. Recent studies have shown that Drosophila OSNs encode not only the odor concentration but also its rate of change as a function of time. Building on this advance, the study aimed to investigate how PNs further contribute to creating internal representations of dynamic olfactory environments. By testing first- and second-order neurons in the Drosophila olfactory system with a wide range of dynamically varying odor concentrations, the study constructed a simple 2D LN model for the temporal processing of olfactory information. The shape of the 2D nonlinearity and the relatively low prediction error of the model support the hypothesis that the PN spike rate is most strongly dependent on the rate of change of its feedforward OSN input. The study demonstrated that Drosophila's early olfactory system signals the acceleration of odor concentrations to higher brain centers, allowing an animal to detect the odor onset at a very early phase. This encoding mechanism is crucial for flying insects to detect and respond to an encountered odor plume before the fly's position drifts relative to adjacent plumes. The study contributes to our understanding of how dynamic olfactory stimuli are processed in insect early olfactory systems and highlights the importance of acceleration encoding in the Drosophila antennal lobe for rapid response to onsets of slowly rising odor stimuli.
In flowering plants, seed development is initiated by the fusion of the maternal egg and central cells with two paternal sperm cells, leading to the formation of embryo and endosperm, respectively. The fertilization products are surrounded by the maternally derived seed coat, whose development prior to fertilization is blocked by epigenetic regulators belonging to the Polycomb Group ( PcG ) protein family. This study reveals that fertilization of the central cell results in the production of auxin, which is likely exported to the maternal tissues, driving seed coat development by removing PcG function. Furthermore, mutants for the MADS-box transcription factor AGL62 have an impaired transport of auxin from the endosperm to the integuments, resulting in seed abortion. The authors propose that AGL62 regulates auxin transport from the endosperm to the integuments, leading to the removal of the PcG block on seed coat development.

    The study provides multiple lines of evidence supporting this hypothesis, including the rapid accumulation of auxin in the integuments after fertilization, impaired auxin biosynthesis but not auxin signaling in the endosperm causing defects in seed coat development, ovules of transgenic lines producing auxin ectopically in the central cell initiating seed coat development without fertilization, and failure of seed coat development in the agl62 mutant correlating with failure to export auxin from the endosperm. These findings suggest that seed coat development requires production of auxin in the fertilized central cell/endosperm and most likely the transport of auxin to the ovule integuments in an AGL62-dependent manner. The fertilization of the central cell results in the production of auxin that is exported to the integuments, lifting the PRC2 block on seed coat development and allowing for cell elongation and differentiation. However, this process is highly dependent on the activity of the transcription factor AGL62, since agl62 seeds fail to export auxin from the endosperm, leading to auxin accumulation and consequently, failure to develop a seed coat. The study proposes a dual role for post-fertilization auxin production in driving both endosperm and seed coat development.
The interaction between a bacteriophage and its host is mediated by the phage's receptor binding protein (RBP), which plays a crucial role in governing phage activity and host range. Despite its importance, the molecular rules of RBP function remain poorly understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers developed a high-throughput, locus-specific phage engineering method called ORACLE (Optimized Recombination, Accumulation, and Library Expression) to systematically dissect the functional role of every residue in the tip domain of T7 phage RBP. By creating a large, unbiased library of phage variants at a targeted gene locus, the researchers made all single amino acid substitutions at every site (1660 variants) and quantified the functional role of all variants on multiple bacterial hosts.

    The study generated high-resolution functional maps delineating regions concentrated with function-enhancing substitutions and host-specific substitutional patterns, many of which were previously unknown. The results revealed that single amino acid substitutions alone can generate broad functional diversity, highlighting the evolutionary adaptability of the RBP. The researchers discovered T7 variants with far greater virulence than wildtype T7, demonstrating that even natural phages well adapted to a host can be engineered for higher efficacy. Furthermore, the study identified gain-of-function variants against resistant hosts and host-constricting variants that eliminated certain hosts.

    To demonstrate the therapeutic value of ORACLE, the researchers engineered T7 variants that avert emergence of spontaneous resistance in pathogenic Escherichia coli causing urinary tract infections. The study explains the molecular drivers of adaptability of the tip domain and identifies key functional regions determining activity and host range. The results indicate that ORACLE can generate phage variants superior to wildtype phage that could serve as starting points for further engineering of therapeutic phages. The study presents a generalized framework for characterizing sequence-function relationships in many phage-bacterial systems, providing valuable insights into the molecular mechanisms governing phage-host interactions.
Individuals with congenital amusia, a lifelong condition characterized by unreliable pitch processing, have been found to downweight pitch cues during speech perception and instead rely on other dimensions such as duration. To investigate the neural basis for this strategy, a functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) study was conducted on 15 individuals with amusia and 15 controls. Participants read sentences where a comma indicated a grammatical phrase boundary and then heard two sentences spoken that differed only in pitch and/or duration cues, selecting the best match for the written sentence. The results showed prominent reductions in functional connectivity between left prefrontal language-related regions and right hemisphere pitch-related regions in the amusia group, reflecting the between-group differences in cue weights. These connectivity differences were not present during a control task. The study suggests that the reliability of perceptual dimensions is linked with functional connectivity between frontal and perceptual regions, and that individuals with amusia may have implicitly learned to neglect pitch due to its unreliability. This down-weighting of pitch is reflected in decreased functional connectivity between right auditory areas and downstream task-relevant areas that integrate information from perceptual regions. The findings are consistent with prior reports that amusics and controls do not differ in pitch representations within sensory regions, and that brainstem encoding of pitch in speech and musical stimuli is unimpaired in individuals with amusia. The study provides evidence for a compensatory mechanism in individuals with amusia, where they rely on other dimensions such as duration to process spoken language. The results have implications for understanding the neural basis of speech perception and language processing in individuals with congenital amusia.
The Devil Facial Tumour 2 (DFT2) is a contagious cancer recently discovered in the Tasmanian devil (Sarcophilus harrisii), a species that already harbours a more widespread contagious cancer, Devil Facial Tumour 1 (DFT1). In contrast to DFT1, DFT2 cells express major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class I molecules, indicating that loss of MHC is not necessary for the emergence of a contagious cancer. However, the most highly expressed MHC class I alleles in DFT2 cells are common among host devils or non-polymorphic, reducing immunogenicity in a population sharing these alleles. This suggests that contagious cancers may exploit partial histocompatibility between the tumour and host, but loss of allogeneic antigens could facilitate widespread transmission of DFT2.

    DFT2 cells express classical and non-classical MHC class I molecules both in vitro and in vivo, in contrast to DFT1, which has lost expression of MHC class I. The non-polymorphic, non-classical MHC class I molecule (Saha-UK) expressed by DFT2 cells likely reduces the immunogenicity of these tumour cells. Furthermore, the classical MHC class I allele with the highest expression in DFT2 cells is shared with host devils. MHC class I expression varies among DFT2 tumours in vivo, suggesting that MHC class I-negative subclones could emerge as DFT2 transmits more widely.

    The similarity of MHC class I alleles between DFT2 and host devils may reflect a shared geographical range and genotype pool to the cancer founder. The frequency of this allele among the wider population may impact the ability of DFT2 to spread further. The heterogenic expression of MHC class I molecules in DFT2 biopsies suggests that MHC class I expression is not fixed and may be gradually lost in DFT2. Further analysis is needed to define the host immune response to DFT2 and its role in shaping tumour evolution.
The granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG) plays a crucial role in both spatial and non-spatial behaviors, but the underlying neural codes remain poorly understood. Recent optogenetic circuit mapping in mice has revealed a double dissociation between parallel circuits in superficial RSG that process disparate inputs. The anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum, sources of spatial information, strongly and selectively recruit small low-rheobase (LR) pyramidal cells in RSG. In contrast, neighboring regular-spiking (RS) cells are preferentially controlled by claustral and anterior cingulate inputs, sources of mostly non-spatial information. Precise sublaminar axonal and dendritic arborization within RSG layer 1 permits this parallel processing.

    The study found that LR neurons receive directional and spatial inputs from the anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum. The synaptic dynamics of thalamic inputs to LR cells can give rise to rate coding of angular head speed in LR cells, despite receiving head direction inputs that do not explicitly encode speed. This suggests that parallel input streams identify a distinct principal neuronal subtype ideally positioned to support spatial orientation computations in the RSG.

    The results highlight a superficial retrosplenial circuit enabled by the precise sublaminar organization of distinct principal neuronal subtypes and axonal afferents. The anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum preferentially control LR neurons, whereas claustrum and anterior cingulate control RS cells. The synaptic dynamics of thalamic inputs allow for the robust encoding of angular speed by LR cell models, even though these inputs only explicitly contain information about head direction, not speed.

    Layer 3 of the granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG) contains two types of principal pyramidal neurons: the low-rheobase (LR) neuron and the regular-spiking (RS) neuron. The distinct morphology and intrinsic properties of LR neurons make them ideally suited to integrate head direction input from the thalamus and spatial inputs from the hippocampal formation via the dorsal subiculum. This integration of various types of directional and distance information is often stated as the key computational function of the retrosplenial cortex as a whole.
Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a significant risk factor for the development of dementias, including tauopathies such as chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE). The mechanisms underlying the prion-like spreading of Tau aggregates after TBI are not fully understood, partly due to the lack of tractable animal models. To investigate the role of seizures in promoting the spread of tauopathy, researchers developed a novel zebrafish model expressing a genetically encoded fluorescent Tau biosensor. This biosensor reliably reports the accumulation of human Tau species when seeded via intraventricular brain injections.

The zebrafish larvae were subjected to a novel TBI paradigm, which produced various TBI features, including cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions. The researchers found that bath application of dynamin inhibitors or anticonvulsant drugs rescued TBI-induced tauopathy and cell death. These findings suggest a role for seizure activity in the prion-like seeding and spreading of tauopathy following TBI.

Zebrafish larvae have emerged as a potent complement to rodent models of TBI and cellular models of tauopathy. The engineered fish allowed the researchers to reveal post-traumatic seizures as a druggable mechanistic link between TBI and the prion-like progression of tauopathy. The study introduced two new approaches to test the hypothesis that neuronal excitability and seizure activity after TBI can accelerate the wide dissemination of Tau pathology. The first approach was to engineer a novel in vivo Tau biosensor model in zebrafish that can visualize pathological Tau spreading and accumulation within the intact and vibrant CNS.
