The researchers found that a substantial burst of neural activity occurs during TBI, and zebrafish larvae exposed to TBI subsequently exhibit a significantly higher propensity for spontaneous seizures. After validating that their method was able to induce TBI upon zebrafish larvae, the researchers next asked whether TBI induces Tau aggregates in their Tau biosensor model. Combining these novel approaches, they found that post-traumatic seizures correlate strongly with spreading tau pathology following TBI. Manipulating this seizure activity mitigated Tau aggregation and revealed a critical role for endocytosis in the prion-like spread of Tau seeds in vivo following TBI. The results from their novel in vivo TBI model implicate seizures and dynamin-dependent endocytosis in the spread of Tau seeds, thereby offering potential therapeutic targets.
The relationship between spatial configuration and functional connectivity of brain regions was investigated in a recent study using resting state functional magnetic resonance imaging (rfMRI) data. The study aimed to identify how the spatial arrangement of functional brain regions and the coupling strength between neural populations influence derived measures of functional connectivity and relate to trait variability in behavior and lifestyle across individuals. It was found that the shape and exact location of brain regions interact strongly with the modeling of brain connectivity, and that the spatial arrangement of functional regions is strongly predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle. This suggests that cross-subject variations in the spatial configuration of functional brain regions may be misinterpreted as changes in functional connectivity. The study highlights the importance of disambiguating the influence of spatial topography to enable the estimation of fMRI measures that uniquely reflect coupling strength between neural populations. To address this issue, significant advances have been made in recent years to tackle the problem of spatial misalignment across individuals. The organization of the human brain into large-scale functional networks has been extensively investigated using rfMRI, revealing complex patterns of functional connectivity between distinct brain regions. However, the study found that network matrices estimated from rfMRI data contain little unique trait-level cross-subject information that is not also reflected in the spatial topographical organization of functional regions. This implies that the cross-subject information represented in commonly adopted 'connectivity fingerprints' largely reflects spatial variability in the location of functional regions across individuals, rather than variability in coupling strength. The findings of this study have important implications for the interpretation of functional imaging data and cognitive traits, and highlight the need for a better understanding of the relationship between spatial configuration and functional connectivity of brain regions.
The TBP/TFIID-dependent activation of MyoD target genes in skeletal muscle cells is a complex process that involves the replacement of the canonical TFIID-TBP complex with the TRF3/TBP2 complex to activate muscle-gene expression. However, recent studies have proposed that TBP2 does not replace TBP during muscle differentiation, but rather that limiting amounts of TFIID-TBP are required to promote muscle-specific gene expression. To investigate this further, researchers examined the role of TBP2 in adult skeletal muscle regeneration and myogenesis. TBP2 null mice were found to have an intact regeneration potential upon injury, and TBP2 was not expressed in established C2C12 muscle cells or primary mouse MuSCs. Furthermore, TFIID subunits and TBP were downregulated during myoblast differentiation, but reduced amounts of these proteins formed a complex that was detectable on promoters of muscle genes and was essential for their expression. These findings suggest that TBP, but not TBP2, is the essential component of the pre-initiation complex that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated skeletal muscles. The results also indicate that TBP2 is not required for adult skeletal muscle regeneration or myogenesis, as MuSCs isolated from TBP2-deficient mice differentiated into myotubes with the same efficiency as wild-type MuSCs. Additionally, the RNA expression profile of Tbp and Tbpl2 genes in MuSCs isolated from skeletal muscles of wild-type mice by FACS and in the C2C12 myogenic cell line showed that Tbp expression was detected in both MuSC-derived myotubes and in C2C12 myotubes. Overall, these findings provide evidence that the TFIID complex is present in myogenic cells and drives muscle gene transcription during myoblast differentiation into myotubes, and that TBP is the essential component of the pre-initiation complex that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated skeletal muscles.
Title: Targeted Cortical Reorganization Using Optogenetics in Non-Human Primates

    Summary
    This study investigated the effects of optogenetic stimulation on large-scale network dynamics in the brains of two macaques. The researchers used a large-scale optogenetic interface to simultaneously stimulate excitatory neurons and record electrocorticographic activity across the primary somatosensory (S1) and motor (M1) cortex. Two measures of network connectivity were tracked: the network response to focal stimulation and the baseline coherence between pairs of electrodes. The results showed that stimulation in S1 or M1 significantly strengthened the gross functional connectivity between these areas within minutes. At a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous connectivity changes across the network, which reflected the correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity, consistent with Hebbian plasticity models.

    The study demonstrated a robust relationship between stimulus-evoked correlations and changes in baseline connectivity across the network. These findings are well-explained by a Hebbian model of large-scale stimulation-induced plasticity. The researchers also investigated how stimulation impacts connectivity between and within cortical areas, testing whether stimulation-evoked activity drives large-scale network plasticity in a Hebbian manner. The results support a key role for Hebbian learning in sensorimotor cortical plasticity, suggesting that large-scale stimulation-induced changes are consistent with Hebbian plasticity models.

    The large-scale optogenetic interface used in this study enabled robust estimation of functional connectivity and stimulation-evoked correlations. This work extends Hebbian plasticity models to large-scale circuits, with significant implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation. The findings have the potential to inform future neurorehabilitation strategies by providing insights into the mechanisms of large-scale functional reorganization following stimulation.
In a rare case of a Rhesus macaque (monkey S) born without primary visual cortex (V1), researchers investigated the neural consequences of such damage and its impact on visual perception. The study aimed to understand how the brain compensates for the loss of V1 and to inform the development of rehabilitation methods for human patients with similar damage. The results show that despite the absence of V1, monkey S exhibited unremarkable in-group behavior, but was impaired in visual task training. Magnetic resonance imaging revealed that visual structures outside of the lesion, including cortical and subcortical areas, were intact and normal in appearance. Visual stimulation under anesthesia with checkerboards activated the lateral geniculate nucleus, while full-field moving dots activated the pulvinar. Functional connectivity analysis revealed an intact network of bilateral dorsal visual areas temporally correlated with V5/MT activation, even in the absence of V1. However, there was little evidence for strengthened subcortical input to V5/MT supporting residual visual function or blindsight-like phenomena.

The study found that the structural connectivity between subcortical regions and area V5/MT was weak, but the microstructure was intact. This suggests that the residual visual function in monkey S may be supported by an intact network of extrastriate cortical visual areas, comparable to control monkeys. The researchers also found that the ventricles within the visual cortex were enlarged and fluid-filled, which could have affected the pattern of anesthesia action in monkey S. A connectivity analysis was performed to understand the nature of the response in area V5/MT, which showed no significant activation to either checkerboard or moving dots stimuli. However, foci of activation were found in the region of V5/MT in dorsal STS of both hemispheres, suggesting sub-threshold activation. A functional network of cortical areas showing a similar BOLD response over time to V5/MT was also mapped out. The study suggests that retinal information may reach the dorsal visual areas via weak pathways with LGN and pulvinar, but the lack of consistent visual cortex activation makes this question difficult to answer. The lack of significant differences between monkey S and controls in the pathways between LGN-V5/MT and pulvinar-V5/MT suggests that either or both could support residual visual abilities.
The innate immune sensor IFI16 plays a crucial role in recognizing foreign double-stranded (ds) DNA of pathogenic origin in the intracellular environment. However, the molecular mechanisms underlying the distinction between foreign DNA and host genomic material inside the nucleus were not well understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers used biochemical assays and single-molecule techniques to investigate how IFI16 recognizes and responds to foreign dsDNA in the nucleus. The results show that IFI16 one-dimensionally tracks long stretches of exposed foreign dsDNA to assemble into supramolecular signaling platforms. This unique scanning-assisted assembly mechanism allows IFI16 to distinguish between self and non-self DNA and assemble into oligomers efficiently and selectively on foreign DNA.

The study found that nucleosomes, which are the basic units of chromatin, act as barriers that prevent IFI16 from targeting host DNA by directly interfering with its one-dimensional movements. This suggests that chromatinization is a key feature that allows IFI16 to distinguish host from foreign DNA in the nucleus. The researchers used time-resolved bulk and single-molecule fluorescence assays to demonstrate that IFI16 scans along exposed dsDNA to assemble into distinct clusters, and that chromatinization is sufficient to inhibit IFI16 from targeting host dsDNA for assembly.

In vivo, this one-dimensional scanning mechanism allows a limited number of IFI16 molecules to allocate each other on large genomes of invading pathogens. The study provides a unifying molecular mechanism that explains how IFI16 resolves the central issues in initiating its foreign-dsDNA sensing pathways. The results suggest that the 1D-diffusion mediated assembly plays a key role in regulating the overall IFI16-mediated immune responses. Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the recognition of foreign DNA by the innate immune sensor IFI16 and highlights the importance of chromatinization in distinguishing self from non-self DNA in the nucleus.
A novel proteomics technique called spatially targeted optical microproteomics (STOMP) has been developed for interrogating micron-scale regions of interest (ROIs) in mammalian tissue without the need for genetic manipulation. This technique involves staining specimens with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize ROIs, followed by soaking in a solution containing a photo-tag, 4-benzoylbenzyl-glycyl-hexahistidine. Confocal imaging and two-photon excitation are used to covalently couple the photo-tag to all proteins within each ROI, achieving a resolution of 0.67 µm in the xy-plane and 1.48 µm axially. After tissue solubilization, photo-tagged proteins are isolated and identified by mass spectrometry.

STOMP was tested on amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse model and a post-mortem AD case, confirming known plaque constituents and discovering new ones. The technique can be applied to various biological samples, including cell lines, primary cell cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and fixed post-mortem tissue. Gel electrophoresis of indiscriminately photo-tagged proteins revealed a different pattern of protein bands compared to the specimen in which amyloid plaques were specifically targeted for STOMP analysis.

STOMP combines two-photon laser scanning microscopy with photochemical affinity labeling and mass spectrometry. The photo-tagging procedure uses two-photon microscopy to drive microscopic, 3D-resolved photochemistry, offering single-micron-scale three-dimensional resolution that is an order of magnitude better than the current state of the art. The predominant protein photo-tagged in the STOMP sample was Aβ, which is not visible in the non-plaque experiment due to its low overall abundance. This new proteomic technique has great potential to advance research in cell biology by yielding the composition of small features that are not amenable to biochemical purification and may provide clues to the etiology of neurodegenerative disease.
The Frizzled3 (Fz3) gene plays a crucial role in controlling axonal development in distinct populations of cranial and spinal motor neurons. Disruption of the Fz3 gene leads to defects in axonal growth in the VIIth and XIIth cranial motor nerves, the phrenic nerve, and the dorsal motor nerve in fore- and hindlimbs. In Fz3−/− limbs, dorsal axons stall at a precise location in the nerve plexus and do not reroute to other trajectories. This stalling defect is distinct from the phenotypes produced by defects in other signaling systems active in limb axon guidance. Affected motor neurons undergo cell death 2 days prior to the normal wave of developmental cell death that coincides with innervation of muscle targets. This provides in vivo evidence for the idea that developing neurons with long-range axons are programmed to die unless their axons arrive at intermediate targets on schedule. The experiments implicate planar cell polarity (PCP) signaling in motor axon growth and highlight the question of how PCP proteins, which form cell–cell complexes in epithelia, function in the dynamic context of axonal growth. In the limb, the absence of Fz3 leads to a specific stalling defect among LMCL axons that is distinct from the phenotypes produced by defects in several other signaling systems active in limb axon guidance. Affected cranial and spinal motor neurons die shortly after their axons stall, providing in vivo evidence for the general idea that as growing axons arrive at intermediate targets, essential survival signals are relayed to their cell bodies. The primary cause of motor axon deficiency in the VIIth, XIIth, phrenic, and dorsal limb nerves in Fz3−/− mice is not defective motor neuron differentiation or excessive motor neuron death, but rather defective axon growth and/or guidance. The majority of Fz3−/− motor axons do not follow erroneous trajectories, suggesting that aberrant path-finding is unlikely to be relevant. A model that could account for the precocious motor neuron death in Fz3−/− embryos invokes the existence of neuronal survival factors produced by intermediate targets along an axon’s trajectory. In Fz3−/− embryos, a discrete subset of motor neurons exhibits both axon growth defects and apoptotic cell death at E11. 5-E12. 5. This suggests that controlling axonal growth may be the only function of Fz3 in motor neurons.
The study investigated the role of transposable elements (TEs) as enhancers in gene regulation in mouse embryonic and trophoblast stem cells. TEs are repetitive DNA sequences that have been co-opted by the genome to regulate gene expression. While previous studies have suggested that TEs are involved in establishing gene regulatory networks, their functional significance remains unclear. To address this, the authors tested a set of long terminal repeat (LTR) TE families for their roles as enhancers in mouse embryonic and trophoblast stem cells. Epigenomic and transcriptomic data revealed that a large number of TEs contribute to tissue-specific gene expression programs. However, genetic editing experiments using CRISPR interference (CRISPRi) and CRISPR-mediated genetic excision showed that only a minority of TEs play significant roles in gene regulation. The results suggest that a subset of TEs are important for gene regulation in early mouse development. The study highlights the importance of functional experiments in evaluating the gene regulatory roles of TEs, as some TEs may play minor, redundant, or no role in gene regulation despite strong correlative evidence. The findings also emphasize the need for extensive genetic work to determine the proportion of TE+ enhancers that act in a redundant fashion with other regulatory elements.
The evolution of C4 photosynthesis, a complex trait that has independently emerged in at least 60 plant lineages, has long been a subject of interest in the scientific community. Despite its widespread occurrence, the evolutionary paths that led to the development of C4 photosynthesis have remained unclear. To address this knowledge gap, researchers conducted a meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species that use C3, C4, or intermediate C3–C4 forms of photosynthesis to parameterise a 16-dimensional phenotypic landscape. This analysis revealed that the polyphyletic appearance of C4 photosynthesis is associated with diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories.

    A novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model was developed to predict how the C4 phenotype evolved. This model was experimentally verified and used to determine the alternative evolutionary histories underlying the appearance of C4 photosynthesis. The results showed that the order of C4 trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers. This flexibility is thought to have facilitated the convergent evolution of this complex trait.

    The researchers also developed a predictive Bayesian approach that can infer likely evolutionary trajectories connecting phenotypes from sparsely sampled, highly stochastic data. This model provided insights into the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, which is an ideal system for the mathematical modelling of complex trait evolution due to the difficulty of comprehensively exploring the emergence of the entire C4 phenotype experimentally.

    The study found that distinct sub-types of C4 photosynthesis evolved as a consequence of alternative evolutionary histories in response to non-photosynthetic pressures. Early evolutionary events determined the downstream phenotypes of C4 sub-types by restricting lineages to independent pathways across phenotype space. This suggests that mechanistic diversity underlies the multiple evolutionary pathways generating C4 photosynthesis and may be a key factor in facilitating the convergent evolution of complex traits.

    The findings of this study have implications for efforts to recapitulate the acquisition of C4 photosynthesis through the genetic engineering of C3 species. By understanding the evolutionary paths that led to the development of C4 photosynthesis, researchers may be able to expand the molecular toolbox available to establish C4 traits in distinct phenotypic backgrounds.
The study investigated the ATPase activity of SpoIVA, a bacterial cytoskeletal protein that hydrolyzes ATP to polymerize irreversibly during Bacillus subtilis sporulation. SpoIVA evolved from a TRAFAC class of P-loop GTPases, but the evolutionary pressure that drove this change in nucleotide specificity is unclear. To address this, the researchers reengineered the nucleotide-binding pocket of SpoIVA to mimic its ancestral GTPase activity. The resulting SpoIVAGTPase protein was functional in vivo and could use either ATP or GTP to induce a similar conformational change after nucleotide hydrolysis. However, SpoIVAGTPase only polymerized in the presence of ATP, suggesting a specific requirement for the nucleotide base, not just the energy released from nucleotide hydrolysis, for its function. The study also found that physiological levels of intracellular ATP, but not GTP, facilitate the requisite conformational changes in SpoIVA and SpoIVAGTPase. Furthermore, the researchers observed that SpoIVA and SpoIVAGTPase failed to undergo nucleotide hydrolysis-mediated structural changes in the presence of low concentrations of GTP, but did so in the presence of higher concentrations of ATP. These findings suggest that the nucleotide base, in addition to the energy released from hydrolysis, can be critical in specific biological functions. The study also presents data suggesting that increased levels of ATP relative to GTP at the end of sporulation was the evolutionary pressure that drove the change in nucleotide preference in SpoIVA. The results of this study provide new insights into the evolution of nucleotide specificity in SpoIVA and highlight the importance of the nucleotide base in specific biological functions.
The present study investigated the long-term consequences of the absence of leptin signaling in early life by restoring leptin signaling in mice formerly null for the leptin receptor (LepR) gene at either the fourth or tenth week of age. Leptin plays a crucial role in regulating energy balance and exhibits neurotrophic effects during critical developmental periods. However, the actual role of leptin during development is not yet fully understood. The study found that some defects previously considered irreversible due to neonatal deficiency of leptin signaling, including the poor development of arcuate nucleus neural projections, were recovered by LepR reactivation in adulthood. However, LepR deficiency in early life led to irreversible obesity via suppression of energy expenditure. LepR reactivation in adulthood also led to persistent reduction in hypothalamic Pomc, Cartpt, and Prlh mRNA expression and to defects in the reproductive system and brain growth. These findings revealed that early defects in leptin signaling cause permanent metabolic, neuroendocrine, and developmental problems. The study suggests that changes in leptin signaling during development can affect the organization of critical neural circuits that regulate energy homeostasis, favoring the incidence of metabolic diseases later in life. The results indicate that early postnatal leptin blockage in rats leads to long-term leptin resistance and susceptibility to diet-induced obesity. The study highlights the importance of leptin signaling during development for the long-term energy homeostasis and other aspects regulated by leptin. The findings contribute to the understanding of the metabolic programming by indicating which specific metabolic, neuroendocrine, and developmental consequences arise from the absence of leptin signaling in early life, especially in the context of the alarming growth of childhood obesity worldwide.
Arabidopsis heterotrimeric G proteins, composed of XLG2, AGB1, and AGG1/2, play a crucial role in regulating immunity by directly coupling to the FLS2 receptor. The FLS2 receptor perceives bacterial flagellin epitope flg22 to activate defenses through the central cytoplasmic kinase BIK1. In the pre-activation state, XLG2 directly interacts with FLS2 and BIK1, and functions together with AGB1 and AGG1/2 to attenuate proteasome-mediated degradation of BIK1. This allows for optimum immune activation. Following activation by flg22, XLG2 dissociates from AGB1 and is phosphorylated by BIK1 in the N terminus. The phosphorylated XLG2 enhances the production of reactive oxygen species ( ROS ) likely by modulating the NADPH oxidase RbohD. The study demonstrates that the G proteins are directly coupled to the FLS2 receptor complex and regulate immune signaling through both pre-activation and post-activation mechanisms. The G proteins modulate flg22-triggered immunity by directly coupling to the FLS2-BIK1 receptor complex. Prior to activation by flg22, the G proteins attenuate the proteasome-dependent degradation of BIK1, ensuring optimum signaling competence. After flg22 stimulation, XLG2 dissociates from AGB1, indicating a ligand-induced dissociation of Gα from Gβγ. Introduction of a transgenic copy of BIK1 largely restored flg22-triggered immune responses and disease resistance in xlg2 and agb1 mutants, demonstrating that a major function of the heterotrimeric G protein is to maintain the signaling competence of the FLS2-BIK1 complex in the pre-activation state. In addition to the ligand-induced dissociation of XLG2 from AGB1, flg22 induces the phosphorylation of XLG2 in the N terminus. The results show that the G proteins dynamically interact with the FLS2-BIK1 receptor complex during FLS2 signaling, and the findings are consistent with the observed defects in flg22-induced responses and RbohD phosphorylation in the G protein mutants. The study collectively supports that plant receptor kinases fulfill the roles of GPCRs by directly coupling to heterotrimeric G proteins. The G proteins coupled to the FLS2 receptor complex are subject to multiple regulations during FLS2 signaling. The heterotrimeric G proteins formed by XLG2/3, AGB1, and AGG1/2 attenuate proteasome-dependent degradation of BIK1 and PBL20. The regulation of BIK1 stability contributes to the role of heterotrimeric G proteins in flg22-induced immune responses.
The Arabidopsis circadian clock is a complex system that regulates gene expression across the day-night cycle. While a multiple feedback loop circuit has been proposed to generate the 24-hour rhythm, the robustness and coordination of the clock in individual cells and across the plant remain unclear. To address this, researchers examined clock activity at the single cell level in Arabidopsis seedlings over several days under constant environmental conditions. The results reveal robust single cell oscillations, albeit desynchronized, with two waves of clock activity observed, one traveling down and one up the root. The study also found evidence of cell-to-cell coupling of the clock, particularly in the root tip. A simple model suggests that cell-to-cell coupling and measured period differences between cells can generate the observed waves of clock gene expression. The findings provide insight into the spatial structure of the plant clock and suggest that unlike the centralized mammalian clock, the Arabidopsis clock has multiple coordination points. The researchers used a reporter for the core clock gene CIRCADIAN CLOCK ASSOCIATED 1 ( CCA1 ) to examine the dynamics of the Arabidopsis clock across the whole plant at the single cell level over several days. The data reveal that damping of rhythms at the whole plant level is mainly due to desynchronization of oscillating single cells with different periods, rather than noise in gene expression or damping of individual oscillations. The study supports a more decentralized model of clock coordination in plants, which is in contrast to earlier studies that suggested cellular oscillations are uncoupled.
The study investigated the neural signatures of disordered multi-talker speech perception in adults with clinically normal hearing. The researchers found that multi-talker speech intelligibility thresholds varied widely among individuals with normal hearing, but could be predicted from neural phase-locking to frequency modulation (FM) cues measured with ear canal EEG recordings. A combination of neural temporal fine structure processing, pupil-indexed listening effort, and behavioral FM thresholds accounted for 78% of the variability in multi-talker speech intelligibility. These findings suggest that disordered bottom-up and top-down markers of poor multi-talker speech perception could inform the design of next-generation clinical tests for hidden hearing disorders. The study aimed to develop objective physiological markers that might explain complaints of poor speech communication in individuals with clinically normal hearing. To achieve this, the researchers applied parallel psychophysical and neurophysiological tests of sTFS processing in combination with physiological measures of effortful listening. The results showed that individuals with steeper pupil recruitment functions had more difficulty in the multi-talker speech task, leading to a significant correlation between min-max/half-max pupil change and speech intelligibility threshold. The study also found that the strong association between psychophysical tests for FM detection and speech-in-noise intelligibility can be attributed, at least in part, to encoding of FM cues at early stages of auditory processing. Furthermore, the correlation between low-level FM encoding and speech intelligibility might reflect a correlation between any measure of fast temporal processing fidelity and speech intelligibility. Overall, the study presents a simple battery of behavioral and physiological tests that can account for nearly 80% of the variability in a test of multi-talker speech intelligibility that does not involve linguistic cues. These findings have the potential to inform the design of next-generation clinical tests for hidden hearing disorders.
Biomedical researchers have developed a novel imaging technique to noninvasively measure single-cell blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels of the living mouse retina. The technique employs 15 kHz adaptive optics imaging and ultrafast camera capture to visualize blood cells without the need for foreign contrast agents. This approach enables the measurement of blood flow in vessels with diameters ranging from 3.2 to 45.8 micrometers, covering the full spectrum of retinal vessel sizes. The imaging paradigm is paired with an automated analysis workflow, allowing for high-throughput velocity measurement of hundreds of thousands of blood cells per second. This method has the potential to revolutionize the early evaluation of microvascular diseases that originate in capillaries, which are currently limited by the imperfections in the optics of the eye. The study reports the measurement of single-cell velocity and flow rate in 123 vessels across 19 mice, with flow rates ranging from 0.0002 to 1.55 microliters per minute. The results demonstrate the capability to image single blood cells noninvasively in both tight and sparsely packed formations inside microvessels, with sub-micrometer precision when measuring lumen diameters of retinal vessels. This imaging technique may serve as a window to supplement the understanding of microcirculation dynamics in the central nervous system and provide insights into systemic microvascular health. The findings of this study have the potential to be directly applicable to clinical approaches using the same instrumentation, offering a noninvasive and label-free method for studying blood flow in the retina.
Biologists have long sought to understand the mechanisms that govern the development of symbiotic relationships between plants and microorganisms, particularly in the context of phosphate-acquiring arbuscular mycorrhiza ( AM ) and nitrogen-fixing root nodule symbiosis. A key receptor-like kinase, Symbiosis Receptor-like Kinase ( SYMRK ), has been identified as essential for the development of both symbiotic relationships. However, the mechanisms that distinguish between the two distinct symbiotic developmental fates have remained unclear. To address this knowledge gap, researchers investigated the role of SYMRK and other receptor-like kinases in the development of symbiotic relationships in the legume Lotus japonicus.

    The study found that ectopic expression of the receptor-like kinase genes Nod Factor Receptor 1 ( NFR1 ), NFR5, and SYMRK initiated spontaneous nodule organogenesis and nodulation-related gene expression in the absence of rhizobia. Furthermore, overexpressed NFR1 or NFR5 associated with endogenous SYMRK in roots of L. japonicus. Epistasis tests revealed that the dominant active SYMRK allele initiates signalling independently of either the NFR1 or NFR5 gene and upstream of a set of genes required for the generation or decoding of calcium-spiking in both symbioses. Notably, only SYMRK overexpression triggered the expression of AM-related genes, indicating that the receptors play a key role in the decision between AM- or root nodule symbiosis-development.

    The study also found that the SYMRK-mediated loss of signalling specificity may be explained by simultaneous complex formation of SYMRK with NFR1 and NFR5, and related LysM-RLKs that mediate recognition of signals from the AM fungus. This complex formation may result in the release of both negative regulatory mechanisms, or by an unbalanced stoichiometry of SYMRK and putative specific negative regulators of AM- and root nodule symbiosis signalling. The results of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms that govern the development of symbiotic relationships between plants and microorganisms, and highlight the importance of SYMRK in the decision between AM- or root nodule symbiosis-development.
A new software toolkit called DeepPoseKit has been developed to enable fast and robust animal pose estimation using deep learning methods. The toolkit addresses the limitations of current animal pose estimation methods in terms of speed and robustness. DeepPoseKit uses an efficient multi-scale deep-learning model called Stacked DenseNet and a fast GPU-based peak-detection algorithm to estimate keypoint locations with subpixel precision. This approach improves processing speed by more than two times compared to existing methods without compromising accuracy. The toolkit is designed to be easy to use and has been demonstrated to perform well on multiple challenging animal pose estimation tasks in laboratory and field settings, including groups of interacting individuals. DeepPoseKit has the potential to reduce barriers to using advanced tools for measuring behavior and has broad applicability across the behavioral sciences. The software package is freely available under a permissive open-source license and includes code, documentation, and examples. The Stacked DenseNet model used in DeepPoseKit can run inference at a rate of 30–110 Hz, depending on the resolution of the predicted confidence maps, making it suitable for real-time applications. In contrast to conventional computer-vision methods, deep-learning–based methods can achieve near human-level accuracy by manually annotating data and training a general-purpose image-processing algorithm. DeepPoseKit builds on the state-of-the-art for individual pose estimation using convolutional neural networks and offers considerable improvements over existing models. The toolkit has the potential to lead to larger, higher-resolution, and more reliable behavioral datasets that could revolutionize our understanding of relationships between behavior, the brain, and the environment.
The activation of innate immune cells, such as macrophages, in response to environmental stresses or infection by pathogens triggers the assembly of inflammasomes, which are multi-protein complexes responsible for initiating pro-inflammatory responses and a form of cell death termed pyroptosis. A recent study has identified an intracellular trigger of NLRP3-mediated inflammatory signaling, IL-1β production, and pyroptosis in primed murine bone marrow-derived macrophages that is mediated by the disruption of glycolytic flux. This disruption results from a drop in NADH levels and the induction of mitochondrial reactive oxygen species ( ROS ) production, which can be rescued by the addition of products that restore NADH production. The study found that the intracellular pathogen Salmonella typhimurium impairs NADH production, leading to the formation of mitochondrial ROS that are essential for NLRP3 inflammasome activation. This suggests that disruption of glycolytic flux is a biologically relevant trigger of NLRP3 inflammasome activation that is mediated by mitochondrial redox changes, revealing a mechanistic link between cellular metabolism and the initiation of inflammation. Furthermore, the study suggests that innate immune cells sense perturbed metabolite production and flux through the glycolytic pathway, activating NLRP3 to initiate inflammatory responses. Inhibitors of flux-limiting enzymes and S. typhimurium effectively limited glycolysis through distinct mechanisms, each resulting in NLRP3-mediated inflammasome formation and pyroptosis. The study also found that the NAD+ consumption rate increased upon pyruvate treatment, indicating induction of productive metabolism downstream of glycolysis in infected BMDMs. These data indicate that glycolytic perturbation is a mechanism by which innate immune cells sense altered homeostasis during S. typhimurium infection and induce a pro-inflammatory response via NLRP3 inflammasome formation and pyroptotic cell death. Overall, the study reveals an important inflammatory signaling network used by immune cells to sense metabolic dysfunction or infection by intracellular pathogens.
The PALB2 protein plays a crucial role in maintaining chromosomal integrity by facilitating homology-directed repair (HDR) of DNA double-strand breaks (DSBs). PALB2 interacts with BRCA1 and BRCA2, two tumor suppressors linked to breast cancer and Fanconi anemia, to coordinate their functions during HDR. In addition to its role as a scaffold protein, PALB2 directly binds to DNA, but the functional significance of this interaction has been poorly understood. Recent studies have identified a major DNA-binding site of PALB2, mutations in which reduce RAD51 foci formation and HDR efficiency by 50% in cells. The N-terminal DNA-binding domain (N-DBD) of PALB2 stimulates the function of RAD51 recombinase and exhibits strand exchange activity without the presence of RAD51. Furthermore, the N-DBD can facilitate both forward and inverse strand exchange and can use RNA as a substrate. These findings reveal a novel activity of PALB2 and highlight the importance of PALB2 DNA binding for chromosome repair in cells. The PALB2 N-DBD is essential for the recruitment of BRCA2 and RAD51 to DSB sites and efficient DNA repair in cells. Mutations in the DNA-binding residues of PALB2 significantly reduce RAD51 foci formation and HDR efficiency, suggesting that PALB2 interaction with DNA is critical for its function. The PALB2 N-DBD stimulates RAD51-mediated strand exchange in vitro, and its DNA-binding activity is necessary for the recruitment of BRCA2 and RAD51 to DSB sites and efficient DNA repair in cells. Overall, these findings provide new insights into the role of PALB2 in maintaining chromosomal integrity and its potential as a target for cancer therapy.
Bacterial actin homologue MreB forms antiparallel double filaments in vitro and in vivo. Unlike other actin-like proteins, which assemble from pairs of protofilaments in a parallel fashion, MreB forms pairs of protofilaments that are arranged in an antiparallel manner. This unique architecture is revealed by crystal structures of Caulobacter MreB and confirmed by 3D cryo-EM. The antiparallel arrangement of MreB protofilaments allows each subunit to interact with the membrane and membrane-bound components of the elongasome. This non-polar nature of MreB filaments also explains common assembly and disassembly kinetics for both filament ends. The mechanism of protofilament formation of C. crescentus MreB is elucidated by crystallography and shown to be similar to that of other actin-like proteins, involving domain closure upon polymerization. Co-crystallization studies of Caulobacter MreB with the antimicrobial reagents A22 and MP265 reveal their inhibitory mechanism and shed light on the activation mechanism of ATP hydrolysis during polymerization. The antiparallel arrangement of protofilaments of C. crescentus MreB is unprecedented within the actin family of proteins and is essential for MreB's function in cell shape maintenance. In vivo site-specific cross-linking demonstrates the antiparallel orientation of MreB protofilaments in E. coli. The crystal structures of different nucleotide and polymerization states of Caulobacter MreB reveal conserved conformational changes accompanying antiparallel filament formation. Pairs of protofilaments of Caulobacter MreB tightly bind to membranes as shown by 3D cryo-EM. Deleting the N-terminal amphipathic helix and mutating the residues in the hydrophobic loop of Caulobacter MreB ( ΔCcMreBdh ) makes the protein less prone to aggregation. The crystal structure of Caulobacter MreB ( ΔCcMreBdh ) reveals the typical actin fold, containing two domains on either side of a nucleotide binding cleft. Both domains are separated into two subdomains, A and B.
The role of APETALA1 (AP1) in petal number robustness has been investigated in Arabidopsis thaliana and its closely related species Cardamine hirsuta. Arabidopsis thaliana exhibits invariant floral forms, including a consistent number of petals, whereas Cardamine hirsuta displays variable petal number. The genetic basis for this difference between species is unknown. This study demonstrates that divergence in the pleiotropic floral regulator AP1 can account for the species-specific difference in petal number robustness. AP1 is a key regulator of flower development in Arabidopsis thaliana, acting early to initiate flowers and later to specify floral organs. The Arabidopsis thaliana AP1 confers robustness by masking the phenotypic expression of quantitative trait loci (QTL) controlling petal number in Cardamine hirsuta. In contrast, the Cardamine hirsuta AP1 fails to complement this function, resulting in variable petal number. This large effect of AP1 is explained by epistatic interactions, where Arabidopsis thaliana AP1 masks the phenotypic expression of all petal number QTL in Cardamine hirsuta. Regulatory changes in AP1 in Cardamine hirsuta have relaxed its epistasis over alleles that cause petal number to vary, exposing cryptic genetic variation that contributes to maintaining variable petal number within natural accessions of Cardamine hirsuta. The study suggests that AP1 divergence likely exposed hidden genetic variation in Cardamine hirsuta that contributes directly to maintaining variable petal number within natural accessions of Cardamine hirsuta. The results indicate that AP1 is a gene of major effect in the evolutionary transition from a robust phenotype of four petals in Arabidopsis thaliana to a variable petal number in Cardamine hirsuta. However, AP1 polymorphisms do not contribute directly to within-species variation in Cardamine hirsuta petal number. Instead, the decanalization of petal number in Arabidopsis thaliana reflects its robustness to genetic variation. Complementation of Arabidopsis thaliana ap1 mutants with the AP1 genomic locus from Cardamine hirsuta resulted in flowers with variable petal number, suggesting that there may be variability in the gene regulatory network controlling petal number in Arabidopsis thaliana that is hidden beneath the uniformity of wild-type development. Ectopic expression of AP1 in third whorl petals of ag mutants in Cardamine hirsuta is associated with an invariant number of petals, whereas endogenous AP1 expression in whorl two of the Cardamine hirsuta floral meristem is associated with variable petal number.
Proinsulin misfolding has been identified as an early event in the progression to type 2 diabetes (T2D). The biosynthesis of insulin begins with the folding of the proinsulin precursor, which involves the formation of three evolutionarily conserved intramolecular disulfide bonds. However, a subset of proinsulin molecules in normal pancreatic islets contains at least one free cysteine thiol, indicating incomplete folding. In islets with a perturbed endoplasmic reticulum (ER) folding environment, non-native proinsulin forms intermolecular disulfide-linked complexes. These complexes are more abundant in genetically obese mice with otherwise wild-type islets and in leptin receptor-deficient mice, where they track with the onset of islet insulin deficiency and diabetes.

Studies have shown that proinsulin-Cys (B19) and Cys (A20) are necessary and sufficient for the formation of proinsulin disulfide-linked complexes. Proinsulin Cys (B19)-Cys (B19) covalent homodimers resist reductive dissociation, providing a structural basis for aberrant proinsulin complex formation. In human islets, a subfraction of proinsulin molecules bears unpaired cysteine residues, indicating incomplete folding. These improperly folded proinsulin molecules form aberrant disulfide-linked partnerships with other proinsulin molecules in the ER, resulting in misfolded proinsulin complexes.

The presence of aberrant disulfide-linked proinsulin complexes has been confirmed in the islets of human beings, as well as in model systems that alter the ER folding environment and in T2D progression prior to the onset of β-cell dedifferentiation or death. In genetically obese mice with otherwise wild-type islets, proinsulin disulfide-linked complexes are more abundant, and in leptin receptor-deficient mice, the increase in these complexes tracks with the onset of islet insulin deficiency and diabetes. Furthermore, in young, normoglycemic homozygous LepRdb/db male mice that are destined for diabetes, proinsulin disulfide-linked complexes are increased slightly above the level observed in heterozygous LepRdb/+ males that do not progress to diabetes. These findings suggest that increased proinsulin misfolding via disulfide-linked complexes is an early event associated with prediabetes that worsens with β-cell dysfunction in T2D.
The article investigates the role of Suv39h1, a histone H3 lysine 9 (H3K9)-specific methyltransferase, in assembling silent heterochromatin. Suv39h1 recognizes trimethylated H3K9 (H3K9me3) via its chromodomain (CD) and targets specific chromosomal regions. However, the detailed targeting mechanisms, especially for naïve chromatin without preexisting H3K9me3, are poorly understood. The study reveals that Suv39h1's CD (Suv39h1-CD) binds nucleic acids, with a higher affinity for RNA than DNA, and this binding is independent of its H3K9me3 recognition. Suv39h1 binds major satellite RNAs in vivo, and knockdown of major satellite RNAs lowers Suv39h1 retention on pericentromere. Mutational studies indicate that both the nucleic acid-binding and H3K9me-binding activities of Suv39h1-CD are crucial for its pericentric heterochromatin assembly. The results suggest that chromatin-bound RNAs contribute to creating SUV39H's target specificity. Suv39h1's nucleic acid-binding activity integrates and cooperates with its recognition and binding of methylated H3K9 to establish/maintain Suv39h1-mediated pericentric heterochromatin, including H3K9me3 formation, HP1 accumulation, and transcriptionally repressive status. The study also shows that Suv39h1's RNA-binding activity contributes to its stable association with H3K9me3-rich pericentromeric regions. Knockdown of major satellite repeat transcripts further supports the notion that RNA binding of Suv39h1 contributes to its stability on chromatin. The results demonstrate that the four different activities of Suv39h1, including nucleic acid-binding, H3K9me-binding, HP1-binding, and chromatin-binding, cooperatively regulate Suv39h1-mediated pericentric heterochromatin assembly.
Title: Entorhinal cortex receptive fields are modulated by spatial attention, even without movement

    The entorhinal cortex, a region of the brain involved in spatial navigation and memory, contains grid cells that allow for the precise decoding of position in space. These cells have been hypothesized to contribute to mental operations beyond navigation, but this requires that their activity does not depend on physical movement. A recent study investigated whether grid cell activity in the entorhinal cortex is modulated by spatial attention, even in the absence of physical movement.

    In the study, two rhesus macaque monkeys were trained to perform a task of covert attention, where they maintained central fixation while attending to a small dot moving around a computer monitor. The researchers recorded the activity of 141 neurons in the entorhinal cortex while the monkeys performed the task. The results showed that a significant population of entorhinal cells (20/141, 14%) exhibited spatially structured receptive fields, characterized by a triangular tiling of space. This is in contrast to the hippocampus, where grid-like representations were not observed.

    The study's findings provide evidence that neurons in the macaque entorhinal cortex do not rely on physical movement to elicit spatial receptive fields. This is consistent with the hypothesis that grid cells participate in a range of cognitive functions, including attention. The researchers suggest that the activation of cells in the entorhinal cortex with spatially structured firing fields does not require physical movement through an environment. Previous studies have shown that grid cells can be activated by eye movements in stationary monkeys, and the neural substrate for overt and covert attention is largely overlapping.

    The study's results have implications for our understanding of the neural basis of spatial representation and attention. Grid cells in the entorhinal cortex are potentially capable of representing not only gaze location but also the locus of attention in general. The findings provide strong evidence that the activation of cells in the entorhinal cortex with spatially structured firing fields does not require physical movement, supporting the hypothesis that grid cells contribute to mental operations beyond navigation.
The article reports on the development and implementation of a standardized task to measure decision-making in mice, with the goal of achieving reproducible results across multiple laboratories. The task, designed for head-fixed mice, assesses both perceptual and value-based decision-making. To ensure reproducibility, a standardized training protocol and experimental hardware, software, and procedures were adopted across seven laboratories in three countries. A total of 140 mice were trained, and over 5 million choices were collected into a publicly available database. The results show that while learning speed varied across mice and laboratories, once training was complete, there were no significant differences in behavior across laboratories. Mice from different laboratories exhibited similar reliance on visual stimuli, past successes and failures, and estimates of stimulus prior probability to guide their choices. These findings demonstrate that a complex mouse behavior can be reproduced across multiple laboratories, establishing a standard for reproducible rodent behavior. The study also provides an unprecedented dataset and open-access tools for studying decision-making in mice. Furthermore, the results suggest a path toward achieving reproducibility in neuroscience through collaborative open-science approaches. To facilitate reuse and reproducibility, the experimental equipment, software components, and protocols are described and shared with the broader community. The study aims to leverage the arsenal of genetic, imaging, and physiological tools available for mouse brains to explore the neural basis of decision-making in multiple laboratories. By comparing results across laboratories, researchers can identify differences that are unlikely to be due to variations in behavior, and the resources provided are expected to catalyze the development of new adaptations and variations of the approach, ultimately accelerating the use of mice in high-quality, reproducible studies of neural correlates of decision-making.
The article presents a comprehensive analysis of the intestinal transcriptome of Schmidtea mediterranea, a planarian flatworm capable of regenerating all organs, including the gut. Using laser-capture microdissection (LCM) and RNA sequencing (RNA-seq), the authors identified hundreds of genes with intestinal expression that were previously undetected by other approaches. These genes are involved in various physiological roles, including metabolism, nutrient storage and transport, and innate immunity, demonstrating a high degree of functional homology with the digestive systems of other animals, including humans.

Systematic analyses revealed extensive conservation of digestive physiology and cell types across species, including the presence of intestinal goblet cells. Spatial LCM enabled the authors to uncover regionalization of gene expression in the planarian intestine along the medio-lateral axis, particularly among intestinal goblet cells. Two intestine-enriched transcription factors, gli-1 and RREB2, were identified as playing distinct roles in goblet cell differentiation and maintenance. The depletion of goblet cells was found to reduce the planarians' willingness to feed and viability, while having negligible effects on the regeneration of other intestinal cell types or non-intestinal tissues.

The study provides a foundational resource for investigating mechanisms involved in gastrointestinal function, repair, and regeneration. The authors identified 22 intestine-enriched transcription factors, including several required for the production and/or maintenance of goblet cells, setting the stage for further studies of this cell type. The characterization of the planarian intestinal transcriptome enables the identification of genes required for regeneration and functions of distinct intestinal cell types. The findings reinforce the evolutionary conservation of intestinal gene expression and indicate that physiological roles performed by multiple human digestive organs are consolidated in the planarian intestine.
In a study on the sensory coding mechanisms of weakly electric fish, researchers investigated how evolutionary divergence in sensory perception relates to physiological coding of stimuli. Mormyrid fish, which can detect subtle variations in electric communication signals, were found to encode signal waveform into spike-timing differences between sensory receptors. In contrast, species insensitive to waveform variation produce spontaneously oscillating potentials in their receptors. These oscillating receptors respond to electric pulses by resetting their phase, resulting in transient synchrony among receptors that encodes signal timing and location, but not waveform. The receptors were most sensitive to frequencies found only in the collective signals of groups of conspecifics, and this was correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies. The findings suggest that different perceptual capabilities correspond to different receptor physiologies. The researchers hypothesize that these divergent mechanisms represent adaptations for different social environments. The study provides the first evidence for sensory coding through oscillatory synchrony.

    The study describes a novel mechanism for peripheral sensory coding in which sensory receptors with spontaneous oscillatory activity respond to stimuli with phase resets that result in transient synchrony across the population. The degree of synchrony provides information about pulse timing and polarity, but it does not reflect the precise timing cues within the pulse waveform. This is consistent with the inability of these fish to behaviorally detect EOD waveform variation. The researchers investigated how evolutionary divergence in sensory perception relates to differences in peripheral sensory coding. Mormyrid fish communicate by producing a species-specific electric organ discharge (EOD) at variable interpulse intervals (IPIs). However, oscillating receptors are tuned to much lower frequencies than the peak power frequencies of conspecific EODs. Furthermore, these oscillation frequencies confer enhanced sensitivity to IPIs that can only be produced by groups of fish. Therefore, whereas spiking receptors are specialized for detecting signals from individuals, oscillating receptors appear specialized for detecting signals from a large group.

    Behavioral playback experiments demonstrated that a species with oscillating receptors responded strongly to submillisecond IPIs within the range of enhanced receptor sensitivity. The results suggest that this novel peripheral coding mechanism may represent an adaptation for detecting communication signals from large groups of fish. Oscillatory electrical activity is widespread in neural networks. If oscillating receptors facilitate detection of communication signals produced by a group of individuals, then species with oscillating receptors should exhibit selective behavioral responses to submillisecond IPIs, and species with spiking receptors should not. To test this hypothesis, the researchers performed behavioral playback experiments in which they presented a single fish with a train of 10 conspecific EODs at constant IPIs.
Bacteriophage BPP-1 is a virus that infects and kills Bordetella species, the causative agents of whooping cough in humans and respiratory diseases in other mammals. The phage has a T = 7l icosahedral capsid, approximately 670 Å in diameter, composed of a major capsid protein (MCP) and a cement protein (CP). A recent study used cryo-electron microscopy (cryoEM) to determine the three-dimensional structure of the BPP-1 head at a resolution of 3.5 Å. The resulting atomic model revealed that the MCP and CP adopt two characteristic folds of major virus lineages: jellyroll and HK97/Johnson. Notably, the topology of the Johnson fold in BPP-1 MCP is non-circularly permuted compared to the previously known topology of the Johnson fold. This new topology is likely the only feasible alternative to the old topology, as all other known atomic structures of proteins with the Johnson fold adopt the HK97 topology. The BPP-1 structure also features β-sheet augmentation and electrostatic interactions that contribute to the formation of non-covalent chainmail, unlike the covalent inter-protein linkages of the HK97 chainmail. The termini of both CP and MCP are ideally positioned for DGR-based phage-display engineering. The atomic description of the BPP-1 head will enhance bioengineering efforts, reveal non-covalent molecular interactions conducive to stable bacteriophage capsid formation, and clarify evolutionary relationships of BPP-1 MCP and CP with proteins in other viruses.
Dengue virus (DENV) is a mosquito-borne pathogen that cycles between mosquito and mammalian hosts. To understand how DENV populations adapt to these different host environments, researchers used serial passage in human and mosquito cell lines and estimated the fitness effects of all single-nucleotide variants in these populations using ultra-deep sequencing. This approach allowed them to determine the contributions of beneficial and deleterious mutations to the collective fitness of the population.

The analysis revealed that the continuous influx of deleterious mutations counterbalances the effect of rare, host-specific beneficial mutations, shaping the path of adaptation. Beneficial mutations were found to preferentially map to intrinsically disordered domains in the viral proteome and cluster to defined regions in the genome. These phenotypically redundant adaptive alleles may facilitate host-specific DENV adaptation. The evolutionary constraints described in this study mirror trends observed across DENV and Zika strains, indicating that the system recapitulates key biophysical and biological constraints shaping long-term viral evolution.

The researchers used a technique called CirSeq to characterize the fitness landscapes of DENV populations adapting to human and mosquito cells by tracing individual allele trajectories for almost all possible single nucleotide variants across the DENV genome. This analysis revealed the influence of both positive and negative selection in shaping the evolutionary paths of DENV in these distinct cellular environments. Many beneficial mutations were found to be clustered at a few specific locations in the genome, suggesting that adaptation relies on hotspots of host-specific selection.

To further analyze these hotspots of adaptation, the researchers mapped the allele fitness values on the three-dimensional structure of dengue protein E, a well-studied transmembrane protein that forms the outermost layer of the viral envelope. The results suggest that adaptation relies on host-specific beneficial mutations that are clustered in specific regions of the DENV genome and enriched in regions of the proteome that exhibit structural flexibility. The study highlights the importance of understanding the interplay between beneficial and deleterious mutations in shaping the evolution of DENV populations.
The article presents a high-throughput, single-particle tracking study using photoactivated localization microscopy (spt-PALM) and detailed trajectory analysis to investigate the diffusion and trafficking of KRasG12D, an active KRas mutant, in U2OS cells. The study reveals that KRasG12D exhibits distinct membrane domains that dictate its diffusion and trafficking properties. The results show that KRasG12D is partitioned into three mobility states: an immobile state in ~70 nm domains, an intermediate mobility state in ~200 nm domains, and a fast diffusion state in the rest of the membrane. The study also demonstrates that KRasG12D is continuously removed from the membrane via the immobile state and replenished to the fast state, reminiscent of Ras internalization and recycling. Importantly, the partitioning of KRasG12D into the three states remains invariant over a wide range of protein expression levels, indicating that the diffusion and trafficking properties of KRasG12D are maintained in a homeostatic condition.

The study further shows that the intermediate and immobile states of KRasG12D are each associated with distinct membrane domains, with measured sizes of ~70 nm and ~200 nm, respectively. These findings are consistent with previous beliefs that nanoscopic membrane domains regulate Ras organization on the membrane. Based on these results, the authors propose a new model to describe the membrane dynamics of KRasG12D, where nested membrane nanodomains dictate the diffusion and trafficking of KRasG12D, with implications for Ras multimer formation and signaling.

The study provides new insights into the spatiotemporal dynamics of KRasG12D on the membrane and sheds light on how complex nanodomains organize to dictate Ras diffusion and trafficking. The findings offer useful guidance for future experiments aimed at determining the molecular and structural identities of the Ras-associated membrane nanodomains and defining the mechanisms of Ras multimer formation and signaling. Overall, the study provides a comprehensive understanding of the membrane dynamics of KRasG12D and highlights the importance of membrane organization in regulating Ras signaling.
The formation of the Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) signaling gradient is crucial for patterning the dorsoventral (DV) embryonic axis in vertebrates and invertebrates. The prevailing view in vertebrates suggests that the BMP gradient is formed through a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists, often accompanied by ligand shuttling to generate peak signaling levels. However, a recent study in zebrafish aimed to elucidate the mechanism of BMP gradient formation by precisely quantifying the BMP activity gradient in wild-type and mutant embryos and combining these data with a mathematical model-based computational screen. The analysis ruled out a BMP shuttling mechanism and a BMP transcriptionally-informed gradient mechanism, instead supporting a source-sink mechanism. This mechanism relies on a restricted BMP antagonist distribution acting as a sink that drives BMP flux dorsally and gradient formation. The study measured Bmp2 diffusion and found that it supports the source-sink model, suggesting a new mechanism to shape BMP gradients during development.

The researchers established a robust quantitative imaging method to directly measure the BMP signaling gradient in zebrafish embryos. They integrated the results with a mathematical modeling approach, using the experiments to inform their model selection. The modeling provided information on key parameters to measure to identify the mechanism by which the BMP signaling gradient is formed. The study aimed to determine if the BMP signaling gradient in zebrafish is predominantly established by matching the BMP expression gradient, as suggested by the graded expression of bmp2b and bmp7a in wild-type embryos. However, three key results do not support this hypothesis.

The study presents a new view of the mechanism by which BMP antagonists and ligand use to establish the BMP signaling gradient patterning the DV axis in zebrafish. Bone Morphogenetic Proteins (BMPs) act as morphogens repeatedly during development, including in patterning the embryonic DV axis, the neural tube, and the Drosophila wing disc. BMP morphogen systems are established by a network of extracellular regulators. In vertebrates, the mechanism by which the BMP ligands and antagonists shape this gradient is unclear. Several potential mechanisms have been proposed, including an inverse gradient of BMP antagonists imparting the shape of the BMP signaling gradient and BMP antagonists generating peak BMP signaling levels by shuttling BMP ligand to these regions.
The lysosomal transporter MFSD1 plays a crucial role in maintaining liver homeostasis. MFSD1 is a member of the major facilitator superfamily of transporters, which mediate secondary active or passive transport processes across cellular membranes. MFSD1 is not N-glycosylated and contains a dileucine-based sorting motif necessary for its transport to lysosomes. Mfsd1 knockout mice develop splenomegaly and severe liver disease, indicating that MFSD1 is essential for normal liver function. Proteomics analysis of isolated lysosomes from Mfsd1 knockout mice revealed GLMP as a critical accessory subunit for MFSD1. GLMP is a highly glycosylated lysosomal protein of previously unknown function. The physical interaction between MFSD1 and GLMP is essential for maintaining normal levels of MFSD1 in lysosomes, and vice versa. Glmp knockout mice exhibit a similar phenotype to Mfsd1 knockout mice, suggesting that GLMP is also essential for liver homeostasis. The data indicate that MFSD1 and GLMP form a tightly linked lysosomal membrane protein transporter complex. MFSD1 is ubiquitously expressed in murine tissues and is endogenously localized in lysosomes under steady-state conditions. The transport of MFSD1 to lysosomes is mediated by a dileucine-based sorting motif in its N-terminus. Previous studies have identified a large number of novel putative lysosomal transporters, including MFSD1, which was characterized in detail in this study. The characterization of MFSD1 and its accessory subunit GLMP provides new insights into the regulation of lysosomal function and the maintenance of liver homeostasis.
The kinesin motor protein plays a crucial role in microtubule-based transport in eukaryotic cells, which is essential for various cellular processes. To understand the mechanism of kinesin's interaction with microtubules, researchers used cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) to obtain high-resolution structures of the kinesin motor domain attached to microtubules in two different states: without nucleotides (no-nucleotide) and with ATP bound (ADP•Al•Fx). These structures were compared with existing X-ray crystal structures of ADP-bound kinesin to infer a mechanistic scheme for kinesin's nucleotide-gated force-generation.

The cryo-EM reconstructions revealed that microtubule attachment is mediated by a conserved 'linchpin' residue in kinesin (N255), which triggers a 'clamshell' opening of the nucleotide cleft and the release of ADP. The binding of ATP re-closes the cleft, tightly coupling to the translocation of cargo via kinesin's 'neck linker' element. This structural transition is analogous to the nucleotide-exchange steps in myosin and F1-ATPase motors.

The researchers also identified sites in the kinesin fold that uncouple the motor's nucleotide affinity from its microtubule affinity, providing a structural basis for kinesin's nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules. Cryo-EM analysis of the ADP and ATP analog-bound states of a point mutant of the kinesin construct revealed two previously unidentified binding modes of kinesin, where the motor domain attaches tightly to the microtubule but is rotationally mobile.

The high-resolution structures of kinesin on microtubules provide a basis for understanding the nucleotide-gated force-generation mechanism of kinesin. The findings suggest that microtubules regulate kinesin's affinities for ADP and ATP by modulating the conformation of the switch loops, allowing for an elegant structural basis for kinesin's nucleotide-binding behavior on and off microtubules. These results provide new insights into the mechanism of kinesin's microtubule-attached cycle and have implications for understanding the regulation of microtubule-based transport in eukaryotic cells.
A novel protein quality control (QC) pathway has been identified at the mitochondrial outer membrane (MOM) in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. This pathway involves the recognition and degradation of misfolded proteins, which is essential for maintaining mitochondrial function. Two temperature-sensitive (ts-) peripheral MOM proteins, sen2-1HAts and sam35-2HAts, were used as model QC substrates. These proteins were found to be degraded from the MOM by the ubiquitin-proteasome system. The ubiquitination of sen2-1HAts was mediated by the ubiquitin ligase Ubr1, while sam35-2HAts was ubiquitinated primarily by San1.

    The degradation of both substrates required the SSA family of Hsp70s and the Hsp40 Sis1, indicating a role for chaperones in mitochondrial-associated degradation (MAD). Additionally, the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 AAA-ATPase complex, Doa1, and a mitochondrial pool of the transmembrane Cdc48 adaptor Ubx2 were implicated in their degradation. This study reveals a unique QC pathway that involves a combination of cytosolic and mitochondrial factors, distinguishing it from other cellular QC pathways.

    The ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation of sam35-2HAts and sen2-1HAts were found to be dependent on the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 complex, which can be recruited to substrates via Cdc48's interaction with co-factors. The Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 complex is essential for efficient proteasomal degradation of ubiquitinated mitochondrial QC substrates. In contrast, deletion of the proteasome shuttling factor Ddi1 did not affect the degradation of sam35-2HAts or sen2-1HAts, further distinguishing MAD from endoplasmic reticulum-associated degradation (ERAD).

    Mitochondrial proteins are subject to ongoing oxidative insults that can result in damage, misfolding, and dysfunction. The study found that ubiquitinated sam35-2HAts and sen2-1HAts accumulated in the mitochondrial pellet fraction under conditions of proteasome impairment, indicating that ubiquitinated mitochondrial QC substrates require the Cdc48-Npl4-Ufd1 complex for efficient proteasomal degradation.
In vivo imaging of mouse skin using an extracellular signal-regulated kinase ( ERK ) FRET biosensor has revealed the propagation of ERK activation in the epidermis. ERK is a key effector of many growth signalling pathways, and its activation is crucial for homeostasis in the epidermis and other tissues. Under steady-state conditions, bursts of ERK activation patterns were observed in the epidermis, where ERK activity radially propagated from cell to cell. This phenomenon, named Spatial Propagation of Radial ERK Activity Distribution ( SPREAD ), was found to occur at a frequency that correlated with the rate of epidermal cell division. SPREADs were also stimulated by 12-O-tetradecanoylphorbol 13-acetate ( TPA ) in a manner dependent on epidermal growth factor receptors ( EGFRs ) and their cognate ligands. Furthermore, at the wounded skin, ERK activation propagated as a trigger wave in parallel to the wound edge, suggesting that ERK activation propagation can be superimposed. The study also found that SPREADs were associated with G2/M cell cycle progression. The results provide new insights into how cell proliferation and transient ERK activity are synchronised in a living tissue. The ERK activation pattern originated from a few cells and radially propagated mainly in the basal layer of the epidermis and to a lesser extent in the suprabasal layer. SPREADs were also observed in the epidermis of backskin, indicating that SPREADs are generally observed in mouse epidermis. The study exploited transgenic mice expressing a FRET biosensor of ERK to monitor ERK activity in epidermal cells at single cell resolution. Long time lapse imaging of intact epidermis revealed radial propagation of ERK activity, where ERK activation originates from a few cells and propagates radially up to a radius of about 50 μm over 30 min until disappearance. The study proposes how dynamically growth signal is regulated in steady-state and wounded skin in a living tissue by showing two types of novel ERK activation patterns.
The cyanobacterial circadian clock has been found to follow midday in both in vivo and in vitro experiments. The circadian clock is a biological oscillation that regulates daily changes in physiology, but its entrainment to external cycles is not well understood. In this study, the authors developed experimental platforms to drive the cyanobacterial circadian clock in vivo and in vitro, and found that the phase of the circadian rhythm follows a simple scaling law in light-dark cycles, tracking midday across conditions with variable day length. The core biochemical oscillator comprised of the Kai proteins behaves similarly when driven by metabolic pulses in vitro, indicating that such dynamics are intrinsic to these proteins. A general mathematical framework was developed based on instantaneous transformation of the clock cycle by external cues, which successfully predicts clock behavior under many cycling environments. This framework captures the entrained behavior of the circadian clock quantitatively with a small number of parameters, and can be used to guide the design of experiments that probe the molecular origins of key mathematical features. The cyanobacterial clock's precisely defined nature facilitates such experiments, and the model is expected to be useful for other organisms as well. Biochemical simulation of seasonal effects in the reconstituted KaiABC oscillator showed a similar linear scaling of clock phase with simulated day length, indicating that core mechanisms for seasonal adaptation are intrinsic to the Kai proteins. The data reduction achieved in the linear model holds the promise of predicting the behavior of the circadian rhythms in many time-varying environments from a minimal data set that characterizes oscillator response, and may be applicable to clocks in many organisms. The analysis suggests that a key determinant of entrained behavior is how the clock limit cycle is deformed by coupling to the environment.
The loss of the transcription factor Meis1 has been identified as a risk factor for sudden cardiac death (SCD), a leading cause of premature death in the general population. Genome-wide association studies have implicated Meis1 in the regulation of cardiac rhythmicity and susceptibility to SCD. To investigate the role of Meis1 in cardiac regulation, researchers inactivated Meis1 in the neural crest of mice and observed an altered sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac rhythmicity in adults, characterized by chronotropic incompetence and cardiac conduction defects. These defects increased the susceptibility to SCD.

    Further investigation revealed that Meis1 is a major regulator of sympathetic target-field innervation and that Meis1-deficient sympathetic neurons die by apoptosis from early embryonic stages to perinatal stages. The researchers also found that Meis1 regulates the transcription of key molecules necessary for the endosomal machinery, and that the traffic of Rab5+ endosomes is severely altered in Meis1-inactivated sympathetic neurons. These results suggest that Meis1 interacts with various trophic factors signaling pathways during postmitotic neurons differentiation.

    The study demonstrated that Meis1 acts independently of the well-studied network of transcription factors governing early specification of sympathetic neurons to orchestrate later phases of neuronal differentiation. The loss of Meis1 function in early sympathetic neurons leads to an imbalanced sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac functions in adult mice, resulting in increased susceptibility to SCD. The researchers also found that following specific inactivation of Meis1 in peripheral neurons, the traffic of Rab5+ early endosomes is impaired and sympathetic target-field innervation is compromised.

    These findings complement previous studies demonstrating Meis1 function in the regulation of neonatal cardiomyocytes mitosis, particularly following myocardial infarction. However, cardiac functions were not affected following specific Meis1 inactivation or overexpression in cardiomyocytes. The study highlights the importance of Meis1 in the regulation of sympathetic neurons and its role in mediating susceptibility to SCD. The results suggest that Meis1 is a novel actor in sympathetic neurons differentiation and that its loss can lead to an imbalance in sympatho-vagal regulation of cardiac functions, increasing the risk of SCD.
The formation of biofilms, surface-attached bacterial communities, exhibits a wide range of morphologies. Despite the identification of structural and regulatory components necessary for biofilm formation, the mechanisms by which these components contribute to biofilm surface morphology are not well understood. This study investigates the morphogenesis of biofilms using Vibrio cholerae as a model system, combining mechanical measurements, theory, simulation, quantitative image analysis, surface energy characterization, and mutagenesis. The results show that mechanical instabilities, including wrinkling and delamination, play a crucial role in biofilm morphogenesis. These instabilities are driven by differences in interfacial energies, which dictate the creation or annihilation of new or existing interfaces. Feedback between mechanomorphogenesis and biofilm expansion shapes the overall biofilm contour. The study suggests that exploiting mechanical principles to drive key morphogenic events is an ancient process, occurring in bacteria and reused in eukaryotes. Biofilms represent a tractable model system for investigating the general role of mechanical forces in morphogenesis and provide a convenient system for morpho-engineering. The study proposes a mechanomorphogenesis model in which biofilms first wrinkle and then delaminate as growth builds up compressive stress. This model predicts that changing the spatial distribution of mechanical stress can alter biofilm topography.
