The study also highlights the potential for discovering new genes and compounds that alter biofilm morphology by altering mechanics to address biofilm-related problems. The findings have implications for understanding morphogenesis processes in higher organisms and suggest that mechanical instability and interfacial energy are key drivers of biofilm morphogenesis.
The neuron-restrictive silencer factor (NRSF), also known as REST, is a transcription factor that plays a crucial role in regulating gene expression in neurons. Recent studies have shown that NRSF is involved in the development of epilepsy, a neurological disorder characterized by abnormal electrical activity in the brain. In this study, researchers investigated the mechanisms by which NRSF contributes to epileptogenesis, the process of generating epileptic neuronal networks.

The study found that severe seizures, which can lead to epilepsy, increase the levels of NRSF in the mature hippocampus, a region of the brain involved in memory and learning. However, surprisingly, this increase in NRSF levels did not lead to the repression of all potential NRSF target genes. Instead, only a small subset of these genes, approximately 10%, were repressed by NRSF in the context of seizure-induced epilepsy. The repression of these genes was rescued when NRSF binding to chromatin was blocked, suggesting that NRSF is directly responsible for their repression.

Further analysis revealed that the subset of genes repressed by NRSF had mid-range binding frequencies to the repressor, a property that made them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NRSF levels. This selective regulation of NRSF target genes may play a critical role in influencing neuronal properties in both pathological and physiological contexts. The genes selectively regulated by NRSF during epileptogenesis coded for ion channels, receptors, and other crucial contributors to neuronal function, suggesting that their repression may contribute to the pathogenesis of epilepsy.

The study used a combination of large-scale transcriptome arrays, qPCR validation, chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP), and gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) to examine the basis of NRSF-dependent gene expression changes during epileptogenesis. The results showed that a twofold to threefold increase in NRSF levels, induced by epilepsy-provoking seizures, led to the repression of only a subset of potentially regulated genes, and these were rescued by interfering with NRSF function. Overall, the study provides new insights into the role of NRSF in epileptogenesis and highlights the importance of dynamic, selective regulation of NRSF target genes in influencing neuronal properties.
The mammalian olfactory system processes sensory information through parallel pathways formed by mitral cells (MCs) and tufted cells (TCs) in the olfactory bulb. These parallel pathways are a common feature of many sensory systems, but how local circuit activity creates stimulus selectivity in these pathways remains poorly understood. This study investigated the parallel pathways formed by MCs and TCs in mice and characterized the emergence of feature selectivity in these cell types via distinct lateral inhibitory circuits. The results show that differences in activity-dependent lateral inhibition between MCs and TCs likely reflect newly described differences in the activation of deep and superficial granule cells. Simulations suggest that these circuit-level differences allow MCs and TCs to best discriminate odors in separate concentration ranges, indicating that segregating information about different ranges of stimulus intensity may be an important function of these parallel sensory pathways. The study proposes a mechanism of activity-dependent lateral inhibition in MCs and TCs, where the levels of activity in the olfactory bulb circuit consisting of M72 M/TCs (presynaptic M/TCs) and the recorded M/TCs (postsynaptic M/TCs) and the associated GCs (granule cells) are schematically represented. The findings suggest that the combination of activity-dependent lateral inhibition at both low and intermediate rates enables TCs and MCs to collectively encode odors better than either population alone and supports novel computations that are unlikely to occur with a single neuron type. The study highlights the importance of understanding how local circuits support feature selectivity in parallel streams, particularly in brain areas where distinct outputs are generated from initially homogeneous sources. The results also suggest that the mechanisms behind parallel processing may be shared across sensory systems, such as the visual system, where feature selectivity in each ganglion cell type emerges due to its connectivity to specific bipolar and amacrine cell types.
The M2 muscarinic receptor, a prototypic model of allostery in G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs), has been studied to elucidate the molecular and supramolecular determinants of allosteric effects. Allosteric interactions were investigated in monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor using a FRET-based sensor of conformation at the allosteric site and pharmacological assays involving mutants engineered to preclude intramolecular effects. The results showed that allosteric effects in monomers were exclusively negative and derived primarily from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between the allosteric and orthosteric ligands. In contrast, allosteric effects in oligomers could be positive or negative, depending on the allosteric-orthosteric pair, and arose from interactions within and between the constituent protomers.

Molecular dynamics simulations at the atomic level were used to examine the electrostatic, steric, and conformational determinants of allostery. The simulations started from the crystal structure of the monomeric M2 receptor occupied by the agonist iperoxo (4MQS). The ligand and accessory proteins were removed, and conformations of the receptor were sampled from a canonical ensemble accessible within a period of 30 ns. The simulations revealed that the complex behavior of oligomers is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations.

The M2 muscarinic receptor has been a prototype for studying allosteric effects within the broader family of GPCRs. Most studies of the interaction of an allosteric ligand with the allosteric site have been based on the modulation of events at the orthosteric site, typically on receptors in native membranes or detergent-solubilized extracts. However, the oligomeric nature of the M2 receptor is evident in the multiphasic effects of allosteric ligands on the dissociation rate of orthosteric antagonists and their binding at equilibrium. To understand how a multimeric complex might account for allosteric behavior that resists explanation in terms of monomers, the study compared positive and negative allosteric modulators for their effects on oligomers and purified monomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor.
The study investigated the role of SETD2, a histone methyltransferase that trimethylates histone H3 lysine 36 (H3K36me3), in the DNA damage response (DDR) following DNA double-strand breaks (DSBs). The results show that SETD2 is required for the activation of ATM, a key kinase involved in the DDR, and for the recruitment of 53BP1 to sites of DSBs. Furthermore, SETD2 is necessary for the formation of RAD51 presynaptic filaments, which are essential for homologous recombination repair of DSBs. In clear cell renal cell carcinoma (ccRCC) cells, depletion of SETD2 impairs DNA damage signaling and leads to decreased cell survival after DNA damage. Notably, despite the persistence of DNA lesions, SETD2-deficient cells fail to activate p53, a tumor suppressor protein that is rarely mutated in ccRCC. This suggests that loss of SETD2 may provide an alternative mechanism for the inactivation of the p53-mediated checkpoint without the need for additional genetic mutations in TP53. The study reveals a novel role for SETD2 in priming intact chromatin for the response to DNA damage and provides a functional link between altered chromatin states and the mechanisms that maintain genome integrity. The findings have implications for understanding the impact of SETD2 mutations in cancer, particularly in ccRCC, where a high rate of genomic alterations occurs in parallel to inactivating mutations and deletions of histone modifiers, such as SETD2.
A missense variant in the Rasgrp1 gene, Rasgrp1Anaef, has been identified as a major source of genetic variation in mice. This mutation affects the EF hand of the Rasgrp1 Ras guanine nucleotide exchange factor, leading to the accumulation of a distinct population of Helios+ PD-1+ CD4+ T cells and the production of anti-nuclear autoantibodies. In contrast to previously described autoimmune mutations in Rasgrp1, Zap70, or Lat, Rasgrp1Anaef has no detectable effect on thymocyte development in mice with normal TCR repertoires. However, it results in the peripheral accumulation of a distinct population of Helios+ PD-1+ T-helper cells and the production of anti-nuclear autoantibodies. Notably, Rasgrp1Anaef increases tonic mTOR signaling in naïve CD4+ T cells, which is distinct from its well-known role in thymic positive selection.

    Biochemical and genetic studies reveal that overactive mTOR signaling within naïve CD4 T cells is a key component driving two abnormalities: a constitutive increase in CD44 expression in naïve CD4 T cells and a gradual accumulation of peripheral Helios+ CD44hi CD4 cells and autoantibodies. The effect of Rasgrp1Anaef on Ras/ERK signaling in vivo is much milder than expected from its effects in in vitro assays. Genetic reduction of mTOR function in Rasgrp1Anaef mice normalizes CD44 expression on naïve CD4+ T cells and abolishes excessive accumulation of effector T cells and autoantibodies, demonstrating a central role for increased mTOR activity in driving immune dysregulation in Rasgrp1Anaef mice.

    Thymocyte selection in Rasgrp1Anaef mice is mostly normal in vivo, although CD44 is overexpressed on naïve thymocytes and T cells in a T-cell-autonomous manner. A novel mouse strain, chino, with a reduction-of-function mutation in Mtor, was used to identify CD44 expression as a sensitive reporter of tonic mTOR-S6 kinase signaling. Elevated tonic mTOR-S6 signaling occurs in Rasgrp1Anaef naïve CD4+ T cells, and CD44 expression, CD4+ T cell subset ratios, and serum autoantibodies all returned to normal in Rasgrp1AnaefMtorchino double-mutant mice, demonstrating that increased mTOR activity is essential for the Rasgrp1Anaef T cell dysregulation. These findings reveal a new role for Rasgrp1 in the cell-intrinsic regulation of peripheral CD4+ T cells and dissociate this new function of Rasgrp1 from its well-known role in thymic positive selection.
The study investigated the role of Arid1a, a subunit of the SWI/SNF chromatin remodeling complex, in the initiation of pancreatic ductal adenocarcinoma (PDAC) in the presence of oncogenic Kras mutations. The researchers used a mouse model to suppress Arid1a expression in adult acinar cells harboring KrasG12D mutations and found that this led to rapid and irreversible reprogramming of acinar cells into mucinous PDAC precursor lesions. In contrast, silencing Arid1a during embryogenesis, concurrent with KrasG12D activation, resulted in retention of acinar cell fate. These findings suggest that Arid1a acts as a critical modulator of Kras-dependent changes in acinar cell identity and that the timing and genetic context of Arid1a inactivation influence the effects of SWI/SNF complex alterations in epithelial tumorigenesis.

Suppression of Arid1a in adult acinar cells with oncogenic Kras mutations accelerated acinar-to-ductal reprogramming and specification of mucinous pancreatic cancer precursor lesions, which could not be reversed by restoring endogenous Arid1a expression. Alcian blue staining confirmed the retention of mucinous lesions in Arid1a-restored mice. These results indicate that Arid1a loss enables a non-reversible reprogramming of cell fate that is dependent on cellular and genetic context.

The study also investigated the mechanism by which Arid1a regulates pancreas cell identity. Perturbation of the SWI/SNF chromatin-remodeling complex is expected to impact the expression of SWI/SNF target genes. Transcriptional and chromatin profiling data suggested that Arid1a disruption, in the presence of oncogenic Kras, reduces the chromatin accessibility of key transcription factors to the enhancers of genes needed to sustain acinar cell fate. These findings imply that genomic areas rendered accessible by Arid1a harbor elements involved in preventing acinar-to-ductal trans-differentiation in the context of mutant Kras, a critical rate-limiting step in Kras-driven pancreatic neoplasia.

The study demonstrates that acute loss of Arid1a in the setting of a pre-existing Kras mutation rapidly perturbs the identity and function of pancreatic acinar cells, enhancing their sensitivity to metaplastic and PanIN-inducing signals stemming from oncogenic Kras. The results suggest that Arid1a depletion can modulate the early stages of pancreatic neoplasia in a context-specific manner that depends on the timing of Arid1a inactivation and the presence of oncogenic Kras.
The study investigated the effects of social isolation on the intrinsic properties of corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons in the paraventricular nucleus (PVN) of the hypothalamus in preadolescent mice. The researchers found that social isolation for less than 24 hours increased the first spike latency (FSL) and decreased the excitability of CRH neurons in female mice, but not in age-matched male mice. In contrast, acute physical stress, induced by swim stress, increased FSL in both group-housed females and single-housed males. The increase in FSL following social isolation or acute physical stress was blocked by the glucocorticoid synthesis inhibitor, metyrapone, and mimicked by exogenous corticosterone. These findings suggest that social isolation, but not acute physical stress, has sex-specific effects on PVN CRH neurons.

    The study used acute brain slices to examine the synaptic and intrinsic properties of identified CRH neurons in the PVN. Activation of these cells is essential for launching the neuroendocrine response to stress. The researchers hypothesized that female mice, but not male mice, may interpret social isolation as a stress. To test this idea, single-housed male and female mice were subjected to swim stress for 20 minutes, and the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons were assessed. The results showed that males and females react differently to some stressors but not others. Specifically, social isolation has definitive, CORT-dependent effects on CRH neurons in female, but not male mice. By contrast, both males and females show indistinguishable responses to an acute physical swim stress. Additionally, a prior social isolation occludes the effects of subsequent physical stress on FSL in females, but has no effect on FSL in males.

    The study's findings demonstrate that social isolation, but not acute physical stress, has sex-specific effects on PVN CRH neurons. The results suggest that female mice may interpret social isolation as a stress, leading to changes in the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons. In contrast, male mice do not exhibit similar changes in response to social isolation. These findings have implications for understanding the sex-specific effects of social isolation on the neuroendocrine response to stress.
Hemoglobin (Hb) is a model protein for studying molecular adaptation in vertebrates, and its affinity and cooperativity for oxygen binding are crucial for tissue oxygen delivery. Previous research suggested that only Hb affinity determines molecular adaptations, but a new study reveals that both affinity and cooperativity play a role in optimizing tissue oxygen delivery. The study derived the relationship between the Hill coefficient and the relative affinity and conformational changes parameters of the Monod-Wymann-Changeux allosteric model, creating a ‘biophysical Hill landscape’ that describes this relation. The results show that mammalian Hb cooperativity values reside on a ridge of maximum cooperativity along this landscape, allowing for both gross- and fine-tuning of tissue oxygen unloading to meet the distinct metabolic requirements of mammalian tissues for oxygen. This mechanism underlies body size-related adaptation of mammalian Hb. The study found that the maximum cooperativity point of Hb has been tuned during evolution to satisfy the particular physiological requirements of different tissues for oxygen supply. In small-sized animals with high metabolic rates, Hb presents lower affinity for oxygen than in large-sized animals, reflecting the adaptive adjustment of tissue oxygen unloading by Hb. The study also found that the greater an animal’s metabolic rate, the higher the observed maximum Hill cooperativity value of oxygen binding by Hb, and an inverse linear correlation was found between the metabolic rate and Hb affinity. These findings support the hypothesis that changes in mammalian Hb binding and conformation have been tuned during evolution to give rise to close to maximum cooperativity in oxygen binding, yielding a different maximum nH value in each case. The study provides evolutionary and functional insights into the mechanism underlying body-size-related adaptation of mammalian Hb.
Malaria infection begins with the injection of Plasmodium sporozoites into the skin of a mammalian host by an Anopheles mosquito. The process of how sporozoites locate and enter a blood vessel is a critical but poorly understood step in the infection process. A recent study used intravital microscopy in mice to examine sporozoite motility and their interaction with dermal blood vessels. The results suggest that sporozoites exhibit two types of motility: avascular motility and perivascular motility. Avascular motility is characterized by high speed and less confinement, resulting in maximal displacements in areas far from blood vessels. In contrast, perivascular motility is more constrained and observed when the parasite glides in proximity to a blood vessel. The study found that the curvature of sporozoite tracks engaging with vasculature optimizes contact with dermal capillaries. Imaging of sporozoites with mutations in key adhesive proteins highlighted the importance of the sporozoite's gliding speed and its ability to modulate adhesive properties for successful exit from the inoculation site. The gliding motility of Plasmodium sporozoites is essential for their infectivity, and previous reports have observed sporozoite motility in the dermis. However, an in-depth analysis of their motility over time has not been performed until now. Intravital imaging of sporozoites over the first 120 minutes after inoculation revealed significant changes in their motility in this time frame. The data demonstrate that sporozoite motility can change in response to environmental signals encountered by the sporozoite. Mutant sporozoites that display modest changes in motility pattern or speed have dramatic decreases in blood vessel invasion and infectivity. These findings suggest that exit from the inoculation site presents an important barrier for the sporozoite and is therefore an opportune time to intervene. The study provides new insights into how the sporozoite navigates through and out of the dermis, and highlights the importance of gliding speed and adhesive properties for successful blood vessel invasion.
In a study on the evolutionary adaptation of Saccharomyces cerevisiae to the removal of the polarity gene BEM1, researchers observed a reproducible trajectory of adaptive mutations that led to a substantial recovery in cell polarization. BEM1 is a relatively young component in polarity establishment, and its deletion revealed alternative, more evolutionarily ancient polarization modules. The bem1∆ lineages initially increased in fitness and then slowly reached >90% of the fitness of their BEM1 ancestors after 1000 generations of evolution. Genome sequencing and monitoring of polarization revealed a fixed sequence of adaptive mutations, each improving cell polarization by inactivating proteins. These mutations led to a substantial recovery in the speed and accuracy of cell polarization, with bem1∆ cells often containing more than one polarity site, suggesting a weaker positive feedback that ensures a single site of polarization. The results demonstrate that cells can be evolutionarily robust to physiologically destructive perturbations and suggest that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions. The study highlights the multiple layers of regulation that allow cells to polarize rapidly and accurately, which also make it possible for them to adapt to strong perturbations. Under strong selective pressure, the larger target size for mutations that inactivate proteins favors evolutionary trajectories that remove components from modules over those that quantitatively alter their properties. The findings have implications for our understanding of the evolution of cellular functions and the ability of cells to adapt to strong perturbations. The study provides evidence that cells can evolve to perform the same biological functions with different proteins, and that recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in the parts list for cell biologically important functions. The results are consistent with previous research on the loss of genes in the descent of Saccharomyces cerevisiae from an ancestor that existed 100 Mya ago, and the recruitment of new functions by adaptations that alter the function of existing genes and create genes de novo.
The neural mechanisms of economic commitment in the human medial prefrontal cortex were investigated using a multi-alternative choice task in which decisions had financial consequences that persisted over time. Participants were presented with prospects that provided sustained financial returns and had to decide whether to accept or reject them. The study aimed to understand the computational mechanisms underlying economic commitments and their neural implementation in the reward circuitry of the medial prefrontal cortex (mPFC). The results showed that the rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmPFC) encoded stimulus value only when acceptance or rejection was deferred into the future, suggesting a role in integrating value signals over time. In contrast, the dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (dACC) encoded stimulus value only when participants rejected or deferred accepting a prospect. The dACC BOLD signals reflected two decision biases: a bias to defer commitments to later and a bias to weight potential losses more heavily than gains. These biases paradoxically maximized reward in the task. The findings provide fresh insights into the pressures that shape economic decisions and the computation of value in the mPFC. The study builds on previous research that has examined the neural mechanisms of economic behavior by offering participants a succession of independent gambles or unrelated consumer choices. However, this study is the first to investigate the neural mechanisms of economic commitment, which often involves a period of deliberation before final acceptance or rejection. The results suggest that the rmPFC and dACC play distinct roles in economic decision-making, with the rmPFC integrating value signals over time and the dACC encoding value when participants reject or defer accepting a prospect. The study's findings have implications for understanding the neural basis of economic decision-making and the computation of value in the mPFC.
This study investigated the role of paraspeckles in controlling circadian gene expression in pituitary cells. Paraspeckles are nuclear bodies that form around the long non-coding RNA Neat1 and RNA-binding proteins. They are believed to control gene expression at a post-transcriptional level by retaining mRNAs containing 3’-UTR inverted repeats of Alu sequences ( IRAlu ) in the nucleus. The study found that all components of paraspeckles, including four major proteins and Neat1, displayed a circadian expression pattern in pituitary cells. Furthermore, the insertion of IRAlu at the 3’-UTR of the EGFP cDNA led to a rhythmic circadian nuclear retention of the egfp mRNA, which was lost when paraspeckles were disrupted. In contrast, the insertion of a single antisense Alu had only a weak effect. Using real-time video-microscopy, it was shown that IRAlu drove a circadian expression of EGFP protein. These findings suggest that paraspeckles control circadian gene expression at a post-transcriptional level by rhythmically retaining mRNAs in the nucleus. The study also found that the insertion of IRAlu at the 3’-UTR of the EGFP cDNA led to a rhythmic circadian nuclear retention of the egfp mRNA, which was lost when paraspeckles were disrupted. This suggests that paraspeckles play a crucial role in controlling circadian gene expression in pituitary cells. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of circadian gene expression and highlights the importance of post-transcriptional regulation in controlling gene expression. The findings of this study have implications for our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying circadian rhythms and may contribute to the development of new therapeutic strategies for circadian-related disorders.
The active-zone protein Munc13 has been found to play a crucial role in controlling the function of presynaptic voltage-gated calcium channels (VGCCs) at nerve terminals. VGCCs are essential for converting electrical signals into chemical communication, and their proper functioning is critical for synaptic transmission. Munc13, an active-zone protein involved in exocytosis, has been shown to interact with VGCCs via a pair of basic residues in its C2B domain. This interaction is essential for controlling the behavior of VGCCs during various forms of activity, including high-frequency action potential bursts and single action potential stimuli.

    The study demonstrates that the removal of Munc13 or its replacement with a Munc13 C2B mutant alters the response of synaptic VGCCs to and recovery from high-frequency action potential bursts, as well as calcium influx from single action potential stimuli. These findings illustrate a novel form of synaptic modulation and suggest that Munc13 is poised to profoundly impact information transfer at nerve terminals by controlling both vesicle priming and the trigger for exocytosis.

    The interaction between Munc13 and VGCCs has been pinpointed to a specific site on the C2B domain of Munc13, which is orthogonal to the potential membrane-interacting loops of this domain. This interaction site is critical for controlling calcium channel use-dependence on millisecond to second time scales at nerve terminals. The study also shows that Munc13 enhances Ca2+ influx and modulates kinetic properties of VGCCs across a range of stimulation conditions, suggesting that this modulation is mediated by a direct VGCC-Munc13 interaction.

    To further investigate the significance of the Munc13-VGCC interaction, the study examined the impact of removing Munc13 on action potential-driven calcium influx in nerve terminals. The results show that the removal of Munc13 leads to a complete block of exocytosis, and that VGCCs fail to reactivate in the absence of Munc13. This suggests that the increase in exocytosis during high-frequency stimulation in the absence of Munc13 is a direct effect of the lack of Munc13-VGCC interaction, rather than a secondary effect of reduced exocytosis.

    Overall, the study provides compelling evidence that Munc13 plays a critical role in controlling VGCC function and synaptic transmission, and highlights a new class of mechanisms for sculpting synaptic efficacy. The findings have broad implications for understanding how synaptic transmission is controlled, and suggest that Munc13 is a key regulator of information transfer at nerve terminals.
Plant cells in the epidermis of many plant species exhibit a complex, puzzle-shaped morphology that has long been observed but not fully understood. Recent research suggests that this shape is an adaptation to mitigate mechanical stress in tissues that grow isotropically, such as the epidermis of leaves and cotyledons. Isotropic growth, where cells expand equally in all directions, can lead to the formation of large, isodiametric cells that are prone to bulging under the stress produced by turgor pressure. To prevent this, puzzle-shaped cells with lobes and indentations emerge, effectively reducing the open area of the cell and minimizing stress on the cell wall.

    Finite Element Method (FEM) analysis of 3D pressurized cells demonstrates that cell shape significantly influences the direction and magnitude of mechanical stress exerted on the cell wall. In isotropically growing tissues, such as the epidermis of broad leaves, puzzle-shaped cells provide a solution to the problem of excessive stress. By analyzing the stress in a mechanical model of the cotyledon epidermis of Arabidopsis thaliana, the researchers found that puzzle-shaped cells allow cells to enlarge in isotropically growing tissues without inducing high stresses.

    In contrast, stems, roots, and siliques exhibit anisotropic growth, where cells can simply elongate without the need for puzzle-shaped cells. The strict correspondence between fruit shape and puzzle-shaped epidermal cells supports the prediction that puzzle shapes are required to allow cells to enlarge in isotropically growing tissues but are not necessary in elongated organs. The researchers propose that plant cells regulate their shape to prevent their LEC (a proxy for stress) from becoming too large.

    Mechanically, plant cells are similar to small balloons inflated with considerable turgor pressure, up to 10 bar in Arabidopsis leaf cells and up to 50 bar in specialized cells such as stomata. The researchers suggest that the puzzle shape is an adaptation to a developmental constraint related to the mechanical forces acting on turgid plant cells in the epidermis. Simulation models show that a mechanism actively regulating cellular stress plausibly reproduces the development of epidermal cell shape, supporting the idea that mechanical stress is a key driver of cell-shape morphogenesis.
The assembly of human pre-spliceosomes across introns and exons is a crucial step in the splicing process of pre-mRNA molecules. Human genes often contain multiple introns, which necessitate mechanisms to define exons and ensure their proper connection by spliceosomes. A recent study investigated the dynamic interactions of single pre-mRNA molecules with individual fluorescently tagged spliceosomal subcomplexes to understand how cross-intron and cross-exon processes jointly promote pre-spliceosome assembly. The study found that the U1 subcomplex bound to the 5' splice site of an intron acts jointly with U1 bound to the 5' splice site of the next intron to dramatically increase the rate and efficiency of U2 subcomplex recruitment to the branch site/3' splice site of the upstream intron. This synergy of 5' splice sites across introns and exons is likely important in promoting correct and efficient splicing of multi-intron pre-mRNAs. The study suggests an ordered human pre-spliceosome assembly pathway in which stable U2 association in the presence of ATP requires prior U1 binding, in contrast to the branched pathway observed in S. cerevisiae. The experiments reveal that the dynamics of U1 binding to the 5' splice site are largely unaffected by the presence of U2 binding sites positioned either downstream or upstream of the 5' splice site. Pre-spliceosomes can form on multi-intron pre-mRNAs through at least two different pathways: cross-intron interactions leading directly to a catalytically active spliceosome, or cross-exon interactions where the exons flanking an intron are first defined, after which cross-intron interactions between adjacent cross-exon complexes lead to spliceosome assembly. The study also shows that a major contributor to exon inclusion is collaboration between flanking 5' splice sites, which dramatically increases the rate of stable U2 recruitment during pre-spliceosome formation.
A novel ubiquitin-dependent folding system has been identified that operates on the cytosolic side of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) to assist in the folding of membrane proteins. This system involves the ubiquitination of a specific lysine residue on the Wnt signaling coreceptor LRP6, which retains it in the ER and prevents degradation. The ubiquitination of Lys-1403 on LRP6 is thought to interact with a putative ER-ubiquitin binding protein that acts as a chaperone, providing the protein with time to fold. If folding is successful, LRP6 is transported out of the ER, a process favored by palmitoylation. However, if folding is impaired or delayed, polyubiquitination of other cytosolic lysine residues takes over and targets LRP6 to ER-associated degradation (ERAD). This ubiquitination-deubiquitination assisted-folding cycle also appears to operate on other membrane proteins, such as CFTR and the anthrax toxin receptor 2, which are involved in severe human diseases. The deubiquitinating enzyme USP19 is responsible for removing ubiquitin from LRP6, allowing it to exit the ER. This system is conceptually analogous to the glucosylation-deglucosylation cycle that occurs in the ER lumen during the calnexin/calreticulin folding cycle. To avoid infinite futile cycles, folded LRP6 molecules undergo palmitoylation and ER export, while unsuccessfully folded proteins are polyubiquitinated and targeted to degradation. The ubiquitin-dependent folding system provides a novel mechanism for controlling the proteostasis of membrane proteins and has implications for understanding the folding and quality control of proteins in the ER.
Title: Sex differences in learning from exploration
    Abstract: Sex-based modulation of cognitive processes could set the stage for individual differences in vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders. While value-based decision making processes in particular have been proposed to be influenced by sex differences, the overall correct performance in decision making tasks often show variable or minimal differences across sexes. Computational tools allow us to uncover latent variables that define different decision making approaches, even in animals with similar correct performance. Here, we quantify sex differences in mice in the latent variables underlying behavior in a classic value-based decision making task: a restless two-armed bandit. While male and female mice had similar accuracy, they achieved this performance via different patterns of exploration. Male mice tended to make more exploratory choices overall, largely because they appeared to get ‘stuck’ in exploration once they had started. Female mice tended to explore less but learned more quickly during exploration. Together, these results suggest that sex exerts stronger influences on decision making during periods of learning and exploration than during stable choices. Exploration during decision making is altered in people diagnosed with addictions, depression, and neurodevelopmental disabilities, pinpointing the neural mechanisms of exploration as a highly translational avenue for conferring sex-modulated vulnerability to neuropsychiatric diagnoses.

To examine whether there are sex differences in exploration, we trained male and female mice on a classic explore/exploit task, a spatial restless two-armed bandit ( Ebitz et al., 2018 ). Males showed higher levels of exploration than females throughout the task

Here, we applied computational tools to characterize sex differences in the latent variables underlying behavior to understand sex differences in a key cognitive process regulating reward-guided behaviors: balancing exploration and exploitation. In an uncertain world, we must balance two goals: exploiting rewarding options when they are available, but also exploring alternatives that could be more rewarding or provide new information about the world

. The results of HMM model and RL models revealed significant sex differences in exploration, paralleled by sex differences in rate of learning. What remains unclear is how sex, explore-exploit states, and reward outcomes all interact together to influence the animals’ choices

Sex mechanisms biasing the preferred approaches taken during cognitive tasks are potentially significant contributors to task performance. We used a combination of computational modeling approaches to characterize sex differences in a canonical explore/exploit task. While males and females had similar performance, they used different latent explore-exploit strategies to learn about the dynamic environment

In the endoplasmic reticulum-associated protein degradation (ERAD) pathway, membrane proteins are ubiquitinated, extracted from the membrane, and degraded by the proteasome. However, the mechanism by which hydrophobic transmembrane (TM) segments are moved from the phospholipid bilayer into the cytosol, often together with hydrophilic and folded ER luminal protein parts, is not well understood. To address this question, researchers used a reconstituted system with purified proteins from Saccharomyces cerevisiae to investigate the role of the ubiquitin ligase Doa10 (Teb-4/MARCH6 in animals) in ERAD.

The results show that Doa10 is a retrotranslocase that facilitates membrane protein extraction. The TM segment of a substrate interacts with the membrane-embedded domain of Doa10 and then passively moves into the aqueous phase. In contrast, luminal substrate segments cross the membrane in an unfolded state, with their unfolding occurring on the luminal side of the membrane by cytoplasmic Cdc48 action. This process reveals how a membrane-bound retrotranslocase cooperates with the Cdc48 ATPase in membrane protein extraction.

The study used a reconstituted system to mechanistically investigate membrane protein extraction, without relying on indirect read-outs of downstream reactions such as proteasomal degradation. The results demonstrate that Doa10 is a membrane protein retrotranslocase and show how it cooperates with the Cdc48 ATPase in the extraction of proteins with folded luminal domains. The study also addresses the question of whether luminal domains are moved across the membrane in a folded state, if unfolding occurs prior to retrotranslocation, or if unfolding is directly coupled to retrotranslocation.

Doa10 is a 150 kDa protein with 14 TM segments and has been suggested to act as a retrotranslocase for membrane proteins. The study provides new insights into the ERAD pathway and the role of Doa10 in the extraction of membrane proteins. The results have implications for our understanding of protein quality control pathways in the ER and other organelles, where membrane proteins are removed from the organelle for proteasomal degradation in the cytosol.
Drosophila Hedgehog (Hh) signaling plays a crucial role in guiding development and maintaining adult tissue homeostasis in both invertebrates and vertebrates. Hh proteins induce transcriptional changes in target cells by inhibiting the proteolytic processing of full-length Drosophila Ci or mammalian Gli proteins to nuclear transcriptional repressors and activating the full-length Ci or Gli proteins. A study investigated the contributions of these mechanisms to dose-dependent Hh signaling in Drosophila wing imaginal discs using Ci variants expressed at physiological levels. The results showed that Ci variants that cannot be processed supported a normal pattern of graded target gene activation and the development of adults with normal wing morphology when supplemented by constitutive Ci repressor. This indicates that Hh can signal normally in the absence of regulated processing. Furthermore, processing-resistant Ci variants were also significantly activated in the absence of Hh by elimination of Cos2 or PKA, revealing separate inhibitory roles of these two components in addition to their well-established roles in promoting Ci processing. The study suggests that Ci can mediate normal Hh morphogen action in wing discs in the complete absence of regulated processing. The results also show that wing patterning by Hh does not require regulation of the level of either repressor or full-length Ci protein by processing. Animals lacking both Fu kinase and Su(fu) develop normal wings, although late third instar wing discs lack anterior En induction. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms of Hh signaling and its role in development and tissue homeostasis. The findings have implications for understanding the causes of developmental birth defects and cancer associated with aberrant Hh protein production, distribution, and responses.
This study investigated the role of calretinin (CR) positive neurons in the spinal cord dorsal horn in sensory processing and pain signaling. The dorsal horn is a critical area in the spinal cord where nociceptive information is relayed to higher brain centers. The researchers used an optogenetic approach to selectively activate CR interneurons, which are a subpopulation of neurons that express the calcium-binding protein calretinin. The results showed that these interneurons form an interconnected network that can initiate and sustain enhanced excitatory signaling, and directly relay signals to lamina I projection neurons. Photoactivation of CR interneurons in vivo resulted in a significant nocifensive behavior that was morphine sensitive, caused a conditioned place aversion, and was enhanced by spared nerve injury. Furthermore, halorhodopsin-mediated inhibition of these interneurons elevated sensory thresholds. These findings suggest that dorsal horn circuits that involve excitatory CR neurons are important for the generation and amplification of pain and identify these interneurons as a future analgesic target. The study confirms that CR neurons form an excitatory dorsal horn network that contributes to spinal pain signaling and can amplify pain signals in the absence of peripheral input. The strong interconnectivity of this neurochemically-defined subpopulation in the dorsal horn means they are ideally positioned to alter incoming sensory information prior to its relay to higher brain centers. The results are compatible with current models of dorsal horn microcircuitry, which place CR neurons in a polysynaptic circuit that signals through SOM neurons to activate lamina I projection neurons and initiate pain signaling. The study extends the current view of excitatory interneurons in spinal sensory processing, which ascribes a relative limited role of linking low threshold modality ( innocuous ) tactile input to excite more dorsal nociceptive circuitry.
The study investigated the role of Ras-associated binding protein 23 (RAB23) in the regulation of early osteogenesis. RAB23 is a gene that, when mutated, is associated with Carpenter Syndrome, a condition characterized by multiple developmental abnormalities, including polysyndactyly and defects in skull morphogenesis. To understand the function of RAB23 in skull development, researchers generated mice deficient in RAB23 (Rab23-/-) that survived to an age where skeletal development could be studied. These mice exhibited premature fusion of multiple sutures, resulting from aberrant osteoprogenitor proliferation and elevated osteogenesis in the suture.

The study found that FGF10-driven FGFR1 signaling was elevated in Rab23-/- sutures, leading to an imbalance in MAPK, Hedgehog signaling, and RUNX2 expression. Inhibition of elevated pERK1/2 signaling normalized osteoprogenitor proliferation, reduced osteogenic gene expression, and prevented craniosynostosis. The results suggest that RAB23 acts as an upstream negative regulator of both FGFR and canonical Hh-GLI1 signaling, as well as a non-canonical regulator of GLI1 through pERK1/2.

Further investigation revealed that disruption of RAB23 leads to upregulation of Pitx2, Fgf10, and Fgfr1b expression, decreased p38, and enhanced pERK1/2-RUNX2 signaling, along with elevated osteoprogenitor proliferation. RAB23 was found to regulate the sutural stem cell niche marker GLI1, repress Hh, and inhibit osteoprogenitor expansion. Additionally, RAB23 represses FGF10-driven pERK1/2 signaling, which directly regulates osteoprogenitor development, RUNX2-mediated differentiation, and represses GLI1.

The study concludes that RAB23 coordinates suture biogenesis by regulating FGF-ERK1/2-RUNX2 activity, as well as the activation of GLI1. RAB23 controls suture morphogenesis and maintains suture patency through three mechanisms: FGF10-pERK1/2 signaling, the repression of Hh targets through a canonical pathway, and the repression of GLI1 by pERK1/2. GLI1 contributes to the regulation of the mesenchymal stem cell niche, and FGF10-pERK1/2 and Hh-GLI1 signaling regulate osteoprogenitor cell proliferation. The study highlights the importance of RAB23 in the regulation of early osteogenesis and its potential role in the development of craniosynostosis.
The Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2) plays a crucial role in maintaining epigenetic repression of genes in animals and plants by modifying chromatin at its targets. However, it remains unclear whether this epigenetic memory is stored locally in the chromatin environment of individual genes (cis memory) or in the concentrations of diffusible regulatory factors (trans memory). To address this question, researchers investigated the inheritance of gene expression states at the Polycomb target gene FLOWERING LOCUS C (FLC) in Arabidopsis. FLC is a key regulator of flowering time, and its expression is repressed by PRC2 in response to prolonged cold exposure, a process known as vernalization.

The study used a novel assay to visualize the mitotic heritability of FLC expression states at the single-cell level. By crossing FLC-mCherry to FLC-Venus plants, the researchers generated F1 hybrids carrying a single copy of each transgene. The results showed that one copy of FLC can be repressed while the other is active, and this mixed expression state is inherited through many cell divisions as the plants develop. This demonstrates that epigenetic memory of FLC expression is stored locally in the chromatin environment of the gene, rather than in the concentrations of diffusible regulatory factors.

The study provides evidence that Polycomb-repressed chromatin at a single gene can instruct its own inheritance, constituting a cis memory system. This finding has implications for our understanding of how cells remember past events, such as environmental or developmental cues, and how this information is passed on to daughter cells. The results also suggest that local chromatin states are not dictated by the concentrations of diffusible factors, but rather are determined by the chromatin environment of individual genes. This has important implications for our understanding of gene regulation and epigenetic inheritance in plants and animals.
BiP, the endoplasmic reticulum ( ER ) Hsp70 chaperone, forms oligomers that are poorly understood in terms of their structure and functional significance. Using site-specific proteolysis and crosslinking, researchers have probed the architecture of BiP oligomers and found that they consist of adjacent protomers engaging the interdomain linker of one molecule in the substrate binding site of another. This interaction attenuates the chaperone function of oligomeric BiP. Native gel electrophoresis revealed a rapidly-modulated reciprocal relationship between the burden of unfolded proteins and BiP oligomers, as well as slower equilibration between oligomers and inactive, covalently-modified BiP. Lumenal ER calcium depletion caused rapid oligomerization of mammalian BiP and a coincidental diminution in substrate binding, indicating the relative inertness of the oligomers. This suggests that equilibration between inactive oligomers and active monomeric BiP is poised to buffer fluctuations in ER unfolded protein load on a rapid timescale. The findings are consistent with a model in which competition between oligomerization and substrate binding buffers the ER against rapid fluctuations in unfolded protein load in both directions. BiP oligomerization is rapidly tuned to the physiological state of the cell, and given that oligomerization inactivates participating BiP protomers, the oligomeric form is a relatively inactive storage form of the chaperone that is accessible on a short timescale. This scenario could account for BiP favoring itself as a client ( oligomerizing ) over other clients as ER calcium levels decline. The inverse correlation between unfolded protein burden and BiP oligomerization appears to break down in the context of ER calcium depletion, which is both a strong inducer of the unfolded protein response ( UPR ) and promotes rapid oligomerization of BiP. Enhanced production of unfolded proteins rapidly draws on the pool of inactive, oligomeric BiP to service its growing clientele, and reports of enhanced presence of oligomers in longer-term stressed cells may have reflected the partitioning of excess chaperone produced during the activated UPR but made redundant following resolution of the insult. Exploring the relationship between unfolded protein load and the oligomeric state of Hsp70 chaperones is confounded by powerful feedback that dominates the unfolded protein responses and couples chaperone production to the levels of unfolded protein stress.
The near-atomic cryoEM structure of Pepino mosaic virus (PepMV), a representative of the genus Potexvirus (family Alphaflexiviridae), has been determined at 3.9 Å resolution using electron cryomicroscopy. PepMV is a flexible filamentous virus that contains several hundred copies of a helically arrayed coat protein (CP) protecting a positive-sense single-stranded RNA (ssRNA). The structure of PepMV CP has been modeled, revealing its interactions with viral RNA. Notably, the overall fold of PepMV CP shows homology with nucleoproteins (NPs) from the genus Phlebovirus (family Bunyaviridae), a group of enveloped negative-sense ssRNA viruses. The main difference between potexvirus CP and phlebovirus NP lies in their C-terminal extensions, which determine the characteristics of their distinct multimeric assemblies: a flexuous, helical rod or a loose ribonucleoprotein.

The structural homology between PepMV CP and phlebovirus NP suggests gene transfer between eukaryotic positive-sense and negative-sense ssRNA viruses. Phleboviruses are enveloped viruses with a segmented negative-sense ssRNA genome, and their NPs are associated with the viral genome in loose ribonucleoproteins (RNPs) protected inside an envelope. In contrast, potexviruses are non-enveloped and contain several hundred copies of CP arranged in a helical fashion protecting a positive-sense ssRNA.

Flexible filamentous viruses, including PepMV, are economically important plant pathogens that have a significant impact on agriculture. The high-resolution structures of flexuous filamentous plant viruses have been determined for representatives of the genus Potexvirus, including PepMV, PapMV, and BaMV. However, it is unclear whether members of other families of flexuous filamentous viruses, such as Betaflexiviridae, Closteroviridae, and Potyviridae, have a similar fold in their CPs. The structural homology between PepMV CP and phlebovirus NP highlights the evolutionary relationships between eukaryotic positive-sense and negative-sense ssRNA viruses.
Title: Nanoscale Binding Site Localization by Molecular Distance Estimation on Native Cell Surfaces Using Topological Image Averaging

    A novel method, SiteLoc, has been developed for imaging-based molecular localization of binding sites on cellular surface proteins with nanometer-scale precision. This technique is essential for understanding the immunological outcome of host-target interactions, which is crucial for the development of therapeutic monoclonal antibodies and vaccines. Traditional methods for epitope identification are often costly, time-consuming, and unsuitable for high-throughput analysis.

    Current techniques for nanometer-scale localization on microscopy data, such as particle-averaging strategies, are not effective for binding sites on proteins with heterogeneous expression on the cellular surface. These methods are typically designed for identical or spherical particles, making them unsuitable for unaltered cells with varying surface morphology. Super-resolution techniques, such as iPALM, STORM, and STED, can achieve nanometer-scale resolution but are expensive and require complex sample preparation.

    SiteLoc overcomes these limitations by using topological image averaging to estimate the distance between bound ligands and a reference surface. This method achieves nanometer-scale precision by reducing the dimensionality of surface data within an image plane. The location of a binding site is determined by calculating the average distance between the ligand channel and a reference signal channel using the averaged fluorescent signal along a cell contour.

    The efficacy of SiteLoc was demonstrated through site localization measurements on antibody-coated bacteria, which agreed well with previously reported data. The method was also shown to accurately estimate molecular heights on both spherical and non-spherical cellular surfaces. SiteLoc has the potential to facilitate the development of therapeutic monoclonal antibodies and vaccines by providing fast and efficient screening of epitope locations on native cell surfaces.
Systemic vascular pressure in vertebrates is regulated by various factors, including peripheral resistance in tissue capillary beds. However, the relationship between central control of vascular flow and peripheral resistance remains unclear, particularly in the context of hypoxic response in individual tissues and its effect on systemic cardiovascular adaptation to oxygen deprivation. This study investigates the role of peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in the skin, a large vascular bed, in influencing cardiovascular response to hypoxia in mice. The results demonstrate that the response of the skin to hypoxia feeds back on a wide range of cardiovascular parameters, including heart rate, arterial pressures, and body temperature. This study provides the first demonstration of a dynamic role for oxygen sensing in a peripheral tissue directly modifying cardiovascular response to the challenge of hypoxia.

The study focuses on the skin as a model for a peripheral tissue with a substantial vascular bed and analyzes the effect of loss of hypoxia-responsive genes on the cardiovascular hypoxic response. The results show that mice lacking HIF-1α, HIF-2α, NOS2, and Arg-1 expression in the epidermis exhibit significant changes in systemic blood pressures and cutaneous temperatures relative to littermate control animals. Furthermore, the study reveals that keratinocyte HIF-1α and HIF-2α expression affects the immediate, acute, and chronic phases of the cardiovascular response to hypoxia.

The study proposes a model for hypoxic responses involving HIF isoform activity and nitric oxide homeostasis in the skin. According to this model, hypoxia results in an initial HIF-1α/NOS2/NO-mediated increase in vascular diameter and resulting increases in perfusion, followed by a HIF-2α/Arginase-induced reduction of intracellular L-arginine. These findings suggest that peripheral tissues, such as the skin, play a fundamental role in cardiovascular adaptation to hypoxia and that oxygen sensing in these tissues can directly modify cardiovascular response to the challenge of hypoxia. The study highlights the importance of peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in the skin in regulating systemic vascular pressure and cardiovascular response to hypoxia.
Biomedical researchers have developed a refined optogenetic method for mapping neural circuits with single-neuron resolution. This approach combines two-photon excitation of channelrhodopsin (ChR2) with temporal focusing and restricted expression of ChR2 to the soma and proximal dendrites of neurons. The result is a reliable method for evoking action potentials in individual neurons, verifying spike generation with GCaMP6s, and determining the presence or absence of synaptic connections with patch-clamp electrophysiological recording. This method overcomes previous limitations of optogenetic mapping, which often left an indeterminate region of the circuit map coinciding with the dendritic arbors of the recorded neuron, and could be compromised by unintended activation of fibers of passage or local axonal boutons. By restricting ChR2 expression to the soma and proximal dendrites, researchers can selectively target single neurons and avoid confounding sources of light-induced electrical responses. This refined method enables the evaluation of synaptic connectivity with single-neuron resolution, which is critical for understanding the precise patterns of connectivity among distinct types of neurons that comprise neural circuits. The synaptic organization of individual neurons into circuits is the physiological basis for the interpretation of sensory input and production of behavioral responses. The ability to map functional connectivity with single-cell resolution is essential for elucidating circuit function, and this refined optogenetic method provides a powerful tool for achieving this goal. The method has the potential to significantly advance our understanding of neural circuits and their role in various physiological and pathological processes.
The interaction mode of transcription factors (TFs) on eukaryotic genomes has been a subject of ongoing debate. Recent single-molecule data on the TFs Sox2 and Oct4 were previously interpreted as evidence of ordered assembly on DNA. However, a rigorous biophysical analysis reveals that the calculated quantity does not determine binding order, but rather energy expenditure away from thermodynamic equilibrium. Instead, the data suggest that Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit negative reciprocity, where the expression of Sox2 increases Oct4’s genomic binding, while the expression of Oct4 decreases Sox2’s binding. This phenomenon can arise from either energy expenditure or a mixture of positive and negative cooperativity at distinct genomic loci. The genomic-diversity model at thermodynamic equilibrium can account for the observed data, indicating that Sox2 and Oct4 sometimes help and sometimes hinder each other across the genome. This finding is unexpected and highlights the complexity of TF interactions on DNA. The single-molecule methods used in the study provide novel detection capabilities for unraveling such mechanisms. The implications of this finding will need to be addressed in future studies. The analysis also suggests the possibility of energy expenditure in the action of Sox2 and Oct4 on DNA, which has not been previously found in living cells. The study demonstrates that single-molecule data, when coupled with the experimental strategy, can provide more information than expected. The variation in sign of cooperativity across the genome has significant ramifications, indicating that Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit both positive and negative cooperativity across genomic loci in vivo. This can be explained by either energy expenditure maintaining loci at which Sox2 and Oct4 bind away from thermodynamic equilibrium or mixed cooperativity across the genome, where Sox2 and Oct4 help each other through positive cooperativity at some loci and hinder each other through negative cooperativity at other loci.
Drosophila melanogaster display a strong aversive response to bacterial lipopolysaccharides (LPS), which are important immune system infection cues. This response is mediated by gustatory neurons expressing Gr66a bitter receptors, and the expression of the chemosensory cation channel dTRPA1 in these cells is necessary and sufficient for LPS avoidance. LPS stimulates Drosophila neurons in a TRPA1-dependent manner and activates exogenous dTRPA1 channels in human cells. These findings demonstrate that flies detect bacterial endotoxins via a gustatory pathway through TRPA1 activation as a conserved molecular mechanism. The avoidance of LPS-contaminated food during feeding and oviposition may serve to prevent Gram-negative bacterial infections, potentially compensating for the lack of adaptive immunity in these animals. The sensory mechanism exploits dTRPA1, suggesting a broadly conserved principle whereby these channels play a crucial role in LPS detection by sensory neurons in flies and mammals, regardless of the particular sensory modality involved. In support of this, blocking neurotransmission in gustatory neurons by expressing the light chain of tetanus toxin abolished avoidance of LPS, indicating that flies can detect LPS through a gustatory mechanism. Furthermore, dTrpA1-deficient mutants preferred laying eggs on control food rather than on food contaminated with E. coli, highlighting the importance of TRPA1 in LPS avoidance. These data indicate that a TRPA1-dependent mechanism of avoidance of LPS may serve flies to prevent infection with Gram-negative bacteria.
Zfp335 is a zinc finger protein that plays a crucial role in the formation of naïve T cells, which are essential for immune function. A mouse mutant, bloto, harbors a hypomorphic mutation in Zfp335 and exhibits a deficiency in naïve T cells due to an intrinsic developmental defect that begins in the thymus and continues in the periphery. This defect affects T cells that have recently undergone thymic egress and is not attributed to altered thymic selection, proliferation, or Bcl2-dependent survival. The effects of Zfp335bloto are multigenic and cannot be attributed to a single gene. Zfp335 binds to promoter regions via a consensus motif and its target genes are enriched in categories related to protein metabolism, mitochondrial function, and transcriptional regulation. Restoring the expression of one target gene, Ankle2, partially rescues T cell maturation. These findings identify Zfp335 as a transcription factor and essential regulator of late-stage intrathymic and post-thymic T cell maturation. The study reveals that Zfp335 regulates transcription by binding to promoters of target genes and identifies a set of Zfp335 target genes in thymocytes. Decreased Zfp335 occupancy at a subset of these targets alters gene expression in mutant thymocytes, providing evidence that Zfp335 functions as a transcription factor and key regulator of a transcriptional program required for T cell maturation. Ankle2 is identified as a functional target gene of Zfp335 and its dysregulation by Zfp335 contributes to the maturation defect in blt/blt T cells. The study demonstrates that the maturation defect in blt/blt mice is independent of thymic selection or effects on proliferation, but is associated with reduced viability. The findings provide new insights into the mechanisms governing T cell maturation and identify Zfp335 as a critical regulator of this process.
The relationship between messenger RNA (mRNA) abundance and protein expression levels in bacteria and archaea has been a long-standing assumption in gene expression analysis. However, recent studies have shown that this correlation is often imperfect due to various factors, including codon usage and mRNA secondary structure. A new global factor, called mRNA:ncRNA avoidance, has been identified as a significant contributor to the discrepancy between mRNA and protein abundance. This factor refers to the tendency of mRNAs to avoid interactions with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs), which can affect translational efficiency.

    The study found that for 97% of species, there is a reduced capacity for interaction between native RNAs relative to controls, indicating a strong selection for the avoidance of stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions across prokaryotes. By generating synonymously variant green fluorescent protein (GFP) mRNAs with different potential for mRNA:ncRNA interactions, the researchers demonstrated that GFP levels correlate well with interaction avoidance. This suggests that taking stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions into account enables precise modulation of protein abundance.

    A meta-analysis of proteomics and transcriptomics data revealed that mRNAs with low avoidance values produce fewer proteins for each mRNA transcript than those with high avoidance. The study also found that stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions are significantly underrepresented in most (73%) of the prokaryotic phyla, indicating a signature of selection that acts to minimize these interactions. However, the complete avoidance of stochastic interactions is combinatorially unlikely due to the large number of potential interacting RNA species in prokaryotic systems.

    The results indicate that crosstalk between mRNAs and ncRNAs can impact protein expression levels, and that taking crosstalk into account will enable the design of constructs where protein expression levels can be precisely controlled. The study suggests that mRNA:ncRNA avoidance is at least as good an explanation of variation in protein abundance as either codon usage and internal mRNA secondary structure. Overall, the findings provide new insights into the regulation of protein expression in bacteria and archaea and highlight the importance of considering stochastic mRNA:ncRNA interactions in gene expression analysis.
Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a fatal neurodegenerative disease characterized by the loss of upper and lower motor neurons (MN). A key pathological event in ALS is the denervation of neuromuscular junctions (NMJs), which occurs prior to symptom onset and is observed in both human patients and animal models of the disease. However, the underlying mechanisms of NMJ denervation in ALS remain unclear. To investigate this, researchers used in vivo imaging to study the dynamics of NMJ denervation in a mouse model of ALS (SOD1G37R mice) over a three-month period.

The study revealed that single motor-units (MUs) are dismantled asynchronously in SOD1G37R mice, indicating that NMJ denervation is a complex and dynamic process. Weeks prior to complete axonal degeneration, the dismantling of axonal branches is accompanied by contemporaneous new axonal sprouting, resulting in synapse formation onto nearby NMJs. Denervation events tend to propagate from the first lost NMJ, consistent with a contribution of neuromuscular factors extrinsic to motor neurons, with distal branches being more susceptible. These findings suggest that NMJ denervation in ALS is not simply a manifestation of sudden global motor neuron degeneration, but rather a dynamic process of continuous denervation and new innervation.

The study highlights a long temporal window between early branch-specific dismantling and global motor axon degeneration, which could be therapeutically exploited in ALS. However, the rescue of neuronal loss in SOD1 mice does not necessarily improve NMJ denervation, suggesting that local branch-specific events may play a key role in NMJ denervation. Local alterations of synaptic function and molecular signaling occur early at the NMJ in ALS, and the study's findings support the "dying-back hypothesis," which proposes that local branch-specific degeneration contributes to NMJ denervation. The results of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of NMJ denervation in ALS and have implications for the development of therapeutic strategies to prevent or slow disease progression.
Title: Estrogen Receptor Alpha in the Brain Mediates Tamoxifen-Induced Changes in Physiology in Mice

    Adjuvant tamoxifen therapy is a common treatment for breast cancer patients, significantly improving survival rates. However, long-term tamoxifen treatment is associated with undesirable side effects, including hot flashes, changes in bone density, and fatigue. To better understand the mechanisms underlying these side effects, researchers investigated the role of estrogen receptor alpha (ERα) in the brain in mediating tamoxifen-induced changes in physiology in mice.

    Single-cell RNA sequencing revealed that tamoxifen treatment induces widespread gene expression changes in the hypothalamus and preoptic area (hypothalamus-POA) of mice. These expression changes are dependent on ERα, as conditional knockout of ERα in the hypothalamus-POA ablates or reverses tamoxifen-induced gene expression. Furthermore, ERα-deficient mice do not exhibit tamoxifen-induced changes in temperature, bone, or movement. These findings suggest that ERα mediates several physiological effects of tamoxifen treatment in mice.

    The study demonstrated that ERα expression in the hypothalamus-POA is necessary for the tamoxifen-induced changes in gene expression in the hypothalamus-POA and the effects on thermoregulation, bone density, and movement. The results indicate that tamoxifen treatment modulates ERα signaling in the central nervous system to alter fundamental aspects of physiology and health. Given the known roles of ERα in mediating many aspects of physiology, the study suggests that loss of ERα would also affect physiological responses to tamoxifen in mice.

    The researchers measured body temperature, movement, and bone density in ERα conditional knockout (Esr1 cKO) mice treated equivalently and concurrently with wild-type animals. The results showed that ERα-deficient mice do not exhibit tamoxifen-induced changes in these physiological parameters. These findings provide mechanistic insight into the effects of tamoxifen on the hypothalamus-POA and indicate that ERα mediates several physiological effects of tamoxifen treatment in mice. The study highlights the utility of mice as a good model to study many of tamoxifen's physiological effects and provides a framework for understanding the undesirable side effects of tamoxifen therapy in humans.
ISG15, a linear di-ubiquitin-like protein with anti-viral activity, is induced in nonphagocytic cells in response to Listeria monocytogenes infection. This induction occurs independently of type I interferon and is mediated by the cytosolic surveillance pathway, which senses bacterial DNA and signals through STING, TBK1, IRF3, and IRF7. The study reveals that ISG15 expression restricts Listeria infection in vitro and in vivo. Using stable isotope labeling in tissue culture (SILAC), the researchers identified ISGylated proteins responsible for the protective effect of ISG15. The results show that ISG15 modification of ER and Golgi proteins increases the secretion of cytokines that counteract infection in vivo. The study demonstrates that ISG15 can be induced by Listeria in an interferon-independent manner in human nonphagocytic cells. The ISG15-dependent restriction of Listeria infection reinforces the view that ISG15 is a key component of the innate immune response. ISG15's role in host defense against Listeria in vitro requires ISGylation, a process in which ISG15 is covalently linked to hundreds of cellular and viral substrates. The study provides new insights into the mechanisms by which ISG15 counteracts Listeria infection and highlights the importance of ISG15 in the innate immune response. The findings suggest that ISG15 could be a potential target for the development of new therapeutic strategies against Listeria infections.
Title: DeepFRET, a software for rapid and automated single-molecule FRET data classification using deep learning

Single-molecule Förster Resonance energy transfer (smFRET) is a versatile method for studying the structure and dynamics of biomolecules. However, the growth of high-throughput methodologies and commercial instrumentation has outpaced the development of rapid, standardized, and automated analysis methods for smFRET data. To address this gap, researchers have developed DeepFRET, an automated, open-source software based on deep learning. DeepFRET integrates standard features of smFRET analysis and outputs common kinetic information metrics.

The software operates with minimal human intervention, requiring only a user-adjustable quality threshold to transition from raw microscope images to histograms of biomolecule behavior. DeepFRET's classification accuracy on ground truth data exceeded 95%, outperforming human operators and commonly used thresholds, while requiring approximately 1% of the time. The software's precise and rapid operation on real data demonstrates its capacity to objectively quantify biomolecular dynamics.

DeepFRET's neural network was trained using either simulated or high-quality annotated experimental data, enabling it to recognize abstract patterns and learn useful features directly from raw input data. This approach offers several advantages over other methods, including reproducible and less opinionated evaluation of single-molecule data, faster processing times, and the ability to operate without extensive data preprocessing or empirically defined rules.

The software's performance was evaluated using a confusion matrix, which quantified the discordance between ground truth data and data selected and classified by DeepFRET. The results illustrate the power of DeepFRET to operate without human interventions and its potential to benchmark the reproducibility of smFRET data acquisition methods for multiple biomolecular systems across laboratories.

DeepFRET is an all-inclusive analysis software with a pre-trained DNN at its core, providing a rapid, objective, and accurate assessment of smFRET data for quantifying biomolecular dynamics. The software comes as a free-to-use standalone executable for both Windows and Mac users, allowing end-users with limited programming skills to easily operate it. DeepFRET has the potential to contribute to benchmarking smFRET for dynamic structural biology and enable new fully automated analysis methodologies related to biomolecular recognition, protein folding, and dynamics.
The Polycomb repressive complex-1 (PRC1) plays a crucial role in epigenetic regulation of gene expression. SCML2, a mammalian homolog of Drosophila SCM, is a Polycomb-group protein that associates with PRC1. This study reveals that SCML2A, an isoform of SCML2 tightly associated with chromatin, contributes to PRC1 localization and directly enforces repression of certain Polycomb target genes. SCML2A binds to PRC1 via its SPM domain and interacts with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) through a novel RNA-binding region (RBR). The targeting of SCML2A to chromatin involves the coordinated action of the MBT domains, RNA binding, and interaction with PRC1 through the SPM domain. Deletion of the RBR reduces the occupancy of SCML2A at target genes, and overexpression of a mutant SCML2A lacking the RBR causes defects in PRC1 recruitment. These observations suggest a role for ncRNAs in regulating SCML2 function and indicate that SCML2 participates in the epigenetic control of transcription directly and in cooperation with PRC1. Furthermore, SCML2 appears to enforce repression directly at a subset of genes, independent of PRC1 recruitment, via mechanisms that remain to be elucidated. The identification of SCML2 as a PcG protein that binds RNA and contributes to repression of Polycomb targets sheds new light on the interplay between ncRNAs and epigenetic regulation. The study also reveals that RNA binding plays a role in SCML2 function, as it is required for its localization to chromatin. The results support a model in which the RBR contributes to the localization of SCML2A to chromatin, where it interacts with PRC1 and creates a chromatin environment that is refractory to transcription. The study proposes that SCML2A is retained at target genes through the interaction with PRC1 and contributes to stabilizing PRC1. The findings suggest that SCML2 is recruited to a subset of target genes through a process that requires its RNA-binding activity and that the presence of mutant SCML2A lacking the RBR causes a partial release of BMI1 and PRC1 from chromatin.
The regulation of translating ribosomes is a crucial component of the gene expression control network in Escherichia coli. The C-terminal arrest sequence of SecM regulates the SecA-dependent secretion pathway by inducing ribosome stalling. Previous studies have identified critical residues of SecM peptide and the ribosome exit tunnel involved in stalling, but the underlying molecular mechanism remains unclear at the atomic level. This study presents two cryo-EM structures of SecM-stalled ribosomes at 3. 3–3. 7 Å resolution, revealing two distinct stalling mechanisms at different elongation steps of the translation cycle. The first mechanism involves the inactivation of the ribosomal peptidyl-transferase center ( PTC ), which inhibits peptide bond formation with the incoming prolyl-tRNA. The second mechanism involves the prolonged residence of the peptidyl-RNA at the hybrid A/P site, which prevents full-scale tRNA translocation. These findings demonstrate an elegant control of the translation cycle by regulatory peptides through a continuous, dynamic reshaping of the functional center of the ribosome. The study used an in vitro translation system from E. coli to purify SecM-stalled ribosome nascent chain complexes ( RNCs ) and analyzed their structures using single-particle cryo-EM. The analysis of ribosome–SecM interactions at near-atomic level reveals two different mechanisms of stalling and provides detailed insights into how SecM nascent peptide modulates translation in distinct steps. The study provides a comprehensive understanding of the molecular mechanism of SecM-dependent translational stalling and sheds light on the complex interactions between SecM and the ribosome.
Title: Human foveal cone photoreceptor topography and its dependence on eye length

    A recent study used an Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope to investigate the relationship between foveal cone density and eye length in 28 eyes of 16 subjects. Cone density and other metrics were computed in both visual angle and linear retinal units. The results show that the foveal cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fovea, but not in proportion to eye length. Despite retinal stretching, myopes generally have a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea compared to emmetropes. This suggests that myopes may have better visual acuity due to a higher photoreceptor sampling resolution.

    Previous studies have implicated retinal stretching as a possible cause of myopic visual deficits, but the structural changes at the foveal center during myopic progression were not well understood. The current study aimed to investigate how eye length affects cone density at and near the foveal center. Two types of cone densities were discussed in the study: cone density per unit area and cone density per unit visual angle. The results show that myopes have a higher cone density per unit visual angle compared to emmetropes, indicating a higher photoreceptor sampling resolution.

    The study used a new generation of Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope to image cones at and near the fovea.
