This technology allows for high-resolution imaging of the smallest cones at the foveal center, enabling a definitive analysis of cone density as a function of eye length. The study found that the foveal cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fovea, but not in proportion to eye length. This means that myopes have a higher angular sampling density in and around the fovea compared to emmetropes, despite retinal stretching.

    The study's findings have implications for our understanding of myopic visual deficits. Reports of deficits in best-corrected foveal vision in myopes compared to emmetropes cannot be explained by increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching during myopic progression. Instead, the study suggests that myopes may have better visual acuity due to a higher photoreceptor sampling resolution. The results of this study provide new insights into the relationship between foveal cone density and eye length, and highlight the importance of considering the structural changes at the foveal center during myopic progression.
Title: Genome-wide Identification of Lineage and Locus Specific Variation Associated with Pneumococcal Carriage Duration

    A recent study investigated the genetic factors contributing to the duration of Streptococcus pneumoniae carriage in the nasopharynx, a prerequisite for invasive disease, particularly in low-income settings. The researchers developed a model to calculate the duration of carriage episodes from longitudinal swab data and combined these results with whole genome sequence data. The analysis revealed that pneumococcal genomic variation accounted for 63% of the phenotype variation in carriage duration, while host traits such as age and previous carriage accounted for less than 5%. The study further partitioned this heritability into lineage and locus effects, identifying serotype (17%), drug-resistance (9%), and other significant locus effects (7%) as the largest sources of variation in carriage duration.

    A pan-genome-wide association study identified prophage sequences as being associated with decreased carriage duration, independent of serotype, potentially by disrupting the competence mechanism. This finding supports theoretical models of pneumococcal competition and antibiotic resistance. The study's analysis of heritability showed that variants other than serotype affect carriage duration, consistent with recent theoretical work. The researchers also identified candidates for the loci that fulfill this role, suggesting that variants exist in the pneumococcal genome that alter carriage duration, which is linked to antibiotic resistance.

    The study found that despite other factors such as host age, sequence variation of the pneumococcal genome explains most of the variability in carriage duration. Common serotypes and resistance to erythromycin cause some of this effect, as does the presence or absence of particular prophage sequences in the genome. The researchers provide a quantitative estimate of how closely transmission pairs share their carriage duration and show evidence for differences both between and within serotypes. The implication of phage as having a significant effect on carriage duration has interesting corollaries on pneumococcal genome diversification through frequent infection and loss of prophage, even during carriage episodes in this dataset.

    The study's findings suggest that the genetics of S. pneumoniae is an important factor in determining carriage duration in this population. The heritability estimate may differ if environmental conditions are associated with streptococcal genotype between populations, such as host vaccination status. A lower bound on h2 can be calculated by fitting a linear mixed model through maximum likelihood to common SNPs.
The stoichiometry between the α-subunit KCNQ1 and the β-subunit KCNE1 of the slow delayed rectifier current (IKs) channel complex has been a topic of controversy in the scientific community. The IKs channel complex plays a crucial role in stabilizing repolarization and shortening action potential duration at high heart rates in the myocardium. To clarify the stoichiometry, researchers created fusion channels where KCNE1 was linked to one, two, or four KCNQ1 subunits, resulting in 4:4, 2:4, or 1:4 stoichiometries, respectively. Whole cell and single-channel recordings showed that the 2:4 and 1:4 stoichiometries exhibited increasingly hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter first latency of opening compared to the 4:4 stoichiometry. However, the addition of KCNE1 abolished these differences, indicating that the observed effects were due to the stoichiometry of the fusion channels rather than an intrinsic property of KCNE1.

    To further investigate the stoichiometry, the researchers used a UV-crosslinking unnatural amino acid approach to study the association between KCNE1 and KCNQ1 subunits. The results showed that the crosslinking rates of the 2:4 and 1:4 stoichiometries were progressively slowed compared to the 4:4 stoichiometry, suggesting that there is no intrinsic mechanism limiting the association of up to four β-subunits within the IKs complex. This finding supports the idea that the stoichiometry between KCNQ1 and KCNE1 can be regulated based on availability, allowing for a powerful mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart through changes in expression.

    The IKs channel complex is composed of KCNQ1 and KCNE1 subunits, which are expressed in various tissues, including the myocardium and inner ear. In the heart, KCNQ1 and KCNE1 together conduct the slow delayed rectifier current (IKs), which is primarily responsible for stabilizing repolarization and shortening action potential duration at high heart rates. However, the stoichiometry between KCNQ1 and KCNE1 has been a subject of debate, with some studies reporting a strict 2:4 ratio and others suggesting a variable ratio up to 4:4. The results of this study provide new insights into the stoichiometry of the IKs channel complex and highlight the importance of regulating the expression of KCNQ1 and KCNE1 subunits to modulate repolarization in the heart.
In a study on zebrafish fin fold regeneration, researchers have elucidated the role of interleukin-1 beta (Il1b) and the inflammatory response in tissue regeneration. The study found that Il1b, a pro-inflammatory cytokine, is expressed by epithelial cells in response to tissue injury and plays a crucial role in regulating the expression of regeneration-induced genes. However, prolonged expression of Il1b leads to apoptosis of regenerative cells, highlighting the double-edged sword nature of Il1b signaling in tissue regeneration.

The study revealed that myeloid cells, such as neutrophils and macrophages, are not the primary source of Il1b, but rather epithelial cells initiate the inflammatory response by expressing Il1b in response to tissue injury. Macrophages then play a critical role in attenuating Il1b expression and resolving inflammation, which is necessary for the survival of regenerative cells. The study also showed that genetic ablation of macrophages in adult zebrafish affects fin ray patterning and fin growth, further emphasizing the importance of macrophages in tissue regeneration.

The researchers demonstrated that Il1b signaling is essential for normal fin fold regeneration by activating the expression of regeneration-induced genes. However, excessive Il1b signaling and inflammation can impair tissue regeneration, leading to apoptosis of regenerative cells. The study suggests that a proper level of Il1b expression and the inflammatory response, tuned by macrophages, is necessary for the progression of tissue regeneration.

The findings of this study provide new insights into the complex interplay between Il1b signaling, inflammation, and tissue regeneration. The study highlights the critical role of macrophages in attenuating Il1b expression and resolving inflammation, which is necessary for the survival of regenerative cells. The results also emphasize the importance of a balanced inflammatory response in tissue regeneration, as excessive inflammation can lead to apoptosis of regenerative cells. Overall, the study contributes to our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying tissue regeneration and has implications for the development of regenerative therapies.
The study investigated the relationship between protein surface properties and translational diffusion in the cytoplasm of Escherichia coli, Lactococcus lactis, and Haloferax volcanii. The researchers used surface-modified fluorescent proteins (FPs) to determine their translational diffusion coefficients (D) in the cytoplasm of these organisms. The results show that the diffusion coefficient of proteins in E. coli depends on their net charge and the distribution of charge over the protein surface, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative ones. This effect is weaker in L. lactis and Hfx. volcanii due to electrostatic screening. The decrease in mobility is primarily caused by the interaction of positive FPs with ribosomes, as demonstrated by in vivo diffusion measurements and confirmed in vitro with purified ribosomes. The study suggests that ribosome surface properties may limit the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome. The findings also indicate that positive proteins not only diffuse slowly but may also inhibit protein synthesis by affecting the assembly or activity of ribosomes. Furthermore, the study analyzed the proteomes of E. coli, L. lactis, Hfx. volcanii, and four endosymbiotic bacteria to determine how protein diffusion is affected by the composition of the proteome and how ribosomes influence the diffusion of proteome constituents. The results have implications for understanding the functioning of prokaryotic endosymbionts and the importance of translational diffusion in biological cells.
Biologists have long sought to understand the mechanisms that initiate regeneration in animals. Planarians, a type of flatworm, are capable of whole-body regeneration and exhibit distinct molecular responses to wounds that result in tissue absence versus those that do not. A key question has been how these distinct responses are activated. Researchers have identified a gene called Smed-follistatin, a homolog of the protein follistatin, which is required for planarian regeneration. Smed-follistatin inhibits the activity of two activin homologs, Smed-activin-1 and Smed-activin-2, which are necessary for the Smed-follistatin phenotype. The study found that Smed-follistatin is wound-induced and expressed at higher levels following injuries that cause tissue absence. This suggests that Smed-follistatin inhibits Smed-activin proteins to trigger regeneration specifically following injuries involving tissue absence. The data indicate that Smed-follistatin plays a critical role in initiating regeneration, a process important across the animal kingdom. The study also found that Smed-follistatin expression is controlled by the extent of tissue absence following injury, and that inhibition of Smed-follistatin blocks responses to tissue absence but does not prevent normal tissue turnover. Furthermore, the study suggests that wound-induced Smed-follistatin expression allows for regenerative responses to be initiated specifically as a consequence of tissue absence. This is the first time a gene has been identified as required for regeneration-specific responses in planarians. The findings of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of regeneration and have implications for understanding how animals distinguish between injuries involving varying amounts of tissue loss and regulate distinct wound response programs.
Mismatch repair (MMR)-deficient tumors exhibit a unique mutation signature characterized by a high frequency of indel mutations in mono- and di-nucleotide repeats, also known as microsatellite instability (MSI). This mutation signature is distinct from other tumor genomes but surprisingly similar to germ-line DNA, suggesting that a substantial fraction of human genetic variation arises from mutations that escape MMR. Whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing of MMR-deficient tumors revealed a 52-fold increase in somatic events compared to MMR-proficient tumors, with a validation rate of 87% for substitutions and 86% for indels.

The study identified a large set of recurrent indels that may serve as markers for MSI detection. Using endometrial tumors with immunohistochemically proven MMR deficiency, a novel marker set was optimized to detect MSI, which showed greater specificity and selectivity than standard MSI tests. The marker set was capable of detecting MSI in 75% of endometrial and colorectal cancer tumors.

Furthermore, the study found that recurrent indels are enriched for the "DNA double-strand break repair by homologous recombination" pathway. Consequently, DNA double-strand break (DSB) repair is reduced in MMR-deficient tumors, leading to a dose-dependent sensitivity of MMR-deficient tumor cultures to DSB inducers. This suggests that MMR-deficient tumors may be more susceptible to therapies that induce DNA double-strand breaks.

The results of this study have significant implications for the diagnosis and treatment of MMR-deficient tumors. The identification of a unique mutation signature and the development of a novel marker set for MSI detection may enable more accurate diagnosis and prognosis of MMR-deficient tumors. Additionally, the finding that MMR-deficient tumors are sensitive to DSB inducers may lead to the development of new therapeutic strategies for these tumors.
The article describes the development of an optimized version of the Magnets photodimerizer system for subcellular optogenetics in mammalian cells. The Magnets system is a light-inducible dimerization module engineered from the Neurospora crassa photoreceptor Vivid, which enables precise temporal and spatial control of biological processes in a non-invasive manner. The original Magnets system consists of two components, nMag and pMag, which are well-tolerated as protein fusion partners and require simultaneous photoactivation to interact, allowing for high spatiotemporal confinement of dimerization with a single excitation wavelength. However, the original Magnets system requires concatemerization for efficient responses and cell preincubation at 28°C to be functional.

    To overcome these limitations, the authors engineered an optimized Magnets pair, referred to as ‘enhanced’ Magnets (eMags), which do not require concatemerization or low-temperature preincubation. The eMags were designed by introducing complementary charges into the Vivid homodimer interface, resulting in a pair with greater thermal stability and dimerization efficiency, as well as faster association and dissociation kinetics. The eMags were validated by using them to rapidly and reversibly recruit proteins to subcellular organelles, induce organelle contacts, and reconstitute OSBP-VAP ER-Golgi tethering, which is implicated in phosphatidylinositol-4-phosphate transport and metabolism.

    The eMags were shown to be effective in a variety of applications, including protein recruitment to different organelles, the generation/expansion of organelle contact sites, and the rapid and reversible reconstitution of VAP-dependent inter-organelle tethers. The eMags offer major advantages over other optogenetic systems, including rapid association and dissociation kinetics, low background activation, and the ability to optogenetically manipulate physiological processes over whole cells or in small subcellular volumes. Overall, the eMags represent a highly effective tool for optogenetically manipulating physiological processes in mammalian cells.
Activation of GCN2 Kinase by Ribosome Stalling Links Translation Elongation with Translation Initiation

    Recent studies have shown that ribosome stalling during translation can lead to neurodegeneration, but the signaling pathways triggered by stalled elongation complexes were unknown. To investigate these pathways, researchers analyzed the brains of C57BL/6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice, in which neuronal elongation complexes are stalled at AGA codons due to deficiencies in a tRNAArgUCU tRNA and GTPBP2, a mammalian ribosome rescue factor.

    The results showed that increased levels of phosphorylation of eIF2α (Ser51) were detected prior to neurodegeneration in these mice, and transcriptome analysis demonstrated activation of ATF4, a key transcription factor in the integrated stress response (ISR) pathway. Genetic experiments revealed that this pathway was activated by the eIF2α kinase, GCN2, in an apparent deacylated tRNA-independent fashion. Furthermore, the ISR was found to attenuate neurodegeneration in C57BL/6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice, underscoring the importance of cellular and stress context on the outcome of activation of this pathway.

    The study demonstrated that GCN2 is activated in the B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mutant brain in a tRNA-deacyl-independent manner prior to the onset of neurodegeneration. Activation of this kinase results in eIF2α phosphorylation and induction of ATF4-target genes in the cerebellum and hippocampus of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice. Genetic studies showed that GCN2 activation functions to attenuate neurodegeneration in the B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- brain, suggesting that ribosome stalling activates GCN2 as a previously unknown regulatory mechanism that links the elongation and initiation steps of translation.

    Failure to resolve stalled elongation complexes in mammalian neurons results in degeneration of these cells, but the signaling pathways evoked by these complexes were unknown. The study shows that GCN2 activates the integrated stress response in the brain of mice with stalled ribosomes, demonstrating an important feedback loop between elongation defects, in particular ribosome stalling, and translation initiation. Furthermore, in the hippocampus of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/-; Gcn2-/- mice, degeneration of CA1 pyramidal cells was observed, suggesting that the eIF2α/ATF4 pathway is vital for survival of these neurons during ribosome stalling. Finally, ATF4 activation also occurs in the brains of B6J-Gtpbp2nmf205-/- mice in which the n-Tr20 is entirely deleted and thus no unprocessed tRNA is produced, raising the possibility that ribosome stalling may also contribute to GCN2 activation in other conditions, such as amino acid deprivation. The exact mechanism for GCN2 activation by ribosome stalling remains unclear.
Over the past century, adult human height has undergone significant and uneven changes across the world’s countries. A comprehensive analysis of 1472 population-based studies involving over 18.6 million participants revealed that the largest gains in adult height have occurred in South Korean women and Iranian men, who have become 20.2 cm and 16.5 cm taller, respectively, since the 1896 birth cohort. In contrast, there has been little change in adult height in some sub-Saharan African countries and South Asia over the past century.

    The tallest populations a century ago were found in Central and Northern Europe, North America, and some Pacific islands, with men born in Sweden, Norway, and the USA surpassing 171 cm, approximately 18-19 cm taller than men in Laos. Swedish women, with an average adult height of 160.3 cm, were also among the tallest. Conversely, women born in Guatemala in 1896 were the shortest, with an average height of 140.3 cm.

    The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations was approximately 19 cm for men and 20 cm for women a century ago, and has remained relatively stable for women but increased for men over the past century, despite changes in the ranking of countries by adult height. Men born in the Netherlands in the last quarter of the 20th century were found to be the tallest, with an average height of 182.5 cm. The gap between the tallest and shortest countries has also increased, with Dutch men being 22-23 cm taller than men in Timor-Leste, Yemen, and Laos, which are among the shortest countries.

    Notably, the largest gains in adult height since the 1896 birth cohort were observed in South Korean women and Iranian men, who experienced increases of 20.2 cm and 16.5 cm, respectively. In contrast, some sub-Saharan African countries and South Asia have seen little change in adult height over the past century. The tallest populations a century ago were found in Central and Northern Europe, North America, and some Pacific islands, while the shortest populations were found in Guatemala, El Salvador, Peru, Bangladesh, South Korea, and Japan. The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations has remained relatively stable for women but increased for men over the past century.
The opioid epidemic in the United States has resulted in a staggering 130 deaths per day due to opioid-induced respiratory depression (OIRD). To combat this crisis, researchers have been working to identify the key brain areas and cell types responsible for OIRD. A recent study has made significant progress in this area by uncovering two primary sites in the brainstem that mediate the effects of opioids on breathing. The study found that the preBötzinger Complex (preBötC), a region responsible for generating the breathing rhythm, is a critical site for opioid-induced respiratory depression. Specifically, the researchers discovered that approximately 140 Oprm1 glutamatergic neurons in the preBötC are responsible for this effect. Furthermore, they found that just 70-140 of these neurons are sufficient to rescue opioid depression of the preBötC rhythm.

    The study used genetic tools to selectively eliminate the µ-Opioid receptor in the preBötC and found that this significantly reduced the depressive effects of opioids on breathing. The researchers also identified a second site in the brainstem that contributes to OIRD, although this site is less dominant than the preBötC. The combined deletion of the µ-Opioid receptor from both sites essentially eliminated OIRD, even at high doses of the potent opioid fentanyl. This suggests that targeting just these two sites is sufficient to rescue opioid respiratory depression.

    The study's findings have significant implications for the development of novel therapies that can prevent respiratory depression while sparing analgesia. The researchers propose that targeting the Oprm1 neurons in the preBötC could lead to the development of safer opioids or rescue strategies for opioid overdose. The study's results also highlight the importance of understanding the neural mechanisms underlying OIRD in order to develop effective treatments for this devastating public health crisis.
Human hepatitis B virus (HBV) and its satellite virus, hepatitis D virus (HDV), are significant public health concerns due to their severe disease outcomes in coinfected individuals. The entry of both viruses into hepatocytes is mediated by HBV envelope proteins, specifically the pre-S1 domain of the large envelope protein. However, the identity of the receptor(s) responsible for binding to this domain has remained unknown. To address this knowledge gap, researchers employed near-zero distance photo-cross-linking and tandem affinity purification to investigate the interaction between the pre-S1 domain and potential receptors. The results revealed that sodium taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide (NTCP), a multiple transmembrane transporter predominantly expressed in the liver, specifically interacts with the pre-S1 domain of the HBV and HDV large envelope proteins. This interaction is crucial for the entry of both viruses into hepatocytes. Silencing NTCP inhibited HBV and HDV infection, while exogenous NTCP expression rendered nonsusceptible hepatocarcinoma cells susceptible to these viral infections. Furthermore, replacing amino acids 157-165 of nonfunctional monkey NTCP with the human counterpart restored the ability of NTCP to support HBV and HDV infections. These findings demonstrate that NTCP is a functional receptor for both HBV and HDV. The pre-S1 domain of the large envelope protein is a key determinant for receptor binding, and the region between amino acids 157 and 165 of NTCP is crucial for binding to the pre-S1 domain of the HBV and HDV large envelope proteins. The identification of NTCP as a receptor for HBV and HDV provides new insights into the mechanisms of viral entry and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies for the treatment of HBV and HDV infections.
Sepsis leads to lasting changes in the phenotype and function of memory CD8 T cells, a type of immune cell that plays a crucial role in long-term immunity. The study found that memory CD8 T cells from septic patients and mice proliferate more than those from healthy individuals, leading to changes in the composition of the memory CD8 T cell compartment and altered tissue localization. This increased proliferation is associated with a numerical recovery of pathogen-specific memory CD8 T cells following sepsis-induced lymphopenia, a condition characterized by a decrease in the number of immune cells in the body.

    The study used a mouse model of sepsis, induced by cecal ligation and puncture ( CLP ), to investigate the effects of sepsis on memory CD8 T cells. The results showed that central memory CD8 T cells ( TCM ) from septic mice proliferate more than effector memory CD8 T cells ( TEM ), leading to an over-representation of TCM in the memory CD8 T cell pool after sepsis. This bias toward TCM alters the localization of memory CD8 T cells, potentially affecting their ability to respond to infections.

    The study also found that memory CD8 T cells from sepsis survivors have an altered transcriptional profile and chromatin accessibility, indicating long-lasting T cell intrinsic changes. These changes in the memory CD8 T cell pool and transcriptional landscape result in altered T cell function and reduced capacity to control Listeria monocytogenes infection. The study suggests that sepsis leads to long-term alterations in memory CD8 T cell phenotype, protective function, and localization, potentially changing the host's capacity to respond to re-infection.

    The findings of this study have important implications for our understanding of the long-term effects of sepsis on the immune system. Sepsis survivors may be more susceptible to re-infection due to the lasting changes in their memory CD8 T cells. The study highlights the need for further research into the mechanisms underlying these changes and the development of strategies to restore immune function in sepsis survivors.
APOBEC3A (A3A) is a potent inhibitor of Long INterspersed Element-1 (LINE-1 or L1) retrotransposition in human cells. L1 retrotransposition poses a mutagenic threat to the human genome, and A3A has been shown to inhibit this process in cultured cell assays. However, the molecular mechanism by which A3A exerts its inhibitory effect has remained unclear. A3A is a cytidine deaminase that catalyzes the deamination of cytidine nucleotides to uridine nucleotides (C-to-U) in single-strand DNA substrates.

    To elucidate the mechanism of A3A-mediated inhibition of L1 retrotransposition, researchers employed an in vitro assay (L1 Element Amplification Protocol or LEAP) that recapitulates the first strand cDNA synthesis step of the L1 integration process. This assay revealed that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. Furthermore, engineered L1 retrotransposition events derived from cultured human cells that co-express A3A and the uracil DNA glycosylase inhibitor protein (UGI) contain strand-specific, A3A-mediated C-to-U deamination events.

    Previous studies have shown that over-expression of wild-type (WT) APOBEC3A cytidine deaminase protein (A3A) can potently inhibit L1 retrotransposition in cultured human cells. However, deamination events were not detected in L1 retrotransposition events generated in the presence of WT A3A. The degradation of deaminated retrotransposition intermediates could reduce L1 retrotransposition levels in cultured cell assays and eliminate evidence of A3A-mediated deamination events in the resultant retrotransposed L1 sequences. This scenario could explain why previous studies failed to detect A3A-mediated deamination events in engineered L1 retrotransposition events.

    The results of this study demonstrate that A3A can inhibit L1 retrotransposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. This finding provides a mechanistic explanation for the inhibitory effect of A3A on L1 retrotransposition and highlights the importance of A3A in regulating L1 retrotransposition in human cells.
The study investigated the role of the activity-regulated, non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity. Rem2 is a member of the Rad/Rem/Rem2/Gem/Kir ( RGK ) protein family, whose expression and function are regulated by neuronal activity in response to calcium influx through voltage-gated calcium channels. The researchers found that Rem2-/- mice failed to exhibit normal ocular dominance plasticity during the critical period, indicating a role for Rem2 in experience-dependent plasticity. At the cellular level, the study established a cell-autonomous role for Rem2 in regulating intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons, prior to changes in synaptic function. In vitro and in vivo recordings revealed increased spontaneous firing rates in the absence of Rem2. These findings suggest that Rem2 is a key molecule that regulates neuronal excitability and circuit function in the context of changing sensory experience. Rem2 functions at the nexus of a signaling network that senses and responds to changes in neuronal activity to preserve proper circuit function in the face of changing sensory experience. The study also found that Rem2 expression is acutely regulated by visual experience and plays a role in activity-dependent processes in the visual cortex following sensory deprivation. The results raise the possibility that Rem2 may play an important role in regulating the absolute responsiveness of the cortex to visual stimulation, such as through regulation of neuronal excitability or synaptic scaling. The study provides an important step forward in connecting molecular regulators of neuronal morphology with broader circuit function and highlights the need to define Rem2-dependent signaling mechanisms to elucidate the molecular mechanisms that instruct activity-dependent modifications of neural circuitry.
The article presents an analysis of the physical limits to magnetogenetics, a field that aims to engineer cells to be responsive to magnetic fields for controlling their activity. Recent studies have reported promising results, including the identification of a protein complex that acts like a compass needle to guide magnetic orientation in animals and the magnetic control of membrane conductance by attaching ferritin to an ion channel protein. However, the article argues that these claims conflict with basic laws of physics, as the forces or torques produced by magnetic fields on biological molecules and cells are too small by many orders of magnitude to achieve the desired effects. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes, such as ferritin, is found to seriously limit their utility for engineering magnetically sensitive cells. The article suggests that if the reported phenomena do occur, they must rely on entirely different mechanisms. The proposed routes to magnetogenetics, based on either pulling or heating a ferritin/channel complex with magnetic fields, have no chance of success without dramatic new discoveries about the structure of biological matter. A recent study reported the design of a molecular system intended to couple magnetic fields to ionic current across the cell membrane by fusing a putative mechano-sensitive cation channel (TRPV4) to two subunits of ferritin. Experimental results suggested that neural activity can be modulated by static magnetic fields, but the evidence is limited and hard to interpret. The article concludes that the underlying biophysical mechanism of magnetogenetics remains unresolved and that further research is needed to understand how organisms sense the Earth's magnetic field for navigation and how to engineer cells to be responsive to magnetic fields.
In a study on Drosophila neural progenitors, also known as neuroblasts, researchers investigated the mechanisms underlying quiescence, a state of dormancy that stem cells can enter to conserve energy and resources. Quiescence is a crucial aspect of stem cell biology, particularly for stem cell-based therapeutics, but it remains poorly understood. The study found that the differentiation factor Prospero is transiently detected in the neuroblast nucleus just prior to entry into quiescence. This pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero precedes the establishment of a unique molecular profile lacking most progenitor and differentiation markers. The researchers observed that loss of Prospero prevents entry into quiescence, while a pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero can drive proliferating larval neuroblasts into quiescence. Furthermore, the study showed that nearly all neuroblasts that were quiescent at 12 hours after induction had reactivated proliferation and re-expressed progenitor markers 24 hours later. The researchers also found that precocious low-level nuclear Prospero can advance the timing of neuroblast quiescence. These findings suggest that Prospero levels distinguish three progenitor fates: absent for self-renewal, low for quiescence, and high for differentiation. The study proposes that a pulse of low-level nuclear Prospero marks neuroblast entry into quiescence, indicating a functional relationship between Prospero and quiescence. Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of quiescence in Drosophila neural progenitors and highlights the importance of Prospero in regulating stem cell fate decisions.
Scientists have developed a new method to study the process of HIV-1 capsid uncoating in real-time using single-particle fluorescence microscopy. This technique allows researchers to observe the disassembly of individual HIV capsids immediately after the viral membrane is permeabilized. The study reveals that the opening of the first defect in the capsid lattice is the rate-limiting step of uncoating, which is followed by a rapid and catastrophic collapse. The capsid-binding inhibitor PF74 accelerates the opening of the capsid but stabilizes the remaining lattice, while binding of a polyanion to a conserved arginine cluster in the lattice delays the initiation of uncoating but does not prevent subsequent lattice disassembly. These findings suggest that different stages of uncoating can be controlled independently, and the interplay between different capsid-binding regulators determines the overall uncoating kinetics.

    The researchers used an in vitro single-molecule fluorescence imaging assay to follow the uncoating kinetics of hundreds of individual HIV capsids in a single experiment. This approach allows for the resolution of intermediates in the disassembly pathway that are averaged out in traditional ensemble assays. The study found that the integrity and stability of capsids in a population are highly heterogeneous, with the majority containing defects and/or uncoating immediately after removal of the viral membrane. In the absence of capsid binders, uncoating is an all-or-none process that proceeds rapidly after the first defect is created. The half-life of the HIV capsid is sufficiently long to use PFO-permeabilized viral particles as a platform to quantify the binding of labeled proteins to authentic viral capsids at the single-particle level.

    The study demonstrates that capsid-binding molecules exhibit differential effects on the steps that control initiation and continuation of uncoating. The structural heterogeneity of the HIV capsid is well described, with the assembly process yielding a variety of conical, tubular, and other shapes that often contain structural defects such as gaps in the CA lattice. By observing the properties of many individual capsids, it is possible to classify them according to their uncoating behaviors. The findings of this study provide new insights into the intrinsic capsid disassembly pathway and how it can be modulated by different capsid-binding regulators.
The cJUN NH2-terminal kinase (JNK) signaling pathway plays a crucial role in maintaining genome stability and preventing tumor initiation in breast cancer. Contrary to the expectation that JNK signaling promotes cell proliferation by activating the AP1 transcription factor, loss-of-function mutations in the JNK pathway are frequently observed in breast cancer genomic DNA. To investigate the role of JNK signaling in breast cancer development, researchers examined the effect of JNK deficiency in the murine breast epithelium. The results showed that loss of JNK signaling caused genomic instability and the development of breast cancer. Moreover, JNK deficiency led to widespread early neoplasia and rapid tumor formation in a murine model of breast cancer. These findings suggest that JNK pathway defects are "driver" mutations that promote genome instability and tumor initiation.

The study found that JNK deficiency in mammary epithelial cells caused breast cancer, and accelerated tumor formation in a murine model of breast cancer. The effects of JNK deficiency were associated with widespread presence of early neoplasia and genomic instability. The researchers demonstrated that JNK plays a key role in the initiation of tumor development. The frequent JNK pathway loss-of-function mutations in human breast cancer may represent "driver" mutations that promote tumor development.

In contrast to the expected role of JNK signaling in promoting tumor growth, the study found that JNK loss plays a key role at the early stages of breast cancer development by promoting mammary gland neoplasia. This observation likely accounts for the effect of mutational inactivation of JNK signaling to promote tumor development. The JNK signaling pathway may be suppressed in many breast cancers, and the association of breast cancer with reduced JNK signaling represents a correlation. The study aimed to test the role of JNK signaling in breast cancer development and found that JNK deficiency in the mammary epithelium can cause murine breast cancer, and rapidly accelerates tumor development in a mouse model of breast cancer. The major conclusions of this study are that JNK deficiency in the mammary epithelium can cause murine breast cancer, and JNK deficiency rapidly accelerates tumor development in a mouse model of breast cancer.
A bioactive peptide amidating enzyme, peptidylglycine α-amidating monooxygenase (PAM), has been identified as a crucial factor in the assembly of cilia in various cell types. Cilia are microtubule-based structures that protrude from the surface of eukaryotic cells and play essential roles in sensory perception, motility, and signaling. PAM is responsible for generating amidated bioactive signaling peptides, and its activity has been linked to ciliogenesis in Chlamydomonas, planaria, and mice.

    In Chlamydomonas, PAM knockdown lines failed to assemble cilia beyond the transition zone, resulting in abnormal Golgi architecture and altered levels of cilia assembly components. Similarly, decreased PAM gene expression in planaria reduced motile ciliary density on the ventral surface and resulted in the appearance of cytosolic axonemes lacking a ciliary membrane. In mice, PAM deficiency led to aberrant architecture of primary cilia on neuroepithelial cells in embryos.

    The study suggests that PAM activity and alterations in post-Golgi trafficking contribute to ciliogenesis defects. PAM is required for the biogenesis of motile cilia in Chlamydomonas and on the ventral epithelium of planaria, as well as the normal assembly of primary cilia of neuroepithelial cells in mammals. The findings indicate a novel and highly conserved link between PAM, amidation, and ciliary assembly in multiple cell types.

    The results demonstrate that PAM plays a key role in the early steps of ciliogenesis, revealing a previously unknown connection between amidation and cilium assembly. The study also highlights the importance of PAM in the trafficking of signaling proteins to the ciliary compartment from the Golgi apparatus. While PAM is essential for ciliogenesis in Chlamydomonas and planaria, its complete loss in mice does not abolish primary cilia formation in neuroepithelial cells, although the cilia are significantly shorter than those in wild-type animals.

    Overall, the study provides new insights into the mechanisms of ciliogenesis and highlights the importance of PAM in the assembly of cilia in various cell types. The findings have significant implications for our understanding of the evolution and function of cilia in eukaryotic cells.
The interaction between class III myosins (Myo3) and the actin-bundling protein Espin plays a crucial role in regulating the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate hair cells. Stereocilia defects caused by mutations in Myo3 or Espin lead to hereditary hearing impairments. Myo3 interacts with Espin1 through its tail homology I motif (THDI), but the mechanism by which Myo3 acts through Espin1 to regulate actin bundle assembly and stabilization was unclear. This study reveals that Myo3 THDI contains a pair of repeat sequences capable of independently and strongly binding to the ankyrin repeats of Espin1, providing an unexpected Myo3-mediated cross-linking mechanism of Espin1. The structures of Myo3 in complex with Espin1 elucidate the binding mechanism and reveal a Myo3-induced release of Espin1 auto-inhibition. Furthermore, Myo3-mediated cross-linking promotes actin fiber bundling activity of Espin1. The authors propose that Myo3 binding can release auto-inhibition and increase the diameter of Espin1-promoted actin bundles. Myo3a and Myo3b co-localize with Espin1 at the tips of stereocilia of hair cells, and co-expression of Myo3a and Espin1 stimulates elongation of stereocilia in cultured organ of Corti hair cells. The study suggests that a key function of the Myo3/Espin1 interaction is to promote the formation of thicker parallel actin bundle fibers in cellular structures such as stereocilia. The Myo3/Espin1 interaction is mediated by the THDI, which allows Myo3 to transport Espin1 to the tips of actin bundle-based structures. Once localized, Espin1 promotes the elongation of actin protrusions. The study reveals a previously unrecognized direct myosin binding-induced actin bundling activity regulation mechanism of an actin filament cross-linker protein. Both Myo3a and Myo3b contain two highly similar repeat sequences in their THDI region, each capable of independently binding to Espin1-AR with high affinity. The full-length Myo3/Espin1 complex may have even higher actin fiber bundling activity than the Myo3-ABR12/Espin1 complex studied in this work.
Voltage-gated calcium channels (CaV) play a crucial role in controlling various cellular functions, including synaptic transmission in neurons and muscle contraction in heart and skeletal muscles. These channels activate at different voltages and with distinct kinetics to accomplish their diverse functions. To understand the molecular mechanisms governing specific voltage-sensing properties, researchers combined structure modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and transition kinetics of the activated and resting states of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) of the eukaryotic calcium channel CaV1.1.

The study found that both VSDs displayed the typical features of the sliding helix model, but differed significantly in ion-pair formation of the outer gating charges. Stabilization of the activated state enhanced the voltage dependence of activation, while stabilization of resting states slowed the kinetics. This mechanism provides a mechanistic model explaining how specific ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can realize the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels.

Voltage-gated calcium channels translate membrane depolarization into calcium influx, contributing to cellular excitability and coupling electrical activity to fundamental cell functions. They form a structurally related ion channel superfamily with a fourfold symmetry with voltage-gated sodium channels (NaV). The study's advanced structure models of the activated and resting states of CaV1.1 VSDs fill an important gap in the understanding of the voltage-sensing mechanism in eukaryotic voltage-gated cation channels.

The researchers used the Rosetta computational modeling software to build a homology model of the human eukaryotic CaV1.1 channel and generated a new structural model to study the molecular mechanisms determining the specific gating properties of this voltage-gated calcium channel. The study's findings were supported by mutagenesis and electrophysiology analysis, which showed that alterations of kinetic properties recognized experimentally were reliably reproduced and explained by the computer model. The structures of the lowest energy states of CaV1.1 VSDs display the basic features of the sliding helix model, providing a mechanistic understanding of how specific ion-pair formation in separate VSDs can realize the characteristic gating properties of voltage-gated cation channels.
The study investigated the role of matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) in adult visual plasticity in the healthy and stroke-affected brain. MMPs have been associated with experience-dependent plasticity in the juvenile brain, but their role in the adult brain is not well understood. The researchers examined the effects of MMP-inhibition on experience-induced plasticity in healthy and stroke-affected adult mice. They found that a 7-day application of MMP-inhibitor prevented visual plasticity in healthy animals. However, treatment with MMP-inhibitor once but not twice following stroke rescued plasticity, which is normally lost under these conditions. These findings suggest that an optimal level of MMP-activity is crucial for adult visual plasticity to occur. The study highlights the importance of precise regulation of MMP-activity for facilitating plasticity in the adult brain and offers new opportunities in treatment and recovery after stroke. The results also indicate that MMP-activity has to be within a narrow window for experience-induced plasticity to occur. The study expands on previous research, which has shown that MMPs play a vital role in adult visual cortex plasticity, particularly for the increase of open eye responses in V1 after monocular deprivation. The findings have implications for understanding the complex role of MMPs in brain development and function, particularly in the context of brain injury and recovery. The study suggests that MMP-inhibition may be a potential therapeutic strategy for rescuing lost plasticity after stroke, but further research is needed to fully explore this possibility. Overall, the study provides new insights into the critical role of MMPs in adult visual plasticity and highlights the importance of precise regulation of MMP-activity for facilitating plasticity in the adult brain.
Biomedical research has made significant progress in understanding the structure and function of AMPA (α-amino-3-hydroxy-5-methyl-4-isoxazole propionate) receptors, which are crucial for signal transduction at vertebrate excitatory synapses. AMPA receptors are tetrameric ligand-gated ion channels composed of four subunits that assemble as a dimer-of-dimers. Recent advances in cryo-electron microscopy have enabled the determination of full-length structures of AMPA receptors, both alone and in combination with auxiliary subunits. These structures suggest that the dimers may undergo substantial lateral motions during gating, resulting in the opening of the extracellular layer along the central twofold symmetry axis.

    To investigate the conformational flexibility of AMPA receptors, researchers used bifunctional methanethiosulfonate cross-linkers to calibrate the conformations of functional AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of the auxiliary subunit Stargazin. The results indicate that the extracellular layer of AMPA receptors can become trapped in stable, opened-up conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate. In contrast, the presence of Stargazin limits this conformational flexibility. Under synaptic conditions, where brief glutamate exposures and the presence of auxiliary proteins dominate, the extracellular domains of AMPA receptors are likely to remain compact during gating.

    The study suggests that the extracellular domains of synaptic AMPA receptors are unlikely to undergo large structural rearrangements during synaptic transmission and instead operate in a relatively compact conformational regime, unless faced with long exposures to glutamate in pathological conditions. The presence of auxiliary subunits, such as Stargazin, appears to play a crucial role in maintaining the compact conformation of AMPA receptors during synaptic transmission. The findings of this study provide new insights into the structural dynamics of AMPA receptors and highlight the importance of auxiliary subunits in regulating their conformational flexibility.
Title: High-Dimensional Analysis of Intestinal Immune Cells during Helminth Infection

    A novel protocol for isolating immune cells from the intestines of mice infected with helminths has been developed, overcoming the challenges posed by the strong anti-parasite type 2 immune response. This response is characterized by mucus production, tissue remodeling, and immune cell infiltration, making it difficult to obtain a representative sample of intestinal immune cells. By optimizing a standard intestinal digestion protocol, the researchers were able to isolate millions of immune cells from the heavily infected duodenum.

    To validate the accuracy of the isolated cells, a 23-color spectral flow cytometry panel was used to analyze the cells, incorporating hallmark surface and intracellular markers associated with helminth infections. The results confirmed the presence of known hallmarks of anti-parasite immune responses throughout the entire course of helminth infection. The findings were further validated using confocal microscopy, which revealed differences in cell ratios between naïve and infected samples.

    The study highlights the importance of good cell isolation techniques for investigating intestinal responses against helminths directly. Many of the observed changes were localized, emphasizing the need for precise cell isolation methods to accurately capture intestinal immune responses. The protocol developed in this study has the potential to accelerate single-cell discoveries of local helminth immune responses that were previously unfeasible.

    The researchers characterized the isolated cells further by quantifying specific immune cell populations, including B220+, Siglec F+, CD3+ CD4+, CD3+ CD4-, and CD64+ cells, using both spectral flow cytometry and confocal microscopy. The results showed that the cell isolation protocol provided an accurate representation of intestinal immune responses, allowing for the identification of many known hallmarks of innate and adaptive anti-parasite immune responses throughout the course of helminth infection.
The article presents a quantitative time-resolved analysis of the regulation of standard- and immuno-proteasomes, which are key determinants of protein half-life and cellular processes. Despite their importance, the mechanisms regulating proteasome activity are not fully understood. To address this knowledge gap, the authors developed and validated a mechanistic model of proteasomal peptide-hydrolysis dynamics using an iterative and tightly integrated experimental and computational approach. The model predicts the locations of proteolytic and regulatory sites on the 20S proteasome and the interactions between them, which are supported by experimental data. The analysis reveals that the rate-limiting step of hydrolysis is the transport of substrates into the proteasome, and that the transport efficiency varies between human standard- and immuno-proteasomes, affecting total degradation rate and substrate cleavage-site usage.

    The study combines carefully designed experimental assays with detailed modelling to shed light on the mechanisms of proteasome functions. The authors use short fluorogenic peptides with a single proteasome-catalyzed cleavage site to follow the kinetics in real-time, providing direct insights into proteasome dynamics. The results show similar proteasome dynamics over time when using purified 20S proteasome and protein homogenates, suggesting that the model describes a core of proteasome features that can reflect proteasome dynamics in more complex cellular environments.

    The 20S proteasome is a dynamic enzyme, and its catalytic activity varies over time due to interactions between substrates and products with proteolytic and regulatory sites. The kinetics change over longer time scales than previously described structural dynamics, and are influenced by the interactions of substrates and products with both proteolytic and regulatory sites. The analysis highlights the importance of understanding the mechanisms of proteasomal action, particularly the causes and extent of differences in substrate-specific proteolysis between different proteasome isoforms. The study provides a comprehensive mathematical model that describes the regulatory components of 20S proteasomes and the complex interactions between substrate/product transport and substrate hydrolysis over time for representative peptides.
The β-alanine transporter BalaT plays a crucial role in visual neurotransmission in Drosophila. The recycling of neurotransmitters is essential for sustained synaptic transmission, and in Drosophila, histamine recycling is required for visual synaptic transmission. Histamine is rapidly taken up by laminar glia and converted to carcinine, which is then delivered back to photoreceptors where it is hydrolyzed to release histamine and β-alanine. However, it was unclear how β-alanine is returned to the laminar glial cells. A new β-alanine transporter, BalaT, was identified and found to be expressed and required in retinal pigment cells for maintaining visual synaptic transmission and phototaxis behavior. Null balat mutants exhibited lower levels of β-alanine and less β-alanine accumulation in the retina. The results suggest that retinal pigment cells play a critical role in visual neurotransmission and that a BalaT-dependent β-alanine trafficking pathway is required for histamine homeostasis and visual neurotransmission. BalaT functions in retinal pigment cells to help recycle β-alanine, which is essential for maintaining histamine homeostasis and photoreceptor synaptic transmission. Disrupting the synthesis of histamine and carcinine abolishes photoreceptor synaptic transmission, and the transport of β-alanine seems less important as it is present at higher concentrations in Drosophila. The majority of pigment granules, which reduce the intensity of light exposure, are formed in retinal pigment cells of the Drosophila compound eye. High levels of β-alanine are detected in retinal pigment cells, suggesting that pigment cells may be critical for the transport and storage of β-alanine in this system. Mutations in balat disrupted transport and storage of β-alanine in the retina, resulting in dramatically reduced photoreceptor synaptic activity and visual behavior. This suggests a conserved function for β-alanine trafficking in mammals and Drosophila, and provides evidence for the formulation of a more complete histamine recycling pathway, which is critical for sustaining photoreceptor synaptic transmission.
The article reports on the discovery of a sudden redox collapse in chronologically aging yeast cells, which affects over 80% of identified thiol-containing proteins. This collapse occurs several days prior to cell death and is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants, contrary to the free radical theory of aging. Instead, it appears to be instigated by a substantial drop in cellular NADPH levels, which normally provides the electron source for maintaining cellular redox homeostasis. The decrease in NADPH levels occurs very early during lifespan and sets into motion a cascade that down-regulates most cellular processes. Caloric restriction, a near-universal lifespan-extending measure, increases NADPH levels and delays each facet of the cascade. The study reveals a time line of events leading up to the system-wide oxidation of the proteome days before cell death. The results suggest that chronological aging might represent a physiological stress condition that utilizes and stresses the redox buffering capacity of the thiol proteome. The study used chronologically aging Saccharomyces cerevisiae to determine the onset, extent, and targets of protein oxidation in postmitotic aging cells. By monitoring the thiol oxidation status of almost 300 different protein thiols, the researchers discovered that yeast cells undergo a global redox collapse that leads to massive thiol oxidation in almost 80% of identified proteins several days prior to cell death. The findings indicate that the observed decrease in cellular NADPH levels and the concomitant increase in cellular redox potential is not just a yeast-specific event but might be shared with other aging organisms as their metabolism changes. The study also found that yeast cells undergo the same pattern of NADPH decrease, Trr1 oxidation, and redox collapse when cultivated in 2% glucose or in 0. 5% glucose, suggesting that the redox collapse is a general feature of aging in postmitotic cells.
The study presents the mechanistic basis of D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD), a configuration-specific enzyme responsible for selectively removing D-amino acids that are erroneously coupled to transfer RNAs (tRNAs). This process is crucial for maintaining the homochirality of the biological macromolecular world, which is essential for cell survival. The researchers determined the crystal structure of DTD from Plasmodium falciparum in complex with a substrate-mimicking analog, revealing how the enzyme captures the chiral center of incoming D-aminoacyl-tRNA using an invariant Gly-cisPro dipeptide. This dipeptide is located in the active site of the dimeric enzyme and plays a central role in the recognition mechanism. The structure shows that no protein residues are directly involved in catalysis, but rather, the unique side chain-independent mode of substrate recognition allows DTD to act on multiple D-amino acids. This mechanism provides a clear explanation for the enzyme's ability to selectively remove D-amino acids from tRNAs. The study also highlights the importance of weak hydrogen bonds in D-chirality selection, as an L-amino acid would have to bind in one of three theoretically possible conformations to be recognized by the enzyme. The findings provide insights into a fundamental enantioselective mechanism involved in the enforcement of homochirality in proteins and elucidate a chiral enforcement mechanism with a crucial role in preventing D-amino acid infiltration during the evolution of the translational apparatus. The study builds upon earlier structural studies on DTD, which provided a mechanistic model based on docking approaches or complex structures with ligands that do not mimic the cognate substrate. The results suggest that DTD acts in trans as a freestanding module, recapturing aminoacyl-tRNAs from elongation factor Tu (EF-Tu) to remove D-amino acids from tRNAs. Overall, the study provides a detailed understanding of the mechanism of chiral proofreading during translation of the genetic code.
The cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) is a chloride ion channel that belongs to the ATP-binding cassette (ABC) protein superfamily. CFTR channels are gated by cytosolic ATP following phosphorylation of the cytosolic regulatory (R) domain by cAMP-dependent protein kinase (PKA). Recent studies have shown that ATP-binding-induced dimerization of two cytosolic nucleotide-binding domains (NBDs) opens the pore, while dimer disruption following ATP hydrolysis closes it. However, in certain cystic fibrosis (CF) mutants, spontaneous openings without ATP are rare in wild-type CFTR but constitute the only gating mechanism, stimulated by ivacaftor, a clinically approved CFTR potentiator.

    Researchers have investigated the molecular motions underlying spontaneous gating in CFTR channels. By correlating energetic coupling between residues across the dimer interface with spontaneous pore opening/closure in single CFTR channels, they found that spontaneous openings are strictly coupled to NBD dimerization, which may occur even without ATP. This suggests that coordinated NBD/pore movements are intrinsic to CFTR, and ATP alters the stability, but not the fundamental structural architecture, of open- and closed-pore conformations.

    The study also explored the mechanism by which binding of the natural ligand ATP promotes CFTR channel opening. The results indicate that ATP acts as a'molecular glue' to stabilize the transition state and the open state, rather than altering the global arrangement of conformations. This understanding provides insights into the correlated effects of phosphorylation, mutations, and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity.

    The findings have significant implications for understanding CF mutation and drug mechanisms. The strict coupling between NBD and pore movements, regardless of the absence or presence of ATP, constrains mechanistic models of ATP-dependent channel gating and helps explain modulatory effects of mutations, or of physiological or pharmacological gating regulators. The study also highlights the importance of understanding the underlying driving forces, which are likely conserved among ABC proteins.
The article presents a novel mechanism for the assembly of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers, which is mediated by a pre-transition effect. This effect arises from the first-order transition between ordered and disordered phases in the membrane. Using large-scale molecular simulations, the authors demonstrate that a protein with a hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer stabilizes a microscopic order-disorder interface. The stiffness of this interface is finite, and when two such proteins approach each other, they assemble due to the reduction in net interfacial energy. This phenomenon is analogous to the hydrophobic effect and is referred to as the "orderphobic effect." The strength and range of forces arising from this effect are significantly larger than those that could arise from membrane elasticity for the membranes considered. The orderphobic effect leverages the power of a phase transition, depending on the ability of the orderphobic protein to induce a disordered layer in its vicinity. This ability depends on the proximity to the membrane's phase transition and the protein's radius and hydrophobic mismatch with the membrane. For instance, a transmembrane protein with a hydrophobic thickness of approximately 2.6 nm will favor the structure of the disordered phase. If the protein is large enough, it can melt the ordered phase near the protein and result in the formation of an order-disorder interface. The prediction of enhanced lipid mobility surrounding orderphobic proteins may be amenable to experimental tests by single molecule tracking techniques. Biological membranes and transmembrane proteins are more complicated than the models considered in this paper, with multiple components allowing for more than one order-disorder transition. A dual to the orderphobic effect also exists, where a transmembrane protein in the disordered phase that favors the ordered phase can nucleate an ordered region and order-disorder interface. Interfaces separating the ordered and disordered regions will again provide a force for assembly. A consequence of the regular tail packing is that the hydrophobic thickness of the ordered phase is larger than that of the disordered phase. The area per lipid in the ordered phase is smaller than that in the disordered phase.
The relationship between simultaneously recorded spiking activity and fluorescence signals in GCaMP6 transgenic mice was investigated in a recent study. Fluorescent calcium indicators are commonly used to examine neural dynamics, but the connection between fluorescence and action potentials ( APs ) remains unclear. Most APs can be detected when the soma almost fills the microscope’s field of view, but calcium indicators are used to image populations of neurons, necessitating a large field of view, which generates fewer photons per neuron and compromises AP detection. The study characterized the AP-fluorescence transfer function in vivo for 48 layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in primary visual cortex, with simultaneous calcium imaging and cell-attached recordings from transgenic mice expressing GCaMP6s or GCaMP6f. Under optimal conditions, most APs were detected, but under conditions typical of population imaging studies, only a minority of 1 AP and 2 AP events were detected ( often <10% and ~20–30%, respectively ), emphasizing the limits of AP detection under more realistic imaging conditions. The study highlights the challenges of inferring the underlying AP train or firing rate from calcium imaging due to population imaging studies employing a large field of view containing many neurons. The AP to calcium-dependent fluorescence transfer function is typically characterized with a soma filling the field of view of the microscope, to maximize photon flux from the soma and thereby signal-to-noise ratio. To construct AP-calcium fluorescence response curves, isolated AP events were identified and summed over 250 ms. The results provide insight into the relationship between spiking activity in vivo and fluorescence signals and will aid the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging datasets. The study facilitates the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging studies using mainstream transgenic mouse lines, such as the Allen Institute’s Brain Observatory Visual Coding dataset. Previous studies have established that most APs can be detected with GCaMP6 indicators under near-optimal conditions. The study tested the performance of several spike inference models, detecting APs under optimal conditions and under the less optimal conditions typical of population imaging experiments. The results will aid the interpretation of existing and future calcium imaging datasets. Genetically encoded calcium indicators ( GECIs ) are widely used with two-photon laser scanning microscopy to report neuronal activity within local populations in vivo. This optical approach is minimally invasive and enables simultaneous measurement of activity from hundreds or even thousands of neurons at single-cell resolution, over multiple sessions.
In the Brazilian cave insect Neotrogla, a unique biological switching valve has been discovered within the female genitalia. This valve complex is composed of two plate-like sclerites, a closure element, and in-and-outflow canals, allowing females to control the direction of seminal flow. The female Neotrogla possesses a penis-like intromittent organ, which is used to coercively anchor males and obtain voluminous semen. The semen is packaged in a capsule that is not only used for fertilization but also consumed by the female as a source of nutrition. The valve complex has two slots for insemination, enabling females to receive multiple seminal packages from the same or different males within a short time span. This mechanism allows females to selectively use one slot for insemination while leaving the other empty, enabling them to immediately receive an additional seminal package from the same or other males. The presence of a spermathecal plate with a valve function further refines our understanding of the causes and consequences of the reversed sexual selection in this genus. The evolution of this switching valve may have been a prerequisite for the reversal of the intromittent organ in Neotrogla, allowing females to compete for seminal gifts in their nutrient-poor cave habitats through long copulation times and multiple seminal injections. The male-holding organ and female-induced long copulation durations of 40–70 hours in Neotrogla are considered exaggerated adaptations for such escalated competition for nuptial gifts in this group of insects inhabiting highly oligotrophic cave habitats. The discovery of this biological switching valve highlights the complexity and diversity of reproductive strategies in insects and provides new insights into the evolution of sex-role reversal in the genus Neotrogla.
Munc18-1 plays a crucial role in regulating the assembly of soluble N-ethylmaleimide-sensitive factor attachment receptor (SNARE) proteins, which are essential for the rapid exocytosis of neurotransmitters at synapses. The study used optical tweezers to observe four distinct stages of SNARE assembly, including the N-terminal, middle, C-terminal, and linker domains (NTD, MD, CTD, and LD, respectively). The researchers found that mutations in different SNARE layers differentially affect SNARE assembly and exocytosis, with the NTD and CTD responsible for vesicle docking and fusion, respectively, and the MD regulating SNARE assembly and fusion.

The study also investigated the role of Munc18-1 in SNARE assembly and found that it initiates SNARE assembly and structures the t-SNARE C-terminus independently of the syntaxin N-terminal regulatory domain (NRD). Munc18-1 stabilizes the half-zippered SNARE complex dependent on the NRD. The researchers used optical tweezers to observe the forces associated with Munc18-1-mediated de novo SNARE assembly and found that Munc18-1 enhances de novo SNARE assembly by holding the SNAP-25 molecule together with the tethered syntaxin or VAMP2 as the t-SNARE unfolds.

The study identified three force-dependent activities of Munc18-1 in SNARE assembly, consistent with previous reports of multiple modes of binding between Munc18-1 and the SNARE complex. The researchers propose a working model to account for the multiple roles of Munc18-1 in stage-wise SNARE zippering and membrane fusion. According to this model, Munc18-1 acts on the folding intermediates of SNAREs to chaperone their assembly. The study suggests that Munc18-1 regulates every stage of SNARE assembly, from initiation to stabilization of the half-zippered SNARE complex. The findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of synaptic exocytosis and the role of Munc18-1 in regulating SNARE assembly and membrane fusion.
The integrin adhesome, a complex of proteins involved in cell-matrix adhesion, has been found to be extensively pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks for adhesion sites. Using fluorescence cross-correlation spectroscopy (FCCS) and fluorescence recovery after photobleaching (FRAP), researchers investigated the dynamic exchange of these building blocks between adhesion sites and the cytosol. The results show that stationary focal adhesions release symmetrically the same types of protein complexes that they recruit, maintaining the cytosolic pool of building blocks in a spatiotemporally uniform state.

The study reveals that the integrin adhesome is pre-assembled in the cytosol as multi-protein building blocks, which can facilitate rapid and modular assembly of adhesion sites. The physical associations between adhesion site components are spatially uniform around stationary focal adhesions, indicating that these sites exchange their building blocks with the cytosol without altering them. In the cytosol, the building blocks cannot form larger structures due to dependencies between protein interactions.

The researchers propose that at the plasma membrane, the system can be locally switched on to self-assemble adhesion sites by passing through checkpoints that induce additional protein interactions in the integrin adhesome. This model suggests that the integrin adhesome is extensively pre-assembled in the cytosol, forming multi-protein building blocks for cell-matrix adhesion sites. The building blocks are size-confined and predominantly correspond to the functional layers across focal adhesions, including anchoring and integrin regulation, force transduction, and actin regulation.

Symmetric material exchange between adhesion sites and cytosol ensures that building blocks enter and exit adhesion sites without being altered, maintaining the assembly logic of the system. This process prevents the self-assembly process from losing control on the identity of co-recruited proteins, which would lead to the formation of aberrant structures. The study highlights the importance of integrating interaction-dependencies across the integrin adhesome to understand the assembly logic of adhesion sites.
Title: An Electrophysiological Marker of Arousal Level in Humans

    Summary
    A recent study has identified a common electrophysiological marker that can track states of reduced arousal in humans, including different sleep stages and anesthesia. The marker, known as the 1/f spectral slope, is a measure of the non-oscillatory, scale-free component of neural activity in the brain. The researchers used intracranial and surface EEG recordings from four independent data sets to demonstrate that the 1/f spectral slope can distinguish between wakefulness and reduced arousal states, including non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, rapid eye movement (REM) sleep, and general anesthesia with propofol.

    The study built on previous research that showed that the 1/f spectral slope can track arousal states in humans under anesthesia with propofol and during sleep. The researchers hypothesized that the spectral slope would become more negative (i.e., the power spectrum steeper) in sleep and under anesthesia compared to wakefulness. They also predicted that the spectral slope could discriminate wakefulness from NREM and REM sleep.

    The study analyzed two datasets obtained during a full night of sleep and found that the 1/f spectral slope can discern wakefulness from different sleep stages, including REM and NREM sleep. The researchers also demonstrated that the spectral slope mirrored the rapid changes in excitability observed over the course of a slow wave, providing a mechanistic link between the spectral slope and population synchrony during slow oscillations.

    The study's findings provide empirical evidence that the 1/f spectral slope in the 30-45 Hz frequency range reliably differentiates wakefulness from all three states of reduced arousal level, namely REM, N3 sleep, and general anesthesia with propofol. The 1/f dynamics are observed across a variety of tasks, and the spectral slope tracks changes in arousal levels in both propofol anesthesia and sleep in humans with high temporal precision and is observable on a wide range of timescales from sub-second epochs to full night recordings.

    The study's results from four independent studies provide five main advances: (1) the spectral slope tracks changes in arousal levels in both propofol anesthesia and sleep in humans with high temporal precision and is observable on a wide range of timescales; (2) the results provide empirical evidence for the notion that the slope in the range from 30 to 45 Hz correlates well with all stages of arousal; (3) the spectral slope can discriminate wakefulness from REM sleep solely based on the neurophysiological brain state; (4) the 1/f spectral slope is a common electrophysiological marker that tracks states of reduced arousal, including different sleep stages and anesthesia in humans; and (5) the spectral slope provides a mechanistic link between the spectral slope and population synchrony during slow oscillations.
Biomedical research has revealed that stromules, dynamic tubular extensions from chloroplasts, play a crucial role in innate immunity. Recent studies have shown that stromules connect with nuclei and are involved in various cellular processes. This study investigated the mechanism of stromule extension and movement in chloroplasts of green leaf tissue and perinuclear chloroplast clustering during innate immunity. The results demonstrate that microtubules (MTs) are essential for stromule extension and movement. MT depolymerization led to stromule retraction, while MT stabilization increased stromule frequency and length. In contrast, actin filaments (AFs) are not required for stromule extension but provide anchor points for stromules. Interestingly, there is a strong correlation between the direction of stromules from chloroplasts and the direction of chloroplast movement. Stromule-directed chloroplast movement was observed in steady-state conditions without immune induction, suggesting it is a general function of stromules in epidermal cells. The study proposes a model in which stromules extend along MTs and connect to AF anchor points surrounding nuclei, facilitating stromule-directed movement of chloroplasts to nuclei during innate immunity. Furthermore, the results show that MTs and AFs may facilitate perinuclear clustering of chloroplasts during an innate immune response. The findings provide mechanistic insights into how interactions with the cytoskeleton form and stabilize stromules, and reveal a new type of organellar movement along MTs that is stromule-directed. This study contributes to our understanding of the role of stromules in innate immunity and highlights the importance of cytoskeletal elements in plant cells. The results suggest that MT-mediated stromule extension and AF-mediated stromule anchoring are two complementary activities during stromule formation and movement. Future studies will investigate the molecular components required for stromule dynamics and stromule-directed movement, as well as the importance of perinuclear chloroplast clustering during innate immunity.
The article presents a method for predicting residue–residue interactions across protein interfaces using evolutionary information. The authors investigate whether amino acid sequence identities of residues that make contact across protein interfaces covary during evolution, which could be used to predict contacts across interfaces and assemble models of biological complexes. Using a pseudo-likelihood-based method, the authors find that residue pairs identified as covarying across protein–protein interfaces in the 50S ribosomal unit and 28 additional bacterial protein complexes with known structure are almost always in contact in the complex, provided that the number of aligned sequences is greater than the average length of the two proteins. This suggests that interactions can be accurately predicted across protein–protein interfaces given a sufficient number of aligned sequences.
