The authors apply this method to 36 evolutionarily conserved complexes of unknown structure and present structure models for four of the complexes, including the tripartite ATP-independent periplasmic ( TRAP ) transporter, the tripartite efflux system, the pyruvate formate lyase-activating enzyme complex, and the methionine ABC transporter. The results indicate that covarying pairs of positions are almost always in contact in the three-dimensional structure, provided there are sufficient aligned sequences. Furthermore, the authors find that significant residue–residue covariance occurs frequently between physically interacting protein pairs but very rarely between non-interacting pairs, suggesting that this method can be used to predict whether two proteins interact. The authors conclude that this method has the potential to contribute to the determination of the structures of biologically important complexes.
In a study on active sensing in visual pattern categorization, researchers employed a novel gaze-contingent paradigm to investigate how participants accumulate information from multiple locations in a scene. The results showed that participants' scan paths follow an active sensing strategy that incorporates information already acquired about the scene and knowledge of the statistical structure of patterns. This strategy was found to be more efficient than heuristic strategies that only employed a subset of the elements of a fully closed-loop active sensing strategy. Furthermore, the study estimated that a major portion of the apparent suboptimality of fixation locations arises from prior biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements. The central process of selecting fixation locations was found to be around 70% efficient in the task. By using a combination of a Bayesian ideal observer and an active sensor algorithm, the researchers were able to dissociate the contributions of the eye-movement selection process from those of perceptual, motor, and decision processes. This allowed them to identify predominant sources of apparent sub-optimality in active sensing and quantify the efficiency of choosing each individual fixation throughout scanning a whole scene. The study suggests that participants select eye movements with the goal of maximizing information about abstract categories that require the integration of information from multiple locations. The findings have implications for understanding how the brain processes visual information and how it can be optimized for tasks that require the integration of information from multiple locations. The study's approach is amenable to multiple tasks in different perceptual domains and provides a formalism that extends the range of active sensing to tasks with arbitrary, not necessarily spatial, latent features.
The nuclear pore complex (NPC) plays a crucial role in regulating the transport of molecules between the nucleus and cytoplasm in eukaryotic cells. Despite its importance, the mechanisms governing the transport of large cargoes, such as pathogens, mRNAs, and pre-ribosomes, through the NPC are not well understood. These large cargoes can be up to 15 nanometers in size and require specific molecular determinants for their nuclear import. This study aimed to investigate the requirements for nuclear transport of large cargoes, focusing on the role of multivalent binding to transport receptors via nuclear localization sequences (NLSs) and the effect of size on import efficiency.

    To address this question, the authors developed 30 large cargo models based on four capsid-like particles in the size range of 17-36 nanometers, with tunable numbers of up to 240 NLSs. The nuclear import kinetics of these cargoes were assayed, and the results showed that the requirements for nuclear transport scale non-linearly with size. A simple two-parameter biophysical model was developed to correlate the import flux with the energetics of large cargo transport through the NPC. This model revealed key molecular determinants of large cargo import in cells.

    Previous studies have shown that biochemical or physicochemical properties of the cargo surface influence nuclear transport for small cargoes. However, for large cargoes, the role of multivalent binding to transport receptors via NLSs is critical. The authors demonstrated that cargoes with multiple NLSs can rapidly enter the nucleus, and their nuclear import kinetics follow a mono-exponential behavior. The study also highlights the importance of the nuclear transport machinery, including Importinβ, Importinα, RanGDP, NTF2, and GTP, in reconstituting functional nucleocytoplasmic transport.

    The findings of this study provide new insights into the molecular determinants of large cargo transport into the nucleus and have implications for understanding the export of large cargoes, such as pre-ribosomal subunits and large RNA export complexes. The two-parameter biophysical model developed in this study can be used to predict the import efficiency of large cargoes and may aid in the development of new therapeutic strategies for diseases related to nucleocytoplasmic transport.
The co-stimulatory molecule CD28 plays a crucial role in the activation of helper T cells, but its function after T cell priming has been unclear. To investigate this, researchers generated mice with CD28 removed from CD4+ T cells after priming. The results show that continued CD28 expression is essential for the expansion of effector CD4+ T cells, particularly T helper type 1 (Th1) cells, and for the differentiation and maintenance of T follicular helper (Tfh) cells during viral infection. Additionally, maintained CD28 expression is required for the clearance of the bacterium Citrobacter rodentium from the gastrointestinal tract after oral infection. These findings demonstrate that CD28 persistence is necessary for helper T cell polarization in response to infection, revealing a novel function for CD28 distinct from its role in T cell priming. The study highlights the importance of CD28 in maintaining T cell responses following activation, which is critical for effective immunity to infection. The results suggest that CD28 expression is required for the expansion of Th1 cells and the differentiation and maintenance of Tfh cells, which are essential for clearing pathogens from the body. Furthermore, the study shows that CD28 expression is necessary for the clearance of Citrobacter rodentium from the gastrointestinal tract, indicating that CD28 plays a critical role in mucosal immunity. Overall, the study provides new insights into the function of CD28 in T cell responses and highlights its importance in maintaining effective immunity to infection.
Helicobacter pylori, a bacterium that colonizes the stomach, relies on its helical shape to efficiently colonize the stomach lining. However, the molecular mechanisms underlying the generation of this helical shape are not well understood. A recent study investigated the role of cytoskeletal proteins in maintaining the helical shape of H. pylori. The researchers used quantitative 3D microscopy to analyze the growth patterns of H. pylori cells and found that cell wall synthesis is enhanced at both the positive and negative Gaussian curvatures of the helix. They also found that the cytoskeletal proteins MreB and CcmA are localized to specific regions of the cell, with MreB enriched at negative Gaussian curvature and CcmA enriched at positive Gaussian curvature. The study suggests that MreB and CcmA play a crucial role in promoting cell wall synthesis at these regions, which is necessary for maintaining the helical shape of H. pylori. The researchers propose a model in which MreB and CcmA work together to pattern cell wall synthesis, with MreB promoting synthesis at negative Gaussian curvature and CcmA promoting synthesis at positive Gaussian curvature. This patterning is thought to be one mechanism necessary for maintaining the helical shape of H. pylori. The study also found that H. pylori achieves a broader distribution of sidewall Gaussian curvature than other curved-rod bacteria, such as Campylobacter crescentus and Vibrio cholerae, and uses distinct mechanisms to achieve these curvatures. Overall, the study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the helical shape of H. pylori and highlights the importance of cytoskeletal proteins in maintaining this shape. The findings have implications for our understanding of the biology of H. pylori and may inform the development of new treatments for H. pylori infections.
Cdc48, a ubiquitin-selective chaperone, plays a crucial role in regulating mitochondrial dynamics by integrating the activities of two deubiquitylases (DUBs), Ubp12 and Ubp2. These DUBs exert opposing effects on mitochondrial fusion by cleaving different ubiquitin chains on the mitofusin Fzo1. Cdc48 promotes the proteolysis of Ubp12, stabilizing pro-fusion ubiquitylation on Fzo1, while the loss of Ubp12 stabilizes Ubp2, facilitating the removal of ubiquitin chains on Fzo1 that inhibit fusion. This cascade ensures a tight control of Fzo1 ubiquitylation, allowing for mitochondrial fusion while preventing unnecessary ubiquitylation that targets Fzo1 for proteasomal turnover.

The study reveals a new cascade of ubiquitylation events involving Cdc48, Ubp12, and Ubp2, which fine-tunes the fusogenic activity of Fzo1. Cdc48 negatively regulates Ubp12, which in turn negatively regulates Ubp2, explaining why these two DUBs exert opposite effects on their targets and on ubiquitin homeostasis. The DUBs Ubp12 and Ubp2 cleave different ubiquitylated forms of Fzo1 that either promote or repress mitochondrial fusion. Ubp12-specific ubiquitylation precedes Ubp2-specific ubiquitylation on Fzo1, and unopposed anti-fusion ubiquitylation disrupts mitochondrial tubulation.

Ubp12 recognizes ubiquitylated chains on Fzo1 composed of a small number of ubiquitin moieties and does not stabilize its substrates because their ubiquitin chains are too short to target proteasomal turnover. In contrast, Ubp2 recognizes and cleaves ubiquitin chains on Fzo1 that target Fzo1 for proteasomal degradation and inhibit mitochondrial fusion. The study provides new insights into the regulation of mitochondrial fusion and highlights the importance of Cdc48 in integrating the activities of Ubp12 and Ubp2 to control the balance between activation and repression of mitochondrial fusion.
The study investigated whether vision is necessary for the development of the categorical organization of the Ventral Occipito-Temporal Cortex (VOTC). Using functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI), the researchers characterized VOTC responses to eight categories presented acoustically in sighted and early blind individuals, and visually in a separate sighted group. The results showed that VOTC reliably encodes sound categories in both sighted and blind people, using a representational structure and connectivity partially similar to the one found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind group than the sighted group, using a representational format closer to the one found in vision.

The study found that VOTC in blind individuals represents the categorical membership of sounds rather than their acoustic features. This suggests that sounds trigger categorical responses in the VOTC of congenitally blind and sighted people that partially match the topography and functional profile of the visual response, despite qualitative nuances in the categorical organization of VOTC between modalities and groups. The results demonstrate that a categorical organization similar to the one found in vision can emerge in VOTC in the absence of visual experience, and that such categorical response to sounds is actually enhanced and more stable in congenitally blind people.

Representational similarity analysis revealed that categorical membership is the main factor that predicts the representational structure of sounds in VOTC in blind people, rather than lower-level acoustic attributes of sounds. The study also found that VOTC reliably encodes the categorical membership of sounds from eight different categories in sighted and blind people, using a topography, representational format, and representational connectivity profile partially similar to the one observed in response to images of similar categories in vision.

However, the categorical representation of the auditory stimuli in VOTC of both blind and sighted individuals exhibits different qualitative nuances compared to the categorical organization generated by visual stimuli in sighted people. The study suggests that the overlap in categorical responses between groups may come from the fact that VOTC represents visual attributes in the sighted and acoustic attributes in the blind due to crossmodal plasticity.
Scientists have discovered a strain of the fungal pathogen Entomophthora muscae that manipulates the behavior of Drosophila melanogaster, a species of fruit fly, in the laboratory. The fungus, known as E. muscae ‘Berkeley’, induces a range of moribund behaviors in infected flies, including climbing upward, extending their proboscides, and raising their wings, which allows the fungus to launch infectious spores from their abdomens. The researchers found that E. muscae invades the nervous system of the host fly, suggesting a direct means by which the fungus could induce behavioral changes.

To study the E. muscae-D. melanogaster system, the researchers isolated and characterized the E. muscae ‘Berkeley’ strain and its impact on D. melanogaster behavior in the laboratory. They found that the invasion of the nervous system by E. muscae ‘Berkeley’ grants the fungus direct access to host neurons, which may be mechanistically important for achieving behavioral manipulation of the host fly. However, the researchers caution that there may be other possible ways E. muscae ‘Berkeley’ could alter host behavior.

The researchers propose four general mechanisms by which E. muscae is able to achieve behavioral manipulation of the host fly. To test whether E. muscae ‘Berkeley’ is capable of causing patent infection in hosts outside of Drosophilidae, they exposed house flies to E. muscae ‘Berkeley’-infected D. melanogaster and observed that the house flies died and subsequently sporulated, indicating that they had been killed by the fungus.

To gain a comprehensive understanding of how E. muscae ‘Berkeley’ infection progresses in D. melanogaster at the molecular level, the researchers measured how transcription changes in both host and fungus at 24-hour time points over 5 days. They found that early on, infected flies are phenotypically indistinguishable from uninfected animals, making it challenging to study the progression of the infection. The researchers believe that the E. muscae-D. melanogaster system will enable rapid progress in understanding how E. muscae manipulates host behavior, given the vast molecular toolkit available for D. melanogaster.
The study presents transcriptome-wide RNA-binding profiles of 30 general RNA degradation factors in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae. These factors are involved in mRNA deadenylation, decapping, exosome-mediated degradation, and RNA surveillance pathways, including nuclear RNA surveillance and cytoplasmic nonsense-mediated decay. The profiles were generated using photoactivatable ribonucleoside-enhanced crosslinking and immunoprecipitation (PAR-CLIP) and reveal the distribution of degradation factors between different RNA classes. The results are consistent with the canonical degradation pathway for closed-loop forming mRNAs after deadenylation. Modeling based on mRNA half-lives suggests that most degradation factors bind intact mRNAs, whereas decapping factors are recruited only for mRNA degradation, consistent with decapping being a rate-limiting step. Decapping factors preferentially bind mRNAs with non-optimal codons, consistent with rapid degradation of inefficiently translated mRNAs. Global analysis suggests that the nuclear surveillance machinery, including the complexes Nrd1/Nab3 and TRAMP4, targets aberrant nuclear RNAs and processes snoRNAs. The study provides a rich resource for future studies of eukaryotic RNA degradation pathways, mechanisms, and the integration of mRNA metabolism. Bioinformatics analysis of the profiles revealed how degradation factors vary in their binding specificities for different classes of RNAs and with respect to their preferred locations on RNA transcripts. Global comparisons of the profiles alongside previously published profiles of other RNA-binding factors revealed clusters of factors that co-occupy RNAs or co-localize on RNAs.
In Arabidopsis thaliana, camalexin is a potent defense compound that can be rapidly induced in response to bacterial and fungal infections. However, the mechanisms underlying the rapid activation of camalexin biosynthesis genes in response to a pathogen signal are not fully understood. To investigate this, researchers combined publicly available epigenomic data with in vivo chromatin modification mapping and found that camalexin biosynthesis genes are marked with two epigenetic modifications : trimethylation of lysine 27 of histone 3 ( H3K27me3 ) and acetylation of lysine 18 of histone 3 ( H3K18ac ). These modifications have opposite effects on gene expression, with H3K27me3 repressing and H3K18ac activating gene expression. The researchers discovered that these two modifications co-localize on camalexin biosynthesis genes to form a previously uncharacterized type of bivalent chromatin, which they named kairostat. Mutants with reduced H3K27me3 or H3K18ac showed that both modifications are required to determine the timing of gene expression and metabolite accumulation at an early stage of the stress response. The study suggests that the H3K27me3-H3K18ac bivalent chromatin plays an important role in controlling the timely induction of gene expression upon stress stimuli in plants. By integrating epigenomic profiles with a genome-wide metabolic network in Arabidopsis, the researchers found that H3K27me3 and H3K18ac are enriched at camalexin biosynthesis and several other specialized metabolic pathways. In mutants with defective deposition of H3K27me3 or H3K18ac, the induction kinetics of camalexin biosynthesis genes and metabolite accumulation was accelerated or delayed, respectively. These findings provide new evidence for how chemical defense mediated by camalexin may be regulated at the epigenetic level. The study highlights the importance of H3K27me3-H3K18ac bivalent chromatin in controlling the timely induction of gene expression in response to stress stimuli in plants.
The study investigated the role of EZH2, a histone methyltransferase, in neurogenic astrocytes of the adult mouse subventricular zone (SVZ). Astrocytes that serve as neural stem cells (NSCs) in the SVZ express EZH2, which is required for neurogenesis independent of its role in SVZ NSC proliferation. The study found that EZH2 directly represses the Ink4a/Arf locus to enable SVZ NSC self-renewal, and its repression of Olig2, a bHLH transcription factor, is critical for neuronal differentiation. Furthermore, EZH2 prevents the inappropriate activation of genes associated with non-SVZ neuronal subtypes. In the human brain, SVZ cells, including local astroglia, also express EZH2, correlating with postnatal neurogenesis. The study establishes EZH2 as a distinguishing feature of astroglial cells in the postnatal neurogenic SVZ of both the mouse and human brain, and identifies distinct and separable roles for EZH2 in the production of new neurons from a multipotent stem cell population. These results provide novel insights into the complex role that EZH2 plays in normal development and gliomagenesis. The study also suggests that EZH2 is a critical epigenetic determinant for the maintenance of multipotent astroglia, enabling both proliferation and cell fate specification of an adult stem cell population. Acute deletion of Ezh2 in SVZ NSCs resulted in decreased neurogenesis, and this deficiency in part related to reduced cell proliferation. Re-expression of Ezh2 in Ezh2-deleted, Ink4a/Arf-null SVZ NSCs rescued neurogenesis, indicating that EZH2 has a role in SVZ neurogenesis that is distinct from its function in Ink4a/Arf repression. The study highlights the importance of EZH2 in regulating distinct and separable aspects of adult stem cell biology, which has important implications for regenerative medicine and oncogenesis. Future studies may address whether re-expression of EZH2 in non-SVZ astrocytes can enable neurogenesis or their directed transdifferentiation into neurons in vivo for potential therapeutic purposes.
This study investigated the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncultivated SUP05 bacteria in a model marine oxygen minimum zone (OMZ). Single-cell amplified genomes (SAGs) from 127 SUP05 bacteria isolated from the OMZ were analyzed to identify 69 viral contigs representing five new genera within dsDNA Caudovirales and ssDNA Microviridae. The results suggest that approximately one-third of SUP05 bacteria are infected with viruses, with higher infection frequencies observed in areas with more severe oxygen deficiency. The study also found evidence of co-infection between dsDNA and ssDNA viruses, indicating previously unrecognized cooperation modes. Analyses of 186 microbial and viral metagenomes revealed that SUP05 viruses persisted for years, but remained endemic to the OMZ. Furthermore, the study identified virus-encoded dissimilatory sulfite reductase, suggesting that SUP05 viruses reprogram their host's energy metabolism. The findings demonstrate closely coupled SUP05 virus-host co-evolutionary dynamics with the potential to modulate biogeochemical cycling in climate-critical and expanding OMZs. The study used single-cell genomics to directly link SUP05 viruses and their hosts across defined gradients of oxygen and sulfide over a 3-year time interval in a model OMZ ecosystem. This spatiotemporal resolution revealed endemic patterns of co-infection between ssDNA and dsDNA viruses and the occurrence of auxiliary metabolic genes (AMGs) with the potential to modulate electron transfer reactions essential to SUP05 energy metabolism. SUP05 Microviridae contigs were inconsistently detected due to methodological biases, so the study focused on dsDNA viral contigs. All 4 SUP05 Caudovirales contigs were absent from surface waters, but repeatedly detected within and below the oxycline, consistent with SUP05 water column disposition. The study also found that putative SUP05 phage contained genes encoding reverse dissimilatory sulfite reductase A and C, positing a role for viruses in modulating the marine sulfur cycle.
The study investigated the role of TTC26/DYF13, a putative intraflagellar transport (IFT) protein, in the assembly and maintenance of cilia and flagella in mammalian cells and Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Using live imaging and biochemical approaches, the researchers found that TTC26/DYF13 is an IFT complex B protein in both mammalian cells and C. reinhardtii. Knockdown of TTC26/DYF13 in zebrafish embryos or mutation of TTC26/DYF13 in C. reinhardtii resulted in short cilia with abnormal motility, but surprisingly, IFT particle assembly and speed were normal in the dyf13 mutant flagella. Proteomic and biochemical analyses revealed that a specific set of proteins involved in motility was depleted in the dyf13 mutant. These results suggest that different IFT proteins are responsible for transporting different cargo subsets, providing a possible explanation for the complexity of the IFT machinery. The study also found that TTC26/DYF13 is not required for ciliogenesis per se, but rather plays a specialized role in transporting a specific set of motility-related ciliary proteins into cilia/flagella. In contrast to other IFT proteins, TTC26/DYF13 is not necessary for the assembly or movement of the IFT particle, but rather is involved in the transport of a specific subset of proteins involved in flagellar motility. The study provides new insights into the role of TTC26/DYF13 in the assembly and maintenance of cilia and flagella, and highlights the complexity of the IFT machinery. Further analysis is necessary to clarify the role of TTC26/DYF13 in determining the length of cilia and flagella.
The interaction between melanoma cells and lymphatic endothelial cells ( LECs ) plays a crucial role in the metastasis of melanoma, a type of skin cancer. Research has shown that the presence of LECs in the tumor microenvironment can stimulate the growth and spread of melanoma cells to distant organs. A recent study investigated the molecular mechanisms underlying this process and found that LECs induce the activation of matrix-metalloproteinase-14 ( MMP14 ), Notch3, and β1-integrin in melanoma cells, leading to an invasive sprouting phenotype. This phenotype is characterized by the ability of melanoma cells to invade and spread through three-dimensional matrices. The study used a combination of 2D and 3D co-culture models, transcriptome profiling, in vitro functional studies, and a zebrafish xenograft/metastasis assay to investigate the effects of LECs on melanoma cells. The results showed that the interaction between LECs and melanoma cells increased the metastasis of melanoma xenografts in mice, which was dependent on MMP14 and Notch3. The study also found that the upregulation of Notch3 upon LEC priming was a key event in the metastatic process, and that β1-integrin activation was required for the 3D sprouting of melanoma cells. The findings of this study provide new insights into the role of LECs in the metastasis of melanoma and highlight the importance of MMP14 and Notch3 in this process. The study also suggests that the LEC-melanoma interaction model can be used to identify previously unrecognized pathways and molecules contributing to lymphatic metastasis of melanoma. The results of this study have important implications for the development of new therapeutic strategies to prevent or treat melanoma metastasis.
The study investigated the role of microRNA-9 (miR-9) in dendritic development and synaptic transmission in the brain. miR-9 is one of the most abundant microRNAs in the brain and has been well-characterized in neural progenitors, but its function in dendritic and synaptic development has remained largely unknown. To address this knowledge gap, the researchers developed a transgenic mouse line that allows for the conditional inactivation of miR-9 in a spatio-temporal-controlled manner. Using this novel approach, the study found that miR-9 plays a crucial role in controlling dendritic growth and synaptic transmission in vivo. Furthermore, the study demonstrated that miR-9-mediated downregulation of the transcriptional repressor REST is essential for proper dendritic growth. The results of this study define a key role for miR-9 during postmitotic differentiation of neurons and reveal a novel developmental function of miR-9 in the postmitotic stages of neuronal differentiation. The study used a double reporter mouse line to characterize the activity pattern of miR-9 in different cell types in the brain and found that inhibition of miR-9 results in aberrant dendritic growth in vivo, accompanied by a concomitant impairment in synaptic transmission. The study also found that basal synaptic transmission was impaired in mice expressing the miR-9 sponge. The results suggest that miR-9 exerts its function in modeling dendritic arbors as a key player of neuronal genetic programs controlling dendrite development. Overall, the study provides new insights into the role of miR-9 in dendritic development and synaptic transmission and highlights the importance of miR-9 in the postmitotic stages of neuronal differentiation.
The study investigated the neural basis of social visual engagement difficulties in autism spectrum disorder (ASD) by examining resting state functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data from a large sample of ASD toddlers and non-ASD comparison groups. The results showed that ASD-related functional hypoconnectivity was observed between the default mode network (DMN) and visual and attention networks. Specifically, a subtype of ASD characterized by pronounced early social visual engagement difficulties, referred to as GeoPref ASD, was found to have marked DMN-occipito-temporal cortex (OTC) hypoconnectivity. This hypoconnectivity was also related to increased severity of social-communication difficulties, but only in GeoPref ASD. The study suggests that early and pronounced social-visual circuit hypoconnectivity is a key underlying neurobiological feature describing GeoPref ASD and may be critical for future social-communicative development and represent new treatment targets for early intervention in these individuals. The findings provide evidence for atypical intrinsic functional brain organization in early ASD development, involving circuitry relevant to social visual engagement, and highlight the importance of heterogeneity in early social engagement in explaining functional connectivity differences present in early ASD toddlers. The study used a simple and easy-to-implement eye tracking test to identify the GeoPref ASD subtype, which can be used as a robust behavioral assay for early ASD diagnosis. The results have implications for understanding the neural basis of social visual engagement difficulties in ASD and may inform the development of targeted interventions for early ASD diagnosis and treatment.
In Drosophila melanogaster, the hematopoietic system produces two types of mature hemocytes: plasmatocytes and crystal cells. Plasmatocytes are phagocytic cells similar to vertebrate macrophages and are mitotically active, increasing in number throughout larval development through self-renewal. However, the increase in crystal cell numbers during larval development remains unexplained, as mature crystal cells have not been observed dividing during this stage and crystal cells in the lymph gland do not enter circulation under homeostatic conditions. To address this paradox, researchers investigated the origin of new crystal cells in third instar larvae and found that they arise through a Notch-dependent process of plasmatocyte transdifferentiation. This process occurs in sessile hemocyte clusters and is contingent upon the integrity of these structures. The study reveals that the microenvironment provided by hemocyte clusters is necessary to induce crystal cell differentiation from plasmatocytes, involving a cell-contact mechanism involving Notch-Serrate signaling. The existence of this hematopoietic tissue, which relies on structure-dependent signaling events to promote blood homeostasis, creates a new paradigm for understanding Drosophila hematopoiesis and establishes further parallels with vertebrate systems. The findings suggest that the study of hematopoietic organs and their structure and function is essential to understand how different mature blood cells arise and how their absolute and relative numbers are controlled. This novel hematopoietic organ, dynamically attached to the circulating hemocyte population and relying on structure-dependent signaling events, brings us closer to addressing outstanding fundamental questions of Drosophila hematopoiesis.
Aneuploidy, or the presence of an abnormal number of chromosomes, has been linked to various diseases but also plays a role in organismal evolution. While laboratory strains are highly sensitive to aneuploidy, researchers have found that aneuploidy is common in wild yeast isolates, which exhibit lower-than-expected expression at many amplified genes. To investigate this phenomenon, the authors generated diploid strain panels with cells carrying two, three, or four copies of the affected chromosomes and observed that gene-dosage compensation functions at 10–30% of amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage compensation are under higher expression constraint in wild populations but show elevated rates of gene amplification, suggesting that copy-number variation is buffered at these genes. The authors also found that aneuploidy provides a clear ecological advantage to oak strain YPS1009 by amplifying a causal gene that escapes dosage compensation. This study presents a model in which dosage compensation buffers gene amplification through aneuploidy to provide a natural, but likely transient, route to rapid phenotypic evolution. The results show that up to 40% of amplified genes in naturally aneuploid strains exhibit lower-than-expected expression, which is in part due to heritable polymorphisms that down-regulate expression from amplified genes. These findings provide new insight into the long-standing conundrum of how cells tolerate duplication of toxic genes long enough for new phenotypes to evolve. The study also raises the possibility of natural genetic variation in dosage compensation, where the expression of dosage-compensated genes is controlled in proportion to gene copy, but many genes escape dosage compensation in response to aneuploidy and can contribute significantly to phenotypic variation.
The article reports the discovery of extraocular, rod-like photoreceptors in the flatworm Maritigrella crozieri that express the xenopsin photopigment. Xenopsin is a recently classified subtype of opsin that was initially thought to belong to the Gαi-coupled ciliary opsins but has since been found to have diverged from ciliary opsins in pre-bilaterian times. The study characterizes xenopsin in Maritigrella crozieri and finds that it is expressed in ciliary cells of the eyes in the larva and in extraocular cells around the brain in the adult. These extraocular cells house hundreds of cilia in an intra-cellular vacuole, known as a phaosome. Functional assays in human cells show that Maritigrella xenopsin drives phototransduction primarily by coupling to Gαi. The findings highlight similarities between xenopsin and c-opsin and reveal a novel type of opsin-expressing cell that, like jawed vertebrate rods, encloses the ciliary membrane within their own plasma membrane. In the adult, xenopsin+ extraocular, ciliary cells were found to have a distinct ultrastructure, similar to that of jawed vertebrate rod photoreceptors. The study also carried out the first functional cellular assays on a xenopsin, exploring whether polyclad xenopsin can form a photopigment and which classes of Gα protein it can couple to. The results suggest that xenopsin couples to Gαi, as evidenced by primary activation of a Gαi signal transduction cascade in living human cells and co-localization of xenopsin and Gαi in the same putative ciliary photoreceptor in a flatworm. Additionally, xenopsin protein expression was found on cilia of cells that form eyes, supporting the possibility that the VxPx motif is involved in transporting xenopsin to cilia. The discovery of xenopsin+ ciliary cells in the eyes of protostome larvae challenges our views on opsin and photoreceptor evolution by deviating from the invertebrate trend of extraocular ciliary photoreceptors and ocular rhabdomeric/microvillar photoreceptors. Overall, the study provides molecular and functional evidence for the presence of ciliary photoreceptors in flatworms and highlights the similarities between xenopsin and c-opsin.
The integration of spatial and temporal cues is crucial for the specification of neuronal diversity in neural progenitors. However, the mechanisms by which these cues are integrated remain unknown. Drosophila neuroblasts, which are individually identifiable and have known spatial and temporal transcription factors, provide a suitable model system to investigate this process. This study aimed to determine whether spatial and temporal factors act independently or sequentially in neuroblasts by examining the genomic binding sites of the temporal transcription factor Hunchback (Hb) in two neuroblasts (NB5-6 and NB7-4) that produce different progeny.

    Targeted DamID was used to identify the genomic binding sites of Hb in NB5-6 and NB7-4. The results showed that Hb targets were different in each neuroblast, ruling out the independent specification model. Furthermore, each neuroblast had distinct open chromatin domains, which correlated with differential Hb-bound loci in each neuroblast. The study also found that the spatial factor Gsb/Pax3, expressed in NB5-6 but not NB7-4, had genomic binding sites correlated with open chromatin in NB5-6, but not NB7-4.

    The data support a model in which early-acting spatial factors like Gsb establish neuroblast-specific open chromatin domains, leading to neuroblast-specific temporal factor binding and the production of different neurons in each neuroblast lineage. The study identified over 100 loci that are differentially bound by Hb in NB5-6 or NB7-4, which are excellent candidates for lineage-specific neuronal specification. These findings contribute to our understanding of how spatial and temporal cues are integrated during neurogenesis and provide insights into the mechanisms of neuronal diversity in Drosophila and potentially in mammals. The study highlights the importance of early-acting spatial factors in establishing neuroblast-specific open chromatin domains, which in turn influence the binding of temporal transcription factors and the production of different neurons in each neuroblast lineage.
Scientists have long sought to understand how P2X receptors function by opening a transmembrane pore in response to extracellular ATP. Recent crystal structures have revealed molecular motions of the extracellular domain following agonist binding, but the mechanism of pore opening remains unclear. To investigate this, researchers used photo-switchable cross-linkers as ‘molecular tweezers’ to monitor inter-residue distances in the transmembrane domain of the P2X2 receptor during activation. These experimentally based structural constraints were combined with computational studies to provide high-resolution models of the channel in the open and closed states. The results show that the extent of the outer pore expansion is significantly reduced compared to the ATP-bound structure, and that the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices come closer during opening, likely through a hinge-bending motion. This study provides new insight into the gating mechanism of P2X receptors and establishes a versatile strategy applicable to other membrane proteins. The researchers used photo-switchable azobenzene tweezers to cross-link engineered cysteine residues in the transmembrane domain ( TMD ) of the rat P2X2 receptor. By controlling the end-to-end distance of the tweezers with light, they were able to monitor a series of inter-residue distances in the TMD during activation. The data revealed a critical ‘hinge’ in the TM2 helices that facilitates their vertical bending, allowing the inner and outer ends of adjacent TM2 helices to come closer during activation. This study provides the first application of photo-switchable derivatives to investigate the mechanism of pore gating in P2X receptors. The results are consistent with previous studies suggesting that breaking of one TM2-TM2 interface is sufficient for gating, and that the light-induced outward expansion of a single TM2-TM2 interface is likely a local rearrangement of the TMD. The study also highlights the importance of understanding the gating mechanism of full-length, membrane-embedded P2X receptors, as previous X-ray structures may represent non-native, open-channel states.
The regulation of cytokinesis, the physical division of one cell into two, has long been thought to be a conserved process across animal cells. However, recent evidence suggests that cytokinetic regulation in individual cell types may exhibit more variation than previously assumed. In the four-cell Caenorhabditis elegans embryo, each blastomere has a distinct cell fate, specified by conserved pathways. Using fast-acting temperature-sensitive mutants and acute drug treatment, researchers identified cell-type-specific variation in the cytokinetic requirement for a robust forminCYK-1-dependent filamentous-actin (F-actin) cytoskeleton. This variation was found to be cell-intrinsically regulated in one cell (P2) and cell-extrinsically regulated in another cell (EMS), dependent on both SrcSRC-1 signaling and direct contact with its neighbor cell, P2.

    The study used thermogenetics, drug treatment, and embryo microdissection to probe the mechanisms of cell-type-based variation in the regulation of cytokinesis among individual blastomeres within the four-cell C. elegans embryo. The results showed cell-type-specific differences in both the functional levels and temporal window of activity required for forminCYK-1 activity, but not myosin-IINMY-2 activity, during cytokinesis. In contrast to the one-cell embryo, forminCYK-1 (ts) embryos showed cell-type variation in the temporal requirement for activity among individual blastomeres.

    The researchers used fast-acting temperature-sensitive (ts) alleles to inactivate two cytokinesis proteins essential for contractile ring constriction in the one-cell embryo: the motor myosin-II (NMY-2 in C. elegans, hereafter myosin-IINMY-2) and the diaphanous formin CYK-1 (hereafter forminCYK-1). They identified cell-type-specific variation in four-cell embryos in the molecular requirement for forminCYK-1, but not myosin-IINMY-2. These findings suggest that both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic regulatory mechanisms contribute to cytokinetic diversity and promote robust cytokinesis when the contractile ring is weakened in specific cell types within the four-cell C. elegans embryo.

    The study demonstrates that each cell in the four-cell C. elegans embryo has a unique cell identity and can be individually scored for contractile ring constriction during cytokinesis. The combination of thermogenetics with cell-type-specific in vivo and ex vivo analysis of cytokinesis in each blastomere of the four-cell C. elegans embryo allowed the researchers to study the mechanisms of cytokinetic variation. The results show that both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms control cytokinetic variation in individual cell types and can protect against division failure when the contractile ring is weakened.
Bacterial flagella play a crucial role in mediating host-microbe interactions through tissue tropism during colonization and activation of immune responses. The flagellar shaft of certain bacteria, including several human pathogens, is encased in a membranous sheath of unknown function. While it has been hypothesized that the sheath may allow these bacteria to evade host responses to the immunogenic flagellin subunit, this structural feature has remained an enigma. A recent study has demonstrated that the rotation of the sheathed flagellum in both the mutualist Vibrio fischeri and the pathogen Vibrio cholerae promotes the release of a potent bacteria-derived immunogen, lipopolysaccharide (LPS), found in the flagellar sheath. This finding reveals a new role for the flagellar sheath in triggering, rather than circumventing, host immune responses in the model squid-vibrio symbiosis. The study used the model squid-vibrio symbiosis to probe the effect of the enigmatic flagellar sheath on host recognition and uncovered a previously unrecognized role for the rotation of sheathed flagella in mediating the release of LPS in both V. fischeri and V. cholerae. The flagellar sheath has been difficult to study due to the lack of mutations that result in the loss of a sheath in otherwise sheathed flagella. The functional role of the sheath, even as it relates to bacterial motility, remains poorly characterized. The study's findings further our understanding of this enigmatic structure and its contribution to bacterial virulence. Further work will build upon these discoveries and clarify the mechanisms underlying signaling during initiation of the squid-vibrio symbiosis, including characterizing the composition of LPS released by V. fischeri during flagellar rotation, identifying the squid receptors that recognize V. fischeri LPS, and determining any role flagellin may have in activating host responses. The study suggests that biosynthesis of the flagellum, and its subsequent functions, may mediate multiple aspects of symbiotic initiation. The findings have implications for the study of bacterial pathogens with sheathed flagella and raise important biophysical questions of sheathed-flagellum function.
The article describes a phenomenon of catastrophic chromosomal restructuring during genome elimination in plants, specifically in Arabidopsis, resulting from the hybridization of plants expressing different centromere histones H3. This process leads to the formation of shattered chromosomes from the genome of the haploid inducer, consistent with genomic catastrophes affecting a single, laggard chromosome compartmentalized within a micronucleus. Analysis of breakpoint junctions suggests that breaks are followed by repair through non-homologous end joining (NHEJ) or stalled fork repair.

    The study found that mutation of the NHEJ factor DNA Ligase 4 results in enhanced haploid recovery, indicating that NHEJ plays an important role in the repair of the haploid inducer chromosomes that contribute to diploid and aneuploid progeny. Inhibition of NHEJ leads to an increase in haploid induction frequency, suggesting that unrepaired double-stranded DNA breaks increase the elimination of the haploid inducer genome. The study also suggests that missegregated chromosomes enter a degradative pathway initiated by endonucleolytic breaks, and occasionally, such chromosomes are rescued.

    The results provide evidence for the occurrence of chromosome restructuring when diverged individuals hybridize, identifying a centromere-based mechanism for genomic instability. This phenomenon depends on chimeric CENH3, but a similar effect was observed when the haploid inducer strain expresses CENH3 of a close species. The study also highlights the importance of NHEJ in repairing breaks on shattered chromosomes, contributing to the formation or persistence of aneuploid and diploid progeny.

    The findings have implications for understanding the causes and mechanisms of complex genomic rearrangements similar to those associated with several human disorders. The study provides a tractable, natural system for investigating the consequences of mitotic malfunction on genome integrity, which can be dire, leading to aneuploidy and catastrophic restructuring. The results suggest that the study of genome elimination in plants can provide insights into the mechanisms of genomic instability and the potential for enduring genomic novelty.
Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) is a causative agent of several human cancers, and its latent infection is characterized by the expression of viral oncogenes that promote cell growth and inhibit the apoptotic response to uncontrolled proliferation. The EBV oncoprotein LMP1 plays a critical role in the survival of EBV-immortalized B cells by constitutively activating NFκB. However, during early infection, EBV induces rapid B cell proliferation with low levels of LMP1 and little apoptosis. To elucidate the mechanism of survival in the absence of LMP1/NFκB early after infection, researchers employed BH3 profiling to investigate mitochondrial regulation of apoptosis in EBV-infected cells. The study revealed a dynamic change in B cell survival mechanisms, transitioning from uninfected B cells (BCL-2) to early-infected cells (MCL-1/BCL-2) and immortalized cells (BFL-1). This transition is unique to virus-infected cells and relies on the regulation of MCL-1 mitochondrial localization and BFL-1 transcription by the viral EBNA3A protein. EBNA3A regulates the BH3 profile of EBV-infected cells, suppressing apoptosis by promoting MCL-1 mitochondrial localization and increasing BFL-1 mRNA transcription. Furthermore, the study identified a new role for EBNA3A in the suppression of apoptosis during both early and late EBV-induced B cell proliferation. The findings suggest that uninfected human peripheral blood B cells depend solely on BCL-2 for survival, while EBV upregulates additional survival proteins, including MCL-1 and BFL-1, to potently suppress apoptosis induced by BCL-2 antagonism. The study highlights the importance of EBNA3A in apoptosis resistance during EBV infection and has implications for EBV lymphomagenesis.
The study of Arabidopsis thaliana, a model organism for plant biology, has revealed that genetic variation, environment, and demography intersect to shape the diversity of defense metabolites across Europe. The researchers analyzed a population of 797 natural Arabidopsis accessions and found that a combination of geography, environmental parameters, demography, and genetic processes influence the specific chemotypes and their distribution. This suggests that causal loci in specialized metabolism contain frequent independently generated alleles with patterns indicating potential within-species convergence. The study highlights the complexity of the selective forces and mechanisms that shape the generation and distribution of allelic variation influencing local adaptation. The Arabidopsis-GSL system is an optimal model to study species-wide processes driving specialized metabolite variation due to the known identity of the whole biosynthetic pathway and major causal loci for natural variation. The level of allelic diversity at these loci raises questions about how pathways with this level of diversity and structural variation pass through speciation boundaries. The study used a family of specialized metabolites, aliphatic GSLs, and measured their amounts in seeds of A. thaliana to query how genetics, geography, environment, and demography intersect to shape chemotypic variation across Europe. The results show that the intersection of environmental pressure, demography, and genomic complexity gives rise to the pattern of metabolic variation across a plant species. The study provides a new perspective on the complexity of the selective forces and mechanisms that shape the generation and distribution of allelic variation influencing local adaptation. The findings have implications for understanding how genomic variation, demography, and environmental pressure shape the variation of specialized metabolism within a species.
Apoptotic caspases have traditionally been regarded as tumor suppressors due to their role in programmed cell death. However, recent studies suggest that under certain conditions, these caspases can also promote tumor growth and proliferation. In Drosophila, a well-studied neoplastic tumor model, oncogenic mutations of the proto-oncogene Ras ( RasV12 ) maintain tumorous cells in an ‘undead’-like condition, transforming caspases from tumor suppressors into tumor promoters. Instead of inducing apoptosis, caspases in RasV12-modified cells generate intra- and extracellular reactive oxygen species ( ROS ), which recruit and activate macrophage-like immune cells. These immune cells signal back to tumorous epithelial cells to activate oncogenic JNK signaling, creating a feedback amplification loop that promotes caspase activity. This loop is essential for uncontrolled tumor growth and invasive behavior. Interfering with the amplification loop significantly reduces the neoplastic behavior of these cells and improves organismal survival. The study demonstrates that caspases can adopt a tumor-promoting role under specific conditions, such as in the presence of oncogenic RasV12 in scrib mutant cells. The tumor-promoting function of apoptotic caspases is dependent on the generation of ROS, which are required for neoplastic behavior. Inhibition of caspases blocks tumor growth and tissue invasion, highlighting the complex and context-dependent role of caspases in cancer development. The findings of this study provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying tumor growth and suggest that targeting the amplification loop involving ROS, macrophages, and JNK signaling may be a potential therapeutic strategy for cancer treatment.
A recent study investigated the relationship between social interaction and subjective time perception, revealing that the perception of social interaction compresses subjective duration in an oxytocin-dependent manner. The study found that motion sequences showing agents acting communicatively were perceived to be significantly shorter in duration compared to those acting noncommunicatively. This effect was negatively correlated with one’s autistic-like tendency, suggesting that socially less proficient individuals are less susceptible to the temporal compression effect. Furthermore, intranasal oxytocin administration restored the temporal compression effect in socially less proficient individuals, whereas the administration of atosiban, a competitive antagonist of oxytocin, diminished the effect in socially proficient individuals. These findings indicate that perceived time is highly idiosyncratic and ingrained with one’s personality trait, rather than being a faithful representation of physical time. The study also suggests that oxytocin is involved in mediating time perception of social interaction, further supporting the role of oxytocin in human social cognition. The results are consistent with previous research associating autistic traits with reduced levels of oxytocin and the amelioration of these traits with intranasal oxytocin administration. The study highlights the complex relationship between social interaction, subjective time perception, and oxytocin, and provides new insights into the neural mechanisms underlying social cognition. The findings have implications for our understanding of the neural basis of social behavior and the potential therapeutic applications of oxytocin in social disorders. The study employed a series of experiments to investigate the relationship between social interaction, subjective time perception, and oxytocin. Experiment 1 found that motion sequences showing agents acting communicatively were perceived to be significantly shorter in duration compared to those acting noncommunicatively. Experiment 2 replicated this finding and highlighted the idiosyncrasy of subjective time for social interactions. Experiment 3 found that the temporal compression effect was negatively correlated with one’s autistic-like tendency. Experiment 4 found that socially less proficient individuals were less susceptible to the temporal compression effect. Experiment 5 probed the role of oxytocin in mediating the temporal compression effect and found that intranasal oxytocin administration restored the effect in socially less proficient individuals. Overall, the study provides new insights into the complex relationship between social interaction, subjective time perception, and oxytocin, and highlights the potential therapeutic applications of oxytocin in social disorders.
The interaction between the SARS-CoV-2 Spike protein receptor binding domain (RBD) and the ACE2 cell surface protein is crucial for viral infection of cells. Mutations in the RBD are present in SARS-CoV-2 variants of concern (VOCs) that have emerged independently worldwide. This study investigated the effects of five common RBD mutations (K417N, K417T, N501Y, E484K, and S477N) and two common ACE2 mutations (S19P and K26R) on the RBD/ACE2 interaction. The study analyzed the effects of individual RBD mutations and combinations found in new SARS-CoV-2 Alpha (B.1.1.7), Beta (B.1.351), and Gamma (P1) variants. The results showed that most of these mutations increased the affinity of the RBD/ACE2 interaction, with the exceptions being mutations K417N/T, which decreased the affinity. The N501Y and S477N mutations were found to enhance transmission primarily by enhancing binding, while the K417N/T mutations facilitate immune escape, and the E484K mutation enhances binding and immune escape. The study also examined the effect on ACE2 binding of combinations of RBD mutations, including combinations present in VOC-202102–02, a subset of the Alpha lineage (N501Y) with the E484K mutation, and the Beta and Gamma variants. The results suggest that carriers of ACE2 variants with mutations S19P and K26R will be at greater risk of infection and/or severe disease. The study provides precise information to assess the extent to which RBD mutations have been selected because they enhance ACE2 binding or facilitate immune evasion.
The study presents a comprehensive genetic interaction map for 21 phenotypic features of Drosophila cells using combinatorial RNA interference and automated single-cell phenotyping. The map comprises 1367 Drosophila genes implicated in various biological processes, including signalling, chromatin biology, cell cycle regulation, and protein turnover control. To generate the map, the researchers co-depleted gene pairs by RNAi in cultured Drosophila S2 cells, high-throughput imaged single-cell phenotypes, and modelled gene–gene interactions. This approach enabled the identification of directional, epistatic interactions between genes, which reflect regulatory relationships between them. The study reveals that the Ras pathway interacts with the SWI/SNF chromatin-remodelling complex, an interaction that is conserved in human cancer cells. The epistatic map also reconstructed the sequence of protein activities in mitosis and provided a detailed circuit diagram of regulatory, temporal, and causal relationships of complexes that function during the M-phase of the cell cycle. Furthermore, the researchers demonstrated an epistatic relationship between Ras/MAPK signalling and SWI/SNF regulators of chromatin remodelling, which was confirmed in vivo. The study presents a powerful approach for reconstructing directional regulatory networks and provides a resource for the interpretation of functional consequences of genetic alterations. The epistatic map highlights an unexpected link between chromatin remodelling and RAS signalling, with genetic interactions between the SWI/SNF complex and the SWI/SNF-associated chromatin remodeller dalao. The findings of this study provide new insights into the regulatory relationships between genes and offer a valuable resource for understanding the functional consequences of genetic alterations in metazoan cells.
Heterostyly, a floral adaptation that promotes outbreeding, is a widespread phenomenon in plants, but its genetic basis and evolutionary origin remain poorly understood. In Primula (primroses), heterostyly is controlled by the S-locus supergene, which determines the reciprocal arrangement of reproductive organs and incompatibility between the two morphs. However, the identities of the component genes have remained unknown. A recent study has identified the CYP734A50 gene, encoding a putative brassinosteroid-degrading enzyme, as the G locus that determines the style-length dimorphism in Primula. CYP734A50 is present only on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, specifically expressed in S-morph styles, and its loss or inactivation leads to long styles. The gene arose by a duplication specific to the Primulaceae lineage and shows an accelerated rate of molecular evolution. The study provides a mechanistic explanation for the Primula style-length dimorphism and sheds light on the evolution of the S-locus as a prime model for a complex plant supergene. The results indicate that CYP734A50 represents the G locus in the Primula heterostyly supergene, whose expression in S-morph styles causes brassinosteroid degradation, limiting cell expansion and style elongation. The proposed duplication and gain of style-specific expression of CYP734A50 in the ancestor of the Primulaceae likely represents the dominant style-shortening mutation. CYP734A50 is a rare example of an identified causal locus in a supergene underlying an adaptive polymorphism, opening the way for understanding the evolutionary origin of the Primula S-locus as an iconic case of a complex supergene. Heterostyly evolved only once in the genus Primula, and the same causal genes should co-segregate with morph phenotypes in all heterostylous Primula species. The study sequenced style transcriptomes of the distantly related Primula species and found that the CYP734A50 presence/absence polymorphism strictly co-segregates with the morph type in all heterostylous species sampled across the Primula phylogeny, and the gene is absent or inactivated in all seven cases of derived long homostyly. The results strongly support the conclusion that CYP734A50 is the G locus of the Primula heterostyly supergene that determines the style-length polymorphism. Heterostyly in Primula is controlled by the S-locus supergene, which likely comprises five tightly linked genes, determining style length, anther height, pollen size, and male and female intra-morph incompatibility.
Kisspeptin (Kiss1) and neurokinin B (NKB) neurocircuits play a crucial role in pubertal development and fertility. Kisspeptin neurons in the hypothalamic arcuate nucleus (Kiss1ARH) co-express Kiss1, NKB, dynorphin, and glutamate, and are thought to provide an episodic, excitatory drive to gonadotropin-releasing hormone 1 (GnRH) neurons. To investigate the cellular basis for synchronized Kiss1ARH neuronal activity, researchers used optogenetics, whole-cell electrophysiology, molecular pharmacology, and single-cell RT-PCR in mice. High-frequency photostimulation of Kiss1ARH neurons was found to evoke the local release of excitatory (NKB) and inhibitory (dynorphin) neuropeptides, which synchronized the firing of Kiss1ARH neurons. This synchronized activity caused robust excitation of GnRH neurons through a synaptic mechanism involving glutamatergic input to preoptic Kiss1 neurons from Kiss1ARH neurons. The researchers propose that Kiss1ARH neurons play a dual role in driving episodic secretion of GnRH through the differential release of peptide and amino acid neurotransmitters to coordinate reproductive function.

The study found that high-frequency photoactivation of Kiss1ARH neurons induced a NKB-mediated slow excitatory postsynaptic potential (EPSP), which was modulated by co-released dynorphin, and caused synchronization of Kiss1ARH neurons. Additionally, activation of Kiss1ARH neurons co-released glutamate that excited the rostral Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons. The combination of synchronization and unified excitation of Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons resulted in robust excitation of GnRH neurons. Kiss1ARH neurons also communicate with the Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons via the fast neurotransmitter glutamate, which stimulates burst-firing of Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons. Activation of these rostral Kiss1 neurons releases kisspeptin to robustly excite GnRH neurons via activation of the GPR54 signaling cascade, thereby stimulating the release of GnRH at the time of the preovulatory surge. Kisspeptin, GPR54, NKB, Tacr3, and GnRH are all required for normal fertility. The study suggests that Kiss1ARH neurons play a dual role in driving pulsatile (episodic) secretion of GnRH through kisspeptin release into the external zone of the median eminence, but also excite Kiss1AVPV/PeN neurons via glutamate release to help generate the preovulatory surge of GnRH through a robust di-synaptic pathway.
Title: Bone morphogenic protein signalling suppresses differentiation of pluripotent cells by maintaining expression of E-Cadherin

    Bone morphogenic protein (BMP) signalling plays a crucial role in maintaining the pluripotency of cells and favouring mesodermal over neural fates during differentiation. However, the mechanisms by which BMP controls differentiation are not well understood. Recent research has shed light on this process, revealing that BMP signalling suppresses differentiation by maintaining the expression of E-Cadherin (Cdh1).

    The study found that the loss of Cdh1 is a limiting requirement for differentiation of pluripotent cells, and that experimental suppression of Cdh1 activity rescues the BMP-imposed block to differentiation. Furthermore, BMP signalling acts prior to and independently of Cdh1 to prime pluripotent cells for mesoderm differentiation, thus reinforcing the block to neural differentiation. This suggests that differentiation depends not only on exposure to appropriate extrinsic cues but also on morphogenetic events that control receptivity to those differentiation cues.

    BMP signalling is known to contribute to the maintenance of pluripotency, but the underlying mechanisms remained unclear. The study proposes that BMP suppresses differentiation by maintaining Cdh1 expression, which interferes with efficient neural and mesodermal differentiation. Higher levels of BMP also impose a posterior identity that primes cells for mesoderm and is incompatible with a neural fate. This finding helps to explain why BMP can simultaneously protect pluripotency and promote mesoderm differentiation.

    The study also identified the BMP target gene Id1, which imposes a proximal posterior identity on undifferentiated cells, priming them towards mesoderm. This suggests that the ability of Id1 to block neural differentiation is not due to inhibition of a neural-inducing transcription factor or direct activation of mesoderm differentiation, but rather to a shift in the positional identity of pluripotent cells that occurs in the absence of overt differentiation.

    In summary, the study reveals that BMP signalling suppresses differentiation of pluripotent cells by maintaining Cdh1 expression, which is a limiting event for the earliest stages of neural differentiation. The findings provide new insights into the mechanisms by which BMP controls differentiation and highlight the importance of morphogenetic events in controlling receptivity to differentiation cues.
A novel Cep120-dependent mechanism has been identified that inhibits centriole maturation in quiescent cells. The study reveals that Cep120, a daughter centriole-associated ciliopathy protein, plays a critical role in maintaining centrosome homeostasis by regulating the levels of pericentriolar material (PCM) components. In quiescent mouse and human cells, depletion of Cep120 leads to an accumulation of PCM components, including pericentrin, Cdk5Rap2, ninein, and Cep170, resulting in increased microtubule-nucleation activity at the centrosome. This, in turn, causes aberrant dynein-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins, dispersal of centriolar satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling.

The study demonstrates that Cep120 inhibits the maturation of the daughter centriole by regulating PCM levels during quiescence. Loss of Cep120 initiates a series of changes in protein localization and abundance that ultimately result in defective ciliary assembly and function. The first step involves changes in the maturation state of the daughter centriole, on which Cep120 is preferentially enriched. These changes disrupt ciliary assembly and signaling, a process that requires CS-dependent delivery of essential factors to the centrosome.

Cep120 regulates the asymmetric distribution of PCM proteins at the centrosome by residing on the outer wall of the centriolar microtubules. Loss of Cep120 during quiescence is detrimental to ciliary assembly and function, likely due to aberrant CS-dependent trafficking of proteins to the centrosome. The study suggests that loss of Cep120 disrupts the localization of CS, due to changes in PCM abundance, microtubule organization, and dynein-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins.

The dispersal of centriolar satellites upon Cep120 loss disrupts the formation and/or function of cilia during quiescence. The study establishes the important and exquisitely sensitive control of centrosome PCM homeostasis and its relationship to primary cilium assembly and signaling. The findings uncover a potentially new type of molecular defect that may underlie the pathogenesis of ciliopathies such as Jeune Asphyxiating Thoracic Dystrophy and Joubert syndrome.
In the developing retina, neuron-glia interactions play a crucial role in the maturation of neural circuits, but the pathways that mediate their communication are not well understood. A recent study investigated neuron-glia signaling in the developing retina and found that retinal waves induce calcium transients in Müller glial cells ( MCs ). The study used whole-cell recordings of retinal ganglion cells and two-photon imaging of calcium indicators or glutamate sensors expressed specifically in MCs to examine the spatio-temporal characteristics and molecular pathways of neuron-glia interactions. The results showed that retinal waves were correlated with calcium transients in a large proportion of MCs, indicating that spontaneous activity encompasses both neuronal and glial networks at this age. During cholinergic retinal waves, MC calcium transients were blocked by muscarinic acetylcholine receptor antagonists, whereas during glutamatergic waves, MC calcium transients were inhibited by ionotropic glutamate receptor antagonists. This suggests that the responsiveness of MCs changes to match the neurotransmitter used to support retinal waves. Using an optical glutamate sensor, the study found that the decline in MC calcium transients is caused by a reduction in the amount of glutamate reaching MCs. The results indicate that neurons and MCs exhibit correlated activity during a critical period of retinal maturation that is enabled by neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses. The study also found that MCs express multiple neurotransmitter receptor types throughout this period, allowing neuronal control of MC activity to be sustained as neuronal synchronization shifts from being dependent on cholinergic to glutamatergic signaling. The proportion of MCs that responded to waves with calcium transients decreased significantly from postnatal day 9 to 11, despite the presence of retinal waves. These findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of neuron-glia interactions in the developing retina and highlight the importance of neurotransmitter spillover in enabling correlated activity between neurons and MCs.
The postmortem interval (PMI), the time elapsed since death, is a critical factor in death investigations. However, current methods for estimating PMI, such as forensic entomology, are prone to errors and biases. A novel approach to estimating PMI has been proposed using microbial communities, which may provide a more accurate and reliable method. In a mouse model system, researchers have demonstrated that postmortem microbial community changes are dramatic, measurable, and repeatable, allowing for the estimation of PMI within approximately 3 days over a 48-day period. This study provides a detailed understanding of bacterial and microbial eukaryotic ecology within a decomposing corpse system and suggests that microbial community data can be developed into a forensic tool for estimating PMI.

    The researchers conducted a laboratory experiment to characterize temporal changes in microbial communities associated with mouse corpses as they decomposed on soil under controlled conditions for 48 days. Bacteria, archaea, and microbial eukaryotes were characterized using a combination of culture-independent, high-throughput DNA sequencing approaches for each sample. The data collected allowed the researchers to assess hypotheses about the role of microbes in corpse decomposition and rigorously test whether changes in microbial communities are predictable over the timeline of decomposition.

    The study expands upon previous research by Pechal et al. (2013), which demonstrated that skin and mouth bacterial communities followed a consistent trajectory of change during decomposition of three swine corpses over 5 days. In contrast, this study characterized microbial community change in three external sites and one invasively sampled internal site in 40 destructively sampled mouse corpses over 48 days. The results provide a proof-of-principle demonstration that microbial community ecology of decomposing corpses has potential to be developed into a complementary forensics tool for estimation of PMI. The study's findings have implications for understanding the underlying microbial ecology of corpse decomposition and the feasibility of using microbes as evidence in forensic investigations.
Despite the vast diversity of body forms in nature, bilaterian animals share common sets of developmental genes that display conserved expression patterns in the embryo. The Hox genes, which define different identities along the anterior–posterior axis, are one such set of genes. Hox proteins exert their function by interacting with TALE transcription factors. However, the presence of Hox and TALE members in some but not all non-bilaterian phyla raises the question of how Hox–TALE interactions evolved to provide positional information. To address this question, researchers used proteins from unicellular and multicellular lineages to show that Hox–TALE networks emerged from an ancestral generic motif present in Hox and other related protein families. These networks experienced additional and extensive molecular innovations that were likely crucial for differentiating Hox functions along body plans. The results highlight how homeobox gene families evolved during eukaryote evolution to eventually constitute a major patterning system in Eumetazoans. The study proposes that Hox–TALE networks constituted an ancestral regulatory module that was later exploited for patterning functions in Bilateria. This network was effective as soon as different interaction modes could exist with duplicated Hox family members, allowing for diversifying patterning functions along the body axis. The Hox genes are expressed along the anteroposterior axis of all bilaterian animals, providing positional information during embryogenesis. Given their important patterning roles, Hox genes are thought to have strongly contributed to morphological diversification of bilaterian organisms during evolution. The study suggests that the molecular cues underlying the Hox–TALE patterning system in Bilateria were established stepwise during eukaryote evolution. The Hox gene family acquired its crucial axial patterning functions during metazoan evolution, with Cnidaria being the only non-bilaterian phylum to possess a bona fide Hox repertoire. The presence of PBC and Meis proteins in non-bilaterian phyla suggests that these proteins played a key role in the evolution of Hox patterning functions. Overall, the study provides insights into the origin of the Hox–TALE patterning system and its evolution during eukaryote evolution.
The study used super-resolution microscopy to investigate the dynamics of AMPA receptors (AMPARs) in post-synaptic densities (PSDs) and extrasynaptic regions of rat neurons. The researchers employed different-sized fluorescent probes, including organic dyes, small quantum dots (sQDs), and large quantum dots (bQDs), to label AMPARs and track their movement. The results showed that the majority of AMPARs labeled with sQDs or organic dyes were diffusing in confined nanodomains within PSDs, which were stable for 15 minutes or longer. In contrast, bQDs labeled AMPARs were mostly found in extrasynaptic regions, consistent with previous observations. The study suggests that AMPARs rapidly enter stable nanodomains in PSDs and do not accumulate in extrasynaptic membranes. The findings contradict the hypothesis that AMPAR entry is limited by the occupancy of open PSD "slots." The researchers used sQDs to study the dynamics of AMPAR nanodomains in synapses for extended periods, up to 60 minutes, and found that the localization of AMPARs is predominantly at synaptic regions. The study also observed that there is typically one nanodomain per synapse, although multiple nanodomains can exist. The use of bQDs was found to be problematic due to their large size, which may prevent AMPARs from entering the synaptic cleft. In contrast, sQDs and organic dyes labeled AMPARs were found to be overwhelmingly in the synapse, with 84-97% of AMPARs immobilized within PSDs and only 5-16% freely diffusing in the dendrite membrane. The study's findings provide new insights into the dynamics of AMPARs in PSDs and extrasynaptic regions, shedding light on the mechanisms of synaptic plasticity and neurotransmission.
The study investigated the early response of central neurons to acute hypoxia, specifically the increase in voltage-gated sodium current (INa). The researchers found that hypoxia activates the Small Ubiquitin-like Modifier (SUMO) pathway in rat cerebellar granule neurons (CGN) and that SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels increases INa. The time-course for SUMOylation of single NaV1.2 channels at the cell surface and changes in INa coincide, and both are prevented by mutation of NaV1.2-Lys38 or application of a deSUMOylating enzyme. Within 40 seconds, hypoxia-induced linkage of SUMO1 to the channels is complete, shifting the voltage-dependence of channel activation so that depolarizing steps evoke larger sodium currents. The study suggests that the SUMO pathway and NaV1.2 are potential targets for neuroprotective interventions, given the recognized role of INa in hypoxic brain damage. The results show that hypoxia-mediated SUMOylation of NaV1.2 is observed in the presence of TTX, indicating that Na+ flux is not a prerequisite for channel SUMOylation. Consistent with tonic control of INa by the SUMO pathway, SUMO1 and SENP1 increased and decreased the current, respectively, under normoxic conditions. The response of INa to hypoxia was ablated by µ-Conotoxin-TIIIA (CnTX), a potent blocker of NaV1.2 channels. The study directly showed that hypoxia increases the interaction of native SUMO1 and NaV1.2 channels. The findings suggest that NaV1.2 channels and SUMO pathway enzymes may be valuable therapeutic targets for reducing damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons. The study demonstrates that SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels at the plasma membrane underlies the immediate increase in INa in response to acute hypoxia in cultured neurons and CHO cells. The results are consistent with previous studies showing that SUMOylation increases excitability by suppressing the activity of background K+ channels and voltage-gated K+ channels.
The article presents a mechanistic model for the thermal response of Ross River virus (RRV), a mosquito-borne pathogen found in Australia, the Pacific Islands, and potentially at risk of emerging worldwide. The model predicts that RRV transmission peaks at moderate temperatures (26.4°C) and declines to zero at thermal limits (17.0 and 31.5°C). This non-linear relationship between temperature and transmission is critical for understanding the dynamics of the disease and predicting responses to climate change. The model accurately predicts that RRV transmission is year-round endemic in the tropics but seasonal in temperate areas, resulting in a nationwide seasonal peak in human cases. Climate warming is likely to increase transmission in temperate areas, where most Australians live, but decrease transmission in tropical areas where mean temperatures are already near the thermal optimum.

The study highlights the importance of non-linear models for inferring the role of temperature in disease dynamics and predicting responses to climate change.
