Thermal responses vary substantially among diseases and vector species, yet mechanistic models based on empirical, unimodal thermal responses are lacking for many diseases and vectors. The authors parameterized a temperature-dependent model for RRV transmission using data from two important vector species (Cx. annulirostris and Ae. vigilax). The results support a general pattern of intermediate thermal optima for transmission, where the well-resolved optimal temperature is driven by adult mosquito lifespan, but upper and lower thermal limits are more uncertain and may be determined by unique traits for different vector-pathogen systems.

The study found that the strongest statistical signal of temperature on disease is expected in temperate regions where mean temperature varies along the rapidly rising portion of the R0 curve (20-25°C). Additionally, upper thermal limits of mosquito-borne disease transmission are primarily determined by vector life history traits with symmetrical thermal performance curves, such as fecundity and survival at various life stages, rather than rate-based traits with asymmetrical thermal performance curves, such as biting rate or pathogen development rate.
Title: Beta band oscillations in motor cortex reflect neural population signals that delay movement onset

    Motor cortical beta oscillations have been extensively studied for decades, yet their behavioral correlates remain poorly understood. Recent studies have linked beta oscillations to changes in underlying neural activity, but the specific behavioral manifestations of these changes have remained elusive. To investigate how changes in population neural activity, beta oscillations, and behavior are linked, researchers recorded multi-scale neural activity from motor cortex while three macaques performed a novel neurofeedback task. The subjects volitionally brought their beta oscillatory power to an instructed state and subsequently executed an arm reach.

    The results showed that reaches preceded by a reduction in beta power exhibited significantly faster movement onset times than reaches preceded by an increase in beta power. Furthermore, population neural activity was found to shift farther from a movement onset state during beta oscillations that were neurofeedback-induced or naturally occurring during reaching tasks. This finding establishes a population neural basis for slowed movement onset following periods of beta oscillatory activity.

    Notably, this shift in neural population was also seen in naturally occurring beta oscillations during reaching tasks, suggesting that beta oscillations reflect a common signature of spiking patterns even in different task contexts. This study ties together existing works on behavioral correlates of beta oscillations with hypotheses of how the motor cortex encodes movement onset through the lens of population-level neural activity.

    The results of this study support the hypothesis that beta oscillations are linked to neural patterns that slow the onset of new movements. The behavioral effect from neurofeedback-induced beta oscillations matches well with hypotheses claiming a movement-slowing role of beta oscillations during natural movements. However, individual unit responses during beta activity were found to be different across tasks.

    Simultaneously recorded units were analyzed and shown to exhibit different activity during neurofeedback-induced beta oscillations compared with natural beta oscillations occurring during typical reaching tasks. Despite differences at an individual unit level, population-level analyses show that beta oscillations promote a consistent movement-slowing state during both the CO and NR tasks, matching what is observed behaviorally.
TRAF2, a component of TNF superfamily signalling complexes, plays a crucial role in the regulation and homeostasis of immune cells. However, the function of TRAF2 in adult tissues is not well-understood due to the early lethality of TRAF2-deficient mice. This study investigated the role of TRAF2 in keratinocytes, the primary cell type of the epidermis, and its relationship with sphingosine kinase 1 ( Sphk1 ). Keratinocyte-specific deletion of Traf2 resulted in disrupted TNF-mediated NF-κB and MAP kinase signalling, leading to epidermal hyperplasia and psoriatic skin inflammation. This inflammation was driven by TNF, cell death, non-canonical NF-κB, and the adaptive immune system, and may represent a clinically relevant model of psoriasis. In contrast, Sphk1 deficiency did not disrupt TNF signalling in keratinocytes, indicating that TRAF2 has essential tissue-specific functions that do not overlap with those of Sphk1. The absence of TRAF2 in keratinocytes caused a delay in TNF-induced activation of NF-κB and MAPK, and increased sensitivity to killing by TNF. Furthermore, TRAF2-deficient keratinocytes produced inflammatory cytokines via the non-canonical NF-κB pathway in the absence of TNF, leading to a psoriasis-like phenotype with slower onset. The study highlights the importance of TRAF2 in protecting keratinocytes from cell death and down-regulating inflammatory responses, and suggests that intrinsic defects in keratinocytes can initiate psoriasis-like skin inflammation. Concomitant deletion of Tnf in mice with Traf2-deficient keratinocytes broke the vicious cycle of inflammation and prevented apoptosis of keratinocytes and the early onset of the psoriasis-like phenotype. These findings provide new insights into the role of TRAF2 in keratinocytes and its relationship with Sphk1, and may have implications for the understanding and treatment of psoriasis. TRAF2-deficient keratinocytes were also sensitive to TNF-induced death, associated with a rapid reduction in cFLIPL levels and caspase-8 cleavage. Primary keratinocytes isolated from wild-type, Traf2EKO, and Sphk1-/- mice were tested, and TNF signalling was delayed and weakened in Traf2-deficient keratinocytes, but not Sphk1-deficient keratinocytes.
The process of synaptic vesicle (SV) recycling involves the rapid endocytosis of SV membranes after exocytosis to form large endocytic vacuoles, known as bulk endosomes. These vacuoles then convert into new SVs to replenish the pool of fusion-competent vesicles. Clathrin-mediated endocytosis (CME) and dynamin 1 and 3 (Dyn1 and Dyn3) have been implicated in this process. However, a recent study investigated the role of Dyn1 and Dyn3 in bulk endocytosis and the conversion of bulk endosomes into SVs in the absence of CME.

Using synapses of Dyn1 and Dyn3 double knockout (DKO) neurons, the study found that bulk endocytosis was not defective, but rather enhanced, in the absence of Dyn1 and Dyn3. Furthermore, the conversion of bulk endosomes into SVs was not accompanied by the accumulation of clathrin-coated buds on their surface and proceeded even after further clathrin knockdown. This suggests that the process of SV reformation from bulk endosomes is independent of clathrin and Dyn1/3.

The study used soluble horseradish peroxidase (HRP) labeling to analyze bulk endocytosis and the conversion of bulk endosomes into SVs in wild-type (WT) and Dyn mutant synapses. The results showed that the absence of Dyn1 and Dyn3 delayed, but did not abolish, SV reformation from internal membranes. Ultrastructural analysis of the time course of SV recovery from bulk endosomes in the presence or near depletion of clathrin led to similar conclusions.

These findings support the existence of a pathway for SV reformation that bypasses the requirement for clathrin and Dyn1/3 and operates during intense synaptic activity. This pathway is distinct from the clathrin-mediated endocytosis pathway and highlights the complexity of SV recycling mechanisms. The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying synaptic plasticity and the regulation of SV recycling.
The study aimed to identify and validate circulating biomarkers of glucocorticoid (GC) action in humans. A randomized, crossover, single-blind trial was conducted in 10 subjects with primary adrenal insufficiency, who underwent physiological GC exposure and withdrawal. The transcriptome in peripheral blood mononuclear cells and adipose tissue, plasma microRNAomic, and serum metabolomics were compared between interventions using integrated multi-omic analysis. A transcriptomic profile derived from two tissues and a multi-omic cluster were identified as predictive of GC exposure. A microRNA (miR-122-5p) was correlated with genes and metabolites regulated by GC exposure (p=0.009) and replicated in independent studies with varying GC exposure (0.01≤p≤0.05). This study provides a basis for the discovery of biomarkers to measure the effects of GCs, allowing for individualized and optimized GC therapy, and shedding light on disease etiology related to unphysiological GC exposure, such as cardiovascular disease and obesity. The study used a multi-omic analysis strategy combining data from gene expression in circulation and adipose tissue, integrated with circulating microRNAs and metabolites, to identify putative biomarkers. The strongest putative biomarkers were then replicated in independent study groups with different GC exposure. A transcriptomic profile under similar GC regulation in both peripheral blood mononuclear cells and adipose tissue transcriptomes was identified and validated by comparison to previously published data from cellular assays. A circulating microRNA, miR-122-5p, was correlated with the circulating transcriptome and metabolome findings, suggesting a functional role in GC action. Two subjects did not show this miR-122-5p response, one man and one woman who experienced mild mental fatigue during GC withdrawal. The study was funded by several organizations, including the Swedish Research Council, the Swedish federal government, and the Wellcome Trust.
The dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids have been examined in a study using live imaging of the mTORC1 component RAPTOR and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of di-leucine methyl ester. The results show that the translocation of mTORC1 to lysosomes is a transient event, occurring within 2 minutes of amino acid addition and peaking within 5 minutes. This translocation is temporally coupled with the appearance of fluorescent leucine in lysosomes and is sustained compared to amino acid stimulation. The study also found that Sestrin2 and the vacuolar ATPase are negative and positive regulators of mTORC1 activity, respectively. Furthermore, phosphorylation of canonical mTORC1 targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation, suggesting a dynamic and transient passage of mTORC1 from the lysosomal surface before targeting its substrates elsewhere. The study used HAP-1 cells and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of leucine to image RAPTOR translocation in response to this compound. The results suggest that the lysosome is a crucial locus for mTOR activation, but the dynamics of this process and how mTORC1 activity is connected to lysosomal residence may be more complicated than current models. The study provides a dynamic view of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids, supporting the idea that amino acids ultimately activate mTORC1 in the cell interior, and that more than one type of sensing (and amino acid) is involved.
The modification of antigens with specific glycan structures can significantly alter their intracellular routing in dendritic cells, thereby influencing the type and strength of immune responses. In a recent study, researchers demonstrated that the addition of the LewisX (LeX) glycan to the model antigen Ovalbumin (OVA) redirected it towards the C-type lectin receptor MGL1. This re-routing resulted in enhanced cross-presentation of OVA to CD8+ T cells and favored the differentiation of naive CD4+ T cells towards Th1 cells, which produce interferon-gamma (IFN-γ) and tumor necrosis factor-alpha (TNF-α). The LeX-modified OVA also reduced the required antigen dose for cross-presentation by 100-fold and eliminated the need for Toll-like receptor (TLR) signaling.

    The study found that MGL1-dependent cross-presentation of OVA-LeX occurred independently of TAP-transporters and Cathepsin-S, and was still observed after prolonged intracellular storage of the antigen in Rab11+LAMP1+ compartments. Furthermore, the use of MGL1-deficient mice revealed that MGL1 is the primary lectin involved in boosting the generation of antigen-specific effector T cells. Immunization of MGL1-deficient mice with LeX-conjugated OVA did not result in enhanced frequencies of OVA-specific IFN-γ and TNF-α-double-producing T cells.

    The findings of this study suggest that controlled neo-glycosylation of antigens can influence intracellular routing, antigen presentation, and the type and strength of immune responses. The conjugation of LeX glycans to OVA enhanced CD8+ T cell priming and Th1 skewing of CD4+ T cells, indicating that this approach may be useful for optimizing current vaccination strategies. Overall, the study highlights the importance of glycan structures in modulating immune responses and provides new insights into the mechanisms of antigen presentation and T cell priming.
In the bacterium Burkholderia thailandensis, quorum sensing (QS) plays a crucial role in regulating hundreds of genes, including those involved in cell-cell contact-dependent type VI secretion (T6S) toxin-immunity systems. Research has shown that QS activates T6S in B. thailandensis, which is essential for constraining the proliferation of QS mutants in colony cocultures. A QS mutant, BtaR1, was found to outcompete its parent in broth cocultures due to the absence of cell contact and a metabolic cost associated with QS gene activation. However, when grown on agar surfaces, the parent strain had a fitness advantage over the BtaR1 mutant, which was dependent on an intact T6SS-1 apparatus. This suggests that QS activates B. thailandensis T6SS-1 growth inhibition, serving as a mechanism to police and constrain QS mutants.

    The study demonstrated that QS control of T6SS-1-mediated toxicity and immunity genes allows QS-competent members of B. thailandensis colonies to police against BtaR1-QS mutants. During planktonic growth in broth, where T6SS-1 does not function due to limited cell contact, BtaR1-mutants had a substantial fitness advantage over wild-type cells, likely due to the cost associated with BtaR1-dependent activities. In contrast, on agar surfaces, wild-type populations can mitigate the cost of BtaR1-BtaI1 gene regulation by hindering the growth of QS mutants via regulation of cell-contact-dependent toxin and immunity proteins. This is a form of microbial policing, where QS-proficient bacteria are outcompeted in populations of signal-blind non-cooperators in the absence of the T6S cell-contact growth inhibition system.

    The study highlights a novel mechanism for limiting QS mutant growth in bacterial groups, which is distinct from previously reported mechanisms in Myxococcus xanthus and Pseudomonas aeruginosa. B. thailandensis T6S-mediated killing is active against Proteobacteria species other than B. thailandensis and is thought to be important for interspecies competition. The results demonstrate that the parent strain, with an intact QS system, expresses quorum-activated T6SS-1 toxin and immunity proteins, whereas the BtaR1-mutant does not fully induce its T6SS-1 effectors and immunity factors.
The study investigated the role of glutamatergic drive along the septo-temporal axis of the hippocampus in boosting prelimbic oscillations in neonatal mice. Long-range coupling within prefrontal-hippocampal networks is crucial for cognitive performance and emerges early in life. Discontinuous hippocampal theta bursts have been proposed to drive the generation of neonatal prefrontal oscillations, but the cellular substrate of these early interactions remains unresolved. To address this, the study selectively targeted optogenetic manipulation of glutamatergic projection neurons in the CA1 area of either dorsal or intermediate/ventral hippocampus at neonatal age. The results show that despite stronger theta and ripples power in the dorsal hippocampus, the prefrontal cortex is mainly coupled with the intermediate/ventral hippocampus by phase-locking of neuronal firing via dense direct axonal projections. Theta band-confined activation by light of pyramidal neurons in the intermediate/ventral but not dorsal CA1 that were transfected with high-efficiency channelrhodopsin boosts prefrontal oscillations. These findings provide causal evidence that theta frequency-specific activation of pyramidal neurons in the CA1 area of intermediate and ventral hippocampus, but not dorsal hippocampus, elicits broad band oscillations in the prefrontal cortex of neonatal mice via dense axonal projections. The tight coupling by synchrony and the directed information flow from hippocampal CA1 area to prefrontal cortex via direct axonal projections suggest that the hippocampus acts as a drive for prelimbic activation already at neonatal age. The differences identified between dorsal and intermediate/ventral hippocampus-prelimbic communication argue for prominent augmentation of driving force along the septo-temporal hippocampal axis. The study provides new perspectives for assessing the adult behavioral readout of long-range communication in the developing brain and highlights the need to investigate how the hippocampal theta drive shapes the maturation of prefrontal-hippocampal networks.
RNA polymerase II (RNAPII) undergoes dynamic post-translational modifications to coordinate the co-transcriptional recruitment of enzymatic complexes that regulate chromatin states and processing of nascent RNA. Phosphorylation of serine residues at the C-terminal domain (CTD) of RNAPII has been extensively studied, with serine-5 and serine-7 phosphorylation marking transcription initiation and serine-2 phosphorylation coinciding with productive elongation. In vertebrates, the CTD has eight non-canonical substitutions of serine-7 into lysine-7 (K7), which can be acetylated (K7ac). This study describes the mono- and di-methylation of CTD lysine-7 residues (K7me1 and K7me2), which are observed during the earliest stages of transcription and precede or accompany serine-5 and serine-7 phosphorylation. In contrast, K7ac is associated with RNAPII elongation, serine-2 phosphorylation, and mRNA expression. The study identifies an unexpected balance between RNAPII K7 methylation and acetylation at gene promoters, which fine-tunes gene expression levels. The results suggest that the extent of productive transcription at active genes with similar RNAPII recruitment to their promoters is modulated at the level of CTD-K7 modifications, with methylation being associated with less productive transcription, and K7 acetylation being most enriched at the highest expressed genes. The study also shows that mutation of CTD-K7 residues is compatible with viability of mouse fibroblasts, and that global serine phosphorylation and RNAPII localization are independent of the presence or absence of K7 residues. Furthermore, the study demonstrates that mono- and di-methylation of CTD-K7 residues is detected primarily in the hypophosphorylated forms of RPB1, suggesting that CTD methylation is associated with early stages of the transcription cycle.
The regulation of skeletal muscle satellite cell (SC) quiescence is crucial for maintaining muscle homeostasis and facilitating muscle repair following injury. A recent study has identified the mRNA decay factor Tristetraprolin (TTP) as a key regulator of SC quiescence. TTP promotes the degradation of MyoD mRNA, a transcriptional regulator of myogenic commitment, by binding to its 3' untranslated region. Upon muscle injury, the p38α/β MAPK signaling pathway is activated, leading to the phosphorylation and inactivation of TTP. This results in the stabilization of MyoD mRNA, allowing SCs to exit quiescence and proliferate to repair damaged muscle tissue.

The study found that TTP is present in quiescent SCs and is rapidly inhibited by p38α/β MAPK signaling upon SC activation. TTP's role in maintaining SC quiescence is further supported by its ability to reduce the half-life of MyoD mRNA. Satellite cell-specific knockdown of TTP resulted in precocious activation of SCs, leading to inappropriate MyoD expression and aberrant cell cycle entry. This suggests that TTP actively suppresses pro-myogenic mRNA targets, including MyoD, to maintain SC quiescence.

The p38α/β MAPK-dependent phosphorylation and inactivation of TTP upon SC activation is consistent with the hypothesis that TTP plays a role in maintaining SC quiescence. Furthermore, the study proposes that TTP may be involved in down-regulating MyoD mRNA and other TTP-target transcripts during the initial formation of a quiescent SC population or in the reacquisition of quiescence following an injury. The regulation of mRNAs by TTP appears to function as one of several critical post-transcriptional regulatory mechanisms controlling SC homeostasis.

The study highlights the importance of post-transcriptional regulation of RNA in skeletal muscle homeostasis, with transcripts encoding proteins that directly regulate mRNAs, including mRNA binding proteins involved in skeletal muscle diseases, being highly represented. The findings suggest that TTP plays a crucial role in maintaining SC quiescence and that its dysregulation may contribute to muscle diseases. Further research is needed to fully understand the mechanisms underlying TTP's role in SC quiescence and its potential as a therapeutic target for muscle diseases.
In a study on infant mice lacking the interleukin-10 receptor (IL10R), researchers investigated the role of IL10R in regulating lamina propria macrophage function during infant development and its impact on the prevention of colitis. The study found that while IL10R-deficient mice did not exhibit signs of colitis during the first two weeks of life, intestinal inflammation and macrophage dysfunction began to develop during the third week of life, coinciding with weaning and changes in the intestinal microbiota. However, IL10R did not directly regulate the microbial ecology during infant development. Instead, macrophage depletion with clodronate inhibited the development of colitis, while the absence of IL10R specifically on macrophages sensitized infant mice to the development of colitis. These results indicate that IL10R-mediated regulation of macrophage function during the early postnatal period is crucial for preventing the development of murine colitis. The study also suggests that the requirement for macrophage-intrinsic IL10R signaling develops during a critical developmental transition, which has important implications for understanding factors that initiate colitis in infants with genetic deficiencies in IL10R signaling. The findings of this study have significant implications for understanding the etiopathogenesis of infantile-onset colitis and could lead to advances in the prevention and treatment of these diseases. The study highlights the importance of IL10R-mediated regulation of macrophage function in maintaining intestinal mucosal homeostasis and preventing intestinal inflammation in infancy.
The Tudor domain protein SIMR-1 plays a crucial role in the regulation of transposons and germline genes in Caenorhabditis elegans by promoting the production of small interfering RNAs (siRNAs) at piRNA-targeted mRNAs. The piRNA pathway is a key mechanism for silencing transposons and other germline genes, and the mutator complex is responsible for synthesizing high levels of siRNAs through the activity of an RNA-dependent RNA polymerase. However, the steps between mRNA recognition by the piRNA pathway and siRNA amplification by the mutator complex were previously unknown. This study identifies SIMR-1 as a protein that acts downstream of piRNA production and upstream of mutator complex-dependent siRNA biogenesis. SIMR-1 localizes to distinct subcellular foci adjacent to P granules and Mutator foci, which are the sites of piRNA-dependent mRNA recognition and mutator complex-dependent siRNA amplification, respectively. These findings suggest a role for multiple perinuclear condensates in organizing the piRNA pathway and promoting mRNA regulation by the mutator complex. The study demonstrates that SIMR-1 is required at a step between the piRNA pathway and the mutator complex, and its localization to cytoplasmic foci near P granules, Z granules, and Mutator foci implicates a series of distinct perinuclear condensates in the regulation of mRNAs by the piRNA pathway and mutator complex. This work identifies SIMR-1 as a factor that acts downstream of PRG-1 to mediate the production of secondary siRNAs by the mutator complex, and suggests a role for multiple perinuclear condensates to promote mRNA regulation by the piRNA pathway and mutator complex. The piRNA silencing pathway in C. elegans involves a small RNA amplification pathway dependent on the mutator complex, which synthesizes secondary downstream siRNAs from piRNA-targeted mRNAs to trigger robust and heritable silencing. Several proteins have been identified in C. elegans that are required for piRNA transcription, trimming, and modification, including GLH-1, GLH-2, GLH-3, GLH-4, DDX-19, LAF-1, MUT-14, and VBH-1, which localize at or near P granules. One of these proteins could potentially serve a function similar to RDE-12, in the shuttling of piRNA-targeted mRNAs into SIMR-1 foci and ultimately to Mutator foci.
In vivo recordings of Purkinje neurons in unanesthetized larval zebrafish (Danio rerio) have unequivocally demonstrated the existence of bistability in these neurons. Bistability refers to the ability of Purkinje neurons to switch between two distinct states: tonic firing and bursting. Tonic firing occurs at depolarized membrane potentials, while bursting occurs at hyperpolarized membrane potentials. This bistability was observed in Purkinje neurons from 4 days post-fertilization (dpf) to 19 dpf, suggesting that it is a conserved feature of Purkinje neurons from fish to mammals.

The bistability in Purkinje neurons was found to be dependent on AMPA receptor (AMPAR)-mediated inputs from climbing fibers (CFs). Activation of AMPARs by CFs was sufficient to trigger bursts, while GABAAR or NMDAR were not required for bursting. Furthermore, the initiation of motor neuron spiking was correlated with increased incidence of CF excitatory postsynaptic potentials (EPSPs) and membrane depolarization.

Intracellular recordings from Purkinje neurons revealed two types of glutamatergic excitation: strong excitation from CFs and weak excitation from parallel fibers (PFs), both mediated by AMPA receptors. The bistability observed in Purkinje neurons of zebrafish larvae is consistent with previous reports of bistability in vitro in amniotes and in vivo under anesthesia in rodents.

The findings suggest that bistability is a conserved feature of Purkinje neurons across species, from fish to mammals. The ability of Purkinje neurons to switch between tonic firing and bursting modes may play a crucial role in motor learning and cerebellar function. The study provides new insights into the neural mechanisms underlying cerebellar function and motor control, and highlights the importance of AMPAR-mediated CF inputs in regulating Purkinje neuron activity.
The actin cytoskeleton plays a crucial role in the process of clathrin-mediated endocytosis by generating force to internalize endocytic pits against membrane tension. An experimentally constrained multiscale model was developed to investigate the principles of self-organization and load adaptation by the actin cytoskeleton during this process. The model revealed that a minimal branched actin network is sufficient to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension. Around 200 activated Arp2/3 complexes are required for robust internalization, and a newly developed molecule-counting method determined that approximately 200 Arp2/3 complexes assemble at sites of clathrin-mediated endocytosis in human cells. Simulations predicted that actin self-organizes into a radial branched array with growing ends oriented toward the base of the pit. Long actin filaments bend between attachment sites in the coat and the base of the pit, storing elastic energy that contributes to endocytic internalization. Cryo-electron tomography confirmed the presence of bent filaments. Elevated membrane tension directs more growing filaments toward the base of the pit, increasing actin nucleation and bending for increased force production. This spatially constrained actin filament assembly utilizes an adaptive mechanism enabling endocytosis under varying physical constraints. The model also showed that actin network self-organization arising from the distribution of Hip1R around the pit and filament bending allows the endocytic actin network to change its organization and force-producing capacity under elevated loads. The results demonstrate that a minimal actin network composed of actin, the Arp2/3 complex, and capping protein, with linker attachments in the clathrin coat and rates constrained by cellular and biochemical measurements, is able to generate sufficient force to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension. The study provides new insights into the principles of self-organization and load adaptation by the actin cytoskeleton during clathrin-mediated endocytosis.
Macropinocytosis is a cellular process by which cells internalize extracellular liquid into large vesicles called macropinosomes. This process relies on the formation of a ring of protrusive actin beneath the plasma membrane, which develops into the macropinocytic cup. Researchers have investigated the molecular mechanisms underlying macropinocytic cup formation in Dictyostelium cells using lattice light sheet microscopy. They found that macropinocytic cups are organized around intense patches of phosphatidylinositol 3,4,5-trisphosphate ( PIP3 ), active Ras, and active Rac. These signalling patches are invariably associated with a ring of active SCAR/WAVE at their periphery. The formation of these patches does not depend on the enclosing F-actin ring, and they become enlarged when the RasGAP NF1 is mutated, indicating that Ras plays an instructive role in their formation. The researchers propose that cup-shaped plasma membrane structures form from self-organizing patches of active Ras/PIP3, which recruit a ring of actin nucleators to their periphery. Macropinosomes develop from cup-shaped projections of the plasma membrane, whose walls are driven outwards by actin polymerization. The central patch of activated Ras and PIP3 is associated with a ring of active SCAR at its edge, which drives a hollow ring of F-actin to extend the walls of the macropinocytic cup. The F-actin in de novo macropinosome cups is closely associated with signalling molecules, including active Ras. Circular ruffles can persist on the cell surface for prolonged periods before either closing successfully or regressing back into the cell body. The closure of the cup can be abrupt and often involves the inward collapse of the rim. The researchers suggest that Ras activity stimulates macropinocytosis in both mammalian and Dictyostelium cells, and macropinocytic cups are associated with an intense domain of PIP3, which is essential for their function. The study provides new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying macropinocytic cup formation and suggests a general hypothesis for the formation of cups from the plasma membrane.
The cohesin ring plays a crucial role in holding newly replicated sister chromatids together until their separation at anaphase in eukaryotic cell division. The initiation of sister chromatid cohesion depends on the Scc2NIPBL/Scc4Mau2 ( Scc2/4 ) complex, which loads cohesin onto DNA and determines its localization across the genome. Proper cohesin loading is essential for cell division, and defects in this process can lead to chromosome missegregation and aberrant transcriptional regulation, resulting in severe developmental defects in multicellular organisms. The Scc4 protein is a TPR array that envelops an extended Scc2 peptide, and a conserved patch on the surface of Scc4 is required to recruit Scc2/4 to centromeres and build pericentromeric cohesion. The structure of yeast Scc4 in complex with an N-terminal fragment of Scc2 has been determined, revealing the role of Scc4 in determining the localization of cohesin loading and establishing a molecular basis for Scc2/4 recruitment to centromeres. Using budding yeast, it has been demonstrated that a conserved patch on the surface of Scc4 is necessary to recruit Scc2/4 to centromeres and build pericentromeric cohesion. This finding shows that Scc4 targets cohesin loading to a specific genomic locus and that this function is separable from its essential role in establishing sister chromatid cohesion across the genome. The results also suggest that the Scc4-dependent cohesin targeting pathway is a general feature of the control of cohesin loading in eukaryotes, as the conserved patch on the surface of Scc4 is present in diverse eukaryotes. Perturbation of the conserved patch eliminates centromeric localization of Scc2 in mitotic cells and reduces association of the cohesin subunit, Scc1, with the centromere and pericentromere, but not with chromosome arms. This indicates that the Scc4-dependent cohesin targeting pathway is essential for proper cohesin loading and chromosome segregation.
This study investigated the effects of being born late in the active season on growth, fattening, torpor use, winter survival, and fecundity in juvenile garden dormice (Eliomys quercinus). The results showed that late-born (LB) juveniles grew and gained mass twice as fast as early-born (EB) individuals. However, LB juveniles had lower body fat content prior to hibernation compared to EB individuals. Despite this, LB juveniles showed similar patterns of hibernation, but had a higher proportion of breeders during the following year than EB dormice.

The study found that torpor use was low during intensive growth, but increased during pre-hibernation fattening. LB juveniles showed higher torpor use and reached similar body sizes but lower fat content than EB individuals before hibernation. The results suggest that torpor is incompatible with growth but promotes fattening and consolidates pre-hibernation fat depots. In garden dormice, being born late in the reproductive season is associated with a fast life history.

The study also investigated the effects of food availability on the rate of growth and pre-hibernation fattening, use of energy-saving strategies such as daily torpor, body size and body fat content prior to hibernation, overwinter hibernating patterns and mass losses, and fecundity at the following spring of female juvenile dormice. The results showed that food availability did not affect the rate of growth and pre-hibernation fattening, but did affect the use of daily torpor and body fat content prior to hibernation.

The study's findings are in line with previous research on common dormice, which also showed no significant differences in winter survival between EB and LB juveniles. The pre-hibernation fat reserves of LB juveniles in this study were sufficient to fuel their shorter winter duration due to the five-week delay of hibernation start. The results also suggest that an extensive use of torpor is incompatible with structural growth, but supports pre-hibernation fattening late in the season and the preservation of the fat reserves previously deposited prior to hibernation.
The C. elegans neural editome reveals an ADAR target mRNA required for proper chemotaxis. Caenorhabditis elegans lacking ADARs exhibit reduced chemotaxis, but the targets responsible for this phenotype remain unknown. To identify critical neural ADAR targets in C. elegans, an unbiased assessment of the effects of ADR-2, the only A-to-I editing enzyme in C. elegans, on the neural transcriptome was performed. Development and implementation of publicly available software, SAILOR, identified 7361 A-to-I editing events across the neural transcriptome. Intersecting the neural editome with ADR-2 associated gene expression changes revealed an edited mRNA, clec-41, whose neural expression is dependent on deamination. Restoring clec-41 expression in ADR-2 deficient neural cells rescued the chemotaxis defect, providing the first evidence that neuronal phenotypes of ADAR mutants can be caused by altered gene expression. This study provides the first analysis of the tissue-specific role of ADR-2, both in A-to-I editing and in regulating neural gene expression in C. elegans. Combining neural cell isolation with RNA-sequencing and editing site detection with SAILOR, over 7300 A-to-I editing sites in the C. elegans neural transcriptome were identified, including 104 novel edited genes. Previous studies have shown that worms lacking ADR-2 exhibit defects in chemotaxis, yet the underlying mechanism has yet to be determined. To elucidate ADR-2 target mRNAs that are important for proper neural function, the neural editome was queried for genes involved in chemotaxis or locomotion. The results suggest that altered neural gene expression, rather than recoding events, is responsible for the aberrant behavioral phenotypes caused by lack of C. elegans ADR-2. This is the first study to link a phenotype associated with loss of C. elegans ADAR enzymes to editing-dependent regulation of a specific gene.
Influenza A virus (IAV) employs host shutoff as a strategy to repress cellular mRNA translation and facilitate the efficient translation of viral mRNAs. A systematic analysis using RNA-sequencing and ribosome profiling was conducted to elucidate the mechanisms underlying IAV-induced host shutoff. The results show that viral transcripts are not preferentially translated, and instead, the decline in cellular protein synthesis is mediated by viral takeover of the mRNA pool. At 8 hours post-infection (hpi), viral mRNAs take over 53.8% of the translation activity in the cells, while 57.3% of the mRNAs in the cells are viral. This indicates that the relative reduction in cellular mRNA expression is the primary cause of host shutoff during IAV infection.

The analysis also reveals strong variability in the levels of cellular transcript reduction, with short transcripts being less affected by IAV. Notably, these refractory mRNAs are enriched in cell maintenance processes such as oxidative phosphorylation. Furthermore, the study shows that continuous oxidative phosphorylation activity is essential for viral propagation. This suggests that IAV exploits the host cell's oxidative phosphorylation machinery to support its own replication.

The findings of this study contradict previous suggestions that direct co-opting of the translation machinery plays a dominant role in cellular gene shutoff during IAV infection. Instead, the results indicate that viral dominance over the mRNA pool is the primary mechanism underlying host shutoff. This is consistent with the notion that the non-structural protein 1 (NS1) is not required for the shutoff of host cell protein production. The study's systematic and unbiased analysis provides new insights into the mechanisms of IAV-induced host shutoff and highlights the importance of oxidative phosphorylation in viral propagation. Overall, the results advance our understanding of IAV-induced host shutoff and suggest a mechanism that facilitates the translation of genes with essential housekeeping functions.
Biologists have long recognized the importance of gradients of signaling proteins in inducing tissue morphogenesis. However, the mechanisms of gradient formation remain poorly understood. To address this knowledge gap, researchers investigated the distribution of fluorescently-tagged signaling proteins, FGF and FGFR, in Drosophila. The study found that FGF produced in the larval wing imaginal-disc moves to the air-sac-primordium ( ASP ) through FGFR-containing cytonemes that extend from the ASP to contact the wing-disc source. The number of FGF-receiving cytonemes extended by ASP cells decreases gradually with increasing distance from the source, generating a recipient-specific FGF gradient. This gradient acts as a morphogen in the ASP, activating concentration-dependent gene expression and inducing pointed-P1 at higher and cut at lower levels. The transcription-factors Pointed-P1 and Cut antagonize each other and differentially regulate formation of FGFR-containing cytonemes, creating regions with higher-to-lower numbers of FGF-receiving cytonemes. This feedback regulation of cytoneme-mediated transport shapes a tissue-specific FGF morphogen gradient. The study reveals a robust mechanism where morphogens self-generate precise tissue-specific gradient contours through feedback regulation of cytoneme-mediated dispersion. This mechanism provides precision and adaptability in shaping long-range positional gradients and tissue architectures in space and time. The findings have implications for our understanding of tissue morphogenesis and the role of cytonemes in cell-to-cell communication. In Drosophila, the primary signal that guides the branching morphogenesis of tracheal epithelial tubes is the FGF family protein Branchless ( Bnl ). Traditionally, FGF family proteins are thought to function as diffusible signals and form a gradient by passive extracellular dispersion. However, the study suggests that Bnl forms a gradient through cytoneme-mediated transport rather than diffusion. The shape of the Bnl gradient can dynamically adapt to diverse tissue morphologies through the counteracting feedback activities of intracellular transcription factors Cut and PntP1, which regulate where and when signal-specific cytonemes are made as a function of the levels of signal received.
Coiled coils are the most well-understood protein fold due to their unique backbone structure, which can be described by parametric equations. This level of understanding has enabled the manipulation of coiled coils in unprecedented detail. Despite their well-characterized structure, researchers have discovered a new type of fiber formed by inserting two or six residues into the heptad repeat of a parallel, trimeric coiled coil. These insertions strain the supercoil, causing the local formation of short β-strands that move the chain path by 120° around the trimer axis. The result is an α/β coiled coil, which retains only one backbone hydrogen bond per repeat unit from the parent coiled coil. This novel structure is possible within the allowed regions of the Ramachandran space with only minor mutations to a known fold.

α-helical coiled coils are ubiquitous protein domains found in various structural and functional contexts. They were the first protein fold described in atomic detail and are also the only one whose backbone structure can be computed with parametric equations. This has placed them at the forefront of protein design efforts. However, the understanding of insertions of 2 and 6 residues, which should cause the greatest strain on α-helical geometry, was still missing. Researchers have found that these insertions break the α-helices to form short β-strands, which associate into a triangular supersecondary structure called the β-layer.

The β-layer is found repetitively in natural coiled coils, forming regular fibers with alternating α- and β-structure. This protein fold has not been described before. The α/β coiled coil is a new class of protein fiber based on a novel supersecondary structure element. It consists of alternating α- and β-layers, with the first β-layer being part of a 6-residue element (hexad) that occurs after a position g of the preceding heptad. The g position is biased towards a c position, yielding the same g/c position as in the structures T6 and T96. The α/β coiled coil is a substantially novel backbone structure that is possible within the allowed regions of the Ramachandran space with only minor mutations to a known fold.
Epsin, an evolutionarily conserved endocytic clathrin adaptor, plays a critical role in clathrin-mediated endocytosis. To elucidate its function, researchers generated embryonic fibroblasts from conditional epsin triple knockout ( KO ) mice. The results showed that triple KO cells exhibited a dramatic cell division defect and a robust impairment in clathrin-mediated endocytosis, with an accumulation of early and U-shaped pits. This defect was correlated with a perturbation of the coupling between the clathrin coat and the actin cytoskeleton, which was confirmed in a cell-free assay of endocytosis. The study suggests that a key evolutionary conserved function of epsin is to help generate the force that leads to invagination and then fission of clathrin-coated pits. This function is distinct from other roles of epsin, such as its low-affinity interaction with SNAREs. The results indicate that epsin is essential for the transition between shallow/U-shaped and deep invaginated pits during clathrin-mediated endocytosis. The study provides conclusive evidence about the function and sites of action of epsin in endocytosis using a gene KO strategy to completely eliminate all epsins. The findings suggest that epsin plays a critical role in the coupling of the endocytic clathrin coat to actin in cooperation with Hip1R, which helps invaginate the pit. The study also highlights the importance of epsin in the organization of the actin cytoskeleton during clathrin-mediated endocytosis. Overall, the results of this study provide new insights into the mechanisms of clathrin-mediated endocytosis and the role of epsin in this process.
A case-control study was conducted in urban Uganda to investigate the risk factors for asthma among schoolchildren aged 5-17 years. The study included 555 cases of asthma and 1115 controls. Asthma was diagnosed by study clinicians. The results showed that the main risk factors for asthma were higher parental socio-economic status, urbanicity, and area of residence at birth. Children born in small towns or cities had an increased risk of asthma compared to those born in rural areas, with adjusted odds ratios (OR) of 2.16 (95% CI: 1.60-2.92) and 2.79 (95% CI: 1.79-4.35), respectively. Additionally, a parental history of asthma, children's own allergic conditions, atopy, and cooking on gas/electricity were also associated with an increased risk of asthma.

The study found a strong rural-town-city risk gradient for asthma, with the highest risk among children born in small towns and cities. This risk increased further in the presence of other asthma risk factors such as parental history of allergic disease, children's own atopy, higher measures of socio-economic status, and urbanicity. The study also found that even among children with no parental history of allergic disease, being born in a town or city was associated with an increased risk of asthma compared to being born in a rural area.

The study's findings suggest that the risk of asthma among schoolchildren in urban Uganda is strongly predicted by their area of residence in early life, particularly at birth. The study provides the basis for future studies to identify specific environmental and lifestyle factors responsible for increasing asthma risk among children in low- and middle-income countries (LMICs). Given the rapid urbanization in Africa and other LMICs, the prevalence of asthma is likely to increase further, highlighting the need for further research in this area.
Zebrafish pigment pattern formation is a complex process involving interactions between three types of pigment cells: melanophores, xanthophores, and iridophores. Mutations in the leopard (leo) gene, which encodes Connexin 41.8 (Cx41.8), a gap junction subunit, result in a phenotypic series of spotted patterns. A new dominant allele, leotK3, leads to a complete loss of the pattern, suggesting a dominant negative impact on another component of gap junctions. A genetic screen identified Cx39.4 (luchs) as the interacting component. Loss-of-function alleles demonstrate that luchs is required for stripe formation in zebrafish, but the fins are almost unaffected. Double mutants and chimeras show that leo and luchs are only required in xanthophores and melanophores, but not in iridophores, suggesting that both connexins form heteromeric gap junctions. The phenotypes indicate that these channels promote homotypic interactions between melanophores and xanthophores, and those cells instruct the patterning of the iridophores. Fish homozygous for leotK3 or trans-heterozygous for leotK3 over leot1 show an identical phenotype of a completely dissolved pattern. Individual melanophores that hardly cluster together are still present, mostly associated with blue iridophores. Gap junctions are composed of two hemi-channels in adjacent cells, each made of six connexin subunits, which can be identical (homomeric) or different (heteromeric). A heteromeric/heterotypic gap junction is schematically shown, and an alignment of the amino acid sequence from zebrafish Cx41.8 with its human orthologue, GJA5, is provided. The transmembrane regions are shaded in grey, and newly identified point mutations in Connexin 41.8 are highlighted in red. The homozygous leotK3 phenotype is considerably stronger than this, indicating that the mutant Cx41.8 protein present in leotK3 cells has a dominant negative effect on some further component(s) involved in pigment pattern formation. A simple model suggests that this additional factor could be another connexin involved in the formation of heteromeric gap junctions together with Cx41.8. The data suggest that Cx41.8 and Cx39.4 interact to form heteromeric gap junctions and that these channels are responsible for some of the interactions that take place among chromatophores while the pigment pattern is established.
The evolution of insect olfactory receptors has been extensively studied in neopteran insects, but the identity of receptors involved in olfaction in more ancient apterygote and paleopteran insects remains unknown. A recent study investigated the olfactory system of the primary wingless bristletail Lepismachilis y-signata (Archaeognatha), the firebrat Thermobia domestica (Zygentoma), and the neopteran leaf insect Phyllium siccifolium (Phasmatodea). The results suggest that olfactory receptors (ORs) and the olfactory coreceptor (Orco) are lacking in Lepismachilis, but are present in Thermobia and Phyllium. In Thermobia, three Orco candidates were identified, while Phyllium has a fully developed OR/Orco-based system. The study proposes that ORs did not arise as an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle, but evolved later in insect evolution, with Orco being present before the appearance of ORs. The evolution of the coreceptor Orco is thought to have occurred after the bristletails split from their last common ancestor with the remaining insects. However, an olfactory system that relies on both ORs and Orco seems to have evolved after the emergence of wings. Insect olfactory receptors function as heteromultimers composed of at least one ligand-specific OR and the coreceptor Orco. The study suggests that the early evolution of the insect olfactory system can be studied by examining species at crucial positions of the phylogenetic tree with a functional olfactory system adapted to terrestrial conditions and detection of volatile chemicals. The selected species, including Lepismachilis, Thermobia, and Phyllium, provide an excellent model for understanding the evolution of the insect olfactory system. The results of this study contribute to our understanding of the evolution of olfactory receptors in insects and provide insights into the early evolution of the insect olfactory system.
The global malaria burden has decreased over the last decade, with many nations attempting to eliminate the disease. However, asymptomatic malaria infections pose a significant challenge to these efforts, as they often go undiagnosed and untreated. Mass drug administration (MDA) has been proposed as a solution to this problem, but its effectiveness depends on adequate population participation. A recent study conducted in Myanmar investigated the potential herd protection against Plasmodium falciparum infections conferred by MDA. The study analyzed data from four villages undergoing MDA and found that individuals from neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had 2.85 times the odds of having a malaria episode post-MDA compared to those from high adherence neighborhoods, regardless of individual participation. This suggests a herd effect, where the protection provided by MDA extends beyond individual participants to the broader population. The study also found that high mosquito biting rates, living in a house with someone else with malaria, or having an asymptomatic malaria infection were predictors of clinical episodes. Spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA, even in villages with high overall participation, may hinder elimination efforts. The study's findings provide evidence for a herd effect conferred by MDA for P. falciparum malaria, which has not been previously documented. The results suggest that MDA can provide additional levels of population protection beyond individual treatment, making it a valuable tool in the fight against malaria. The study's findings have important implications for further research, intervention, and policy work in malaria control and elimination efforts.
The study investigated the conservation of transcription factor (TF) binding specificities across 600 million years of bilateria evolution by comparing the binding specificities of 242 Drosophila TFs to human and mouse data. The analysis revealed a striking level of conservation of TF binding specificity between Drosophila and mammals, with fruit flies having almost as complex a repertoire of TF binding motifs as humans. This conservation extended to secondary modes of binding and even subtle dinucleotide preferences, suggesting that TF binding specificities are not subject to substantial evolutionary drift. The study used high-throughput SELEX (HT-SELEX) to determine TF binding specificities in Drosophila melanogaster and compared them to human and mouse data. The results showed that orthologous TFs have almost identical core DNA binding profiles, flanking sequences, and secondary modes of binding, even though these species diverged over 600 million years ago. This suggests that TF binding specificities are highly conserved across evolution. The few human TFs with divergent specificities function in cell types not found in fruit flies, indicating that evolution of TF specificities contributes to the emergence of novel types of differentiated cells. The study provides new insights into the evolution of TF DNA-binding characteristics and sheds light on the role of divergence and subtle drift in TF DNA binding specificity in animal diversity.
The study presents a novel approach to recapitulating human cardio-pulmonary co-development using simultaneous multilineage differentiation of human-induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs). A stepwise strategy was employed to direct the induction of both mesoderm-derived cardiac and endoderm-derived lung epithelial lineages within a single differentiation of hiPSCs via temporal-specific tuning of WNT and Nodal signaling in the absence of exogenous growth factors. This approach resulted in the formation of concentric cardio-pulmonary micro-Tissues (μTs) that effectively segregated into separate cardiac and pulmonary μTs upon withdrawal of the WNT agonist.

In 3D suspension culture, the cardio-pulmonary μTs exhibited expedited alveolar maturation in the presence of cardiac accompaniment. The cardiac lineage was found to be an essential cellular niche that promoted effective alveolar maturation. The study demonstrated that upon co-induction of mesoderm and endoderm, a series of shared signaling events drove simultaneous cardiac and pulmonary specification from their respective germ-layer progenitors. The co-induced cardiac and pulmonary progenitors were then transitioned to 3D suspension culture, where they exhibited rapid alveolar maturation within 3 days, supported by the accompanying cardiac lineage.

The cardio-pulmonary μTs were found to segregate into separate cardiac and pulmonary μTs upon withdrawal of the WNT agonist, with concurrent initiation of cardiac contraction. This study provides an experimentally tractable system for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue boundary formation during embryogenesis. The model offers a novel approach for studying the molecular and cellular mechanisms underlying human cardio-pulmonary co-development and tissue boundary formation. Further investigation is required to elucidate the morphological and functional maturation of both cardiac and pulmonary lineages, as well as their crosstalk during this process.
The study reveals an unexpected flexibility in cellular mutation rates in Escherichia coli (E. coli) as a response to changes in selective pressure. Contrary to the general assumption that mutation rates are a slowly-evolving parameter, the research demonstrates that E. coli can rapidly adjust its mutation rate to adapt to stressful environments. Specifically, the study shows that hypermutation, a condition characterized by an increased mutation rate, arises independently in different E. coli cultures when they are exposed to high ethanol stress. Furthermore, the study finds that hypermutator states are transitory and repeatedly alternate with decreases in mutation rate as the cells adapt to the stressful environment.

The study used a defined collection of mutators with distinct mutation rates to identify a range of optimal mutation rates that enable rapid growth under high ethanol stress. Experimental evolution revealed an essential role for hypermutation in de novo adaptation to high ethanol stress. The results show that population mutation rates rise when cells experience higher stress and decline again once cells are adapted to the stress. Interestingly, the study identifies cellular mortality as the major force driving the quick evolution of mutation rates.

The findings provide new insights into the dynamics of mutation rate evolution and help explain the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings, including the emergence of antibiotic resistance in microbes and cancer relapses upon chemotherapy. The study sheds light on how organisms balance robustness and evolvability, suggesting that maintaining high mutation rates is limited in time. The research also highlights the importance of understanding the mechanisms underlying changes in mutation rate, which remains a largely fragmentary area of knowledge. Overall, the study demonstrates that E. coli can rapidly adjust its mutation rate to adapt to stressful environments, providing new perspectives on the evolution of mutation rates in response to selective pressure.
SnRK1-triggered switch of bZIP63 dimerization mediates the low-energy response in plants. The low-energy response is a critical adaptation mechanism that allows organisms to survive under changing environmental conditions by balancing growth and defense responses. The AMPK/Snf1/SnRK1 kinases are evolutionary conserved metabolic master regulators that act at two levels: modulating the activity of key metabolic enzymes and massive transcriptional reprogramming. While the first part is well established, the latter function is only partially understood in animals and not at all in plants. This study identifies the Arabidopsis transcription factor bZIP63 as a key regulator of the starvation response and a direct target of the SnRK1 kinase. Phosphorylation of bZIP63 by SnRK1 changes its dimerization preference, affecting target gene expression and ultimately primary metabolism. A bzip63 knock-out mutant exhibits starvation-related phenotypes, which can be functionally complemented by wild-type bZIP63 but not by a version harboring point mutations in the identified SnRK1 target sites. The SnRK1 kinase AKIN10 is the major kinase responsible for the starvation-induced hyper-phosphorylation of bZIP63. The importance of bZIP63 phosphorylation at the SnRK1 target sites for its in planta function is underlined by complementation of the bzip63 mutant with genomic bZIP63 constructs. Wild-type bZIP63, but not a S29/S294/S300A mutant of bZIP63, can complement the metabolic and senescence phenotypes. The study also shows that bZIP63 is an important metabolic regulator, especially under stress/starvation conditions, and that it is phosphorylated at multiple sites in vivo in a sugar- and energy-dependent manner. SnRK1 targets three highly conserved serine residues in the N- and C-terminus of bZIP63. The phosphorylation of these sites is crucial for bZIP63's dimerization and activity in planta, and a molecular model is proposed for a phosphorylation-triggered switch of bZIP63 dimerization partners, which ultimately regulates metabolic reprogramming.
The study identifies ring finger protein 10 (RNF10) as a novel synaptonuclear messenger that encodes activation of N-methyl-D-aspartate receptors (NMDARs) in the hippocampus. RNF10 is activated by calcium signals at the postsynaptic compartment and elicits discrete changes at the transcriptional level. It is enriched at excitatory synapses, where it associates with the GluN2A subunit of NMDARs. Activation of synaptic GluN2A-containing NMDARs and induction of long-term potentiation (LTP) lead to the translocation of RNF10 from dendritic segments and dendritic spines to the nucleus. This process involves importin-dependent long-distance transport from synapto-dendritic compartments to the nucleus. RNF10 silencing prevents the maintenance of LTP as well as LTP-dependent structural modifications of dendritic spines. RNF10 dissociates from the NMDAR complex in an activity-dependent manner and undergoes importin-dependent long-distance transport to the nucleus. The intracellular C-terminal domain of GluN2A contains target sequences for downstream signaling and scaffolding molecules, including RNF10 and calmodulin. RNF10 represents a novel synaptonuclear protein messenger responsible for long-lasting re-shaping of dendritic spines after specific synaptic stimuli and is required for postsynaptic modifications needed to convey LTP induction. The study suggests that impaired RNF10-mediated synaptonuclear signaling, rather than a reduction of RNF10 synaptic levels, is responsible for the reduction of dendritic spine density observed following RNF10 silencing. The findings indicate that the NMDAR complex is a rich source of protein messengers capable of trafficking to the nucleus and encoding synaptic and extrasynaptic origin of GluN2B NMDAR signals. Activation of synaptic but not extrasynaptic NMDARs leads to an increase in RNF10 nuclear localization. The study describes a novel synapse-to-nucleus signaling pathway that specifically links activation of synaptic GluN2A-containing NMDARs to nuclear gene expression. RNF10 is highly expressed in the nucleus as well as at synapses, where it directly interacts with the cytoplasmic tail of the GluN2A subunit of NMDARs.
Swi2/Snf2 ATPases are a family of enzymes that remodel protein-DNA complexes to regulate chromatin structure and epigenetic state during transcription, DNA replication, and DNA repair. The single-subunit remodeler Mot1, which dissociates TATA box-binding protein (TBP):DNA complexes, has been used as a model system to study the structural mechanisms of Swi2/Snf2 ATPases. The crystal structure of the N-terminal domain of Mot1 in complex with TBP, DNA, and the transcription regulator negative cofactor 2 (NC2) has been determined at 3.8 Å resolution. The structure shows that Mot1 reduces DNA:NC2 interactions and unbends DNA compared to the TBP:DNA:NC2 state, suggesting that Mot1 primes TBP:NC2 displacement in an ATP-independent manner. Electron microscopy and cross-linking data indicate that the Swi2/Snf2 domain of Mot1 associates with the upstream DNA and the histone fold of NC2, revealing parallels to some nucleosome remodelers. This study provides a structural framework for how a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase interacts with its substrate DNA:protein complex. The interface between Mot1 and NC2 is small, but the contact between Mot1's insertion domain and NC2 could help separate NC2's main TBP anchor helix H5 from TBP and H4 from DNA. Swi2/Snf2 enzymes typically remodel protein-DNA complexes by disrupting the interface between the nucleic and protein partners of the target complex. The association of Mot1 with the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex results in some unexpected conformational changes that may have implications for understanding the ATP-dependent dissociation mechanism. The study provides the first pseudoatomic view of a Swi2/Snf2 ATPase in complex with a DNA:protein substrate complex, offering insights into the structural basis for recognition and remodeling of the TBP:DNA:NC2 complex by Mot1.
The article describes the discovery of synthetic variants of the natural product ternatin, a cyclic peptide with cytotoxic and anti-adipogenic properties. Ternatin and its variants were found to be up to 500-fold more potent than the original compound in inhibiting cancer cell growth. Using a photo-affinity probe, the researchers identified the translation elongation factor-1A ternary complex (eEF1A·GTP·aminoacyl-tRNA) as a specific target of ternatin and its variants. This complex plays a crucial role in protein synthesis, and the binding of ternatin to this complex was found to be competitive with other natural products, including didemnin and cytotrienin. Mutations in domain III of eEF1A were shown to prevent ternatin binding and confer resistance to its cytotoxic effects, suggesting that the adjacent hydrophobic surface is a functional hot spot for eEF1A modulation. The study demonstrates that the eukaryotic elongation factor-1A and its ternary complex with GTP and aminoacyl-tRNA are common targets for the evolution of cytotoxic natural products. Ternatin and its variants were found to inhibit cellular protein synthesis, with compound 4 showing the greatest potency. Photo-ternatin crosslinked to purified eEF1A only in the context of its ternary complex with GTP and aminoacylated tRNA, highlighting the specificity of ternatin binding to this complex. Two structurally unrelated natural products, didemnin and cytotrienin, were also found to compete with ternatin for binding to the eEF1A ternary complex. The study suggests that ternatins may be attractive leads for future medicinal chemistry efforts due to their relative structural simplicity and accessibility by total synthesis.
The study investigated the role of OCRL in clathrin-coated pit dynamics and uncoating in Lowe syndrome cells. Lowe syndrome is a rare genetic disorder caused by mutations in the OCRL gene, which encodes an inositol 5-phosphatase that interacts directly with clathrin. Previous studies have primarily attributed the clinical manifestations of Lowe syndrome to intracellular sorting defects. However, this study reveals that OCRL loss in Lowe syndrome patient fibroblasts impairs clathrin-mediated endocytosis and results in an endocytic defect. The cells exhibit an accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles and an increase in U-shaped clathrin-coated pits, which may result from the sequestration of coat components on uncoated vesicles. Endocytic vesicles that fail to lose their coat nucleate the majority of the numerous actin comets present in patient cells. The study found that SNX9, a clathrin-binding protein associated with late-stage endocytic clathrin-coated pits, directly binds OCRL. The absence of OCRL in patient cells delays endocytosis by stalling late-stage pits, impairs uncoating, and triggers the nucleation of comets from clathrin vesicles that retain PI(4,5)P2 and fail to uncoat. These findings indicate that OCRL acts as an uncoating factor and that defects in clathrin-mediated endocytosis likely contribute to pathology in patients with OCRL mutations. The study suggests that OCRL participates in uncoating and facilitates the recycling of endocytic factors, a process required for the normal progression of endocytosis. The presence of numerous actin comets driven by clathrin-positive vesicles in Lowe syndrome fibroblasts adds further support to a close relationship between endocytic clathrin coats and actin.
The Wnt/β-catenin signaling pathway plays a crucial role in colorectal cancer (CRC), with aberrant activation contributing to tumorigenesis. However, identifying effective Wnt inhibitors for cancer treatment remains a significant challenge. Recent research has shed light on the regulation of this pathway, revealing a novel FAK/PYK2/GSK3βY216/β-catenin regulatory circuit in intestinal tumorigenesis. Focal adhesion kinase (FAK) and proline-rich tyrosine kinase 2 (PYK2), elevated in adenomas of APCmin/+ mice and human CRC tissues, function redundantly to promote the Wnt/β-catenin pathway by phosphorylating glycogen synthase kinase 3 beta (GSK3β) at tyrosine 216 (Y216). This phosphorylation event reinforces pathway output, leading to β-catenin accumulation and intestinal tumorigenesis.

Pharmacological inhibition of FAK/PYK2 using PF-562271 suppressed adenoma formation in APCmin/+ mice, accompanied by reduced intestinal levels of phospho-GSK3βY216 and β-catenin. These findings indicate that the FAK/PYK2/GSK3βY216 axis is critical for the activation of Wnt/β-catenin signaling in APC-driven intestinal tumorigenesis. The intestinal level of p-GSK3βY216 was significantly higher in APCmin/+ mice than in control mice, and PF-562271 treatment greatly suppressed GSK3βY216 phosphorylation and GSK3β ubiquitination, further supporting the role of FAK/PYK2 kinase in APC-driven intestinal tumorigenesis.

Loss of functional adenomatous polyposis coli (APC) induces the expression of FAK and PYK2, which in turn phosphorylate GSK3βY216 to stabilize β-catenin, initiating and supporting intestinal tumorigenesis. Phosphorylation of GSK3βY216 was first discovered two decades ago, but the underlying mechanisms remained unclear. The current study demonstrates that FAK and PYK2 function redundantly in the regulation of Wnt/β-catenin signaling in CRC cells, raising the possibility that FAK and PYK2 could also function redundantly in Wnt-driven intestinal tumorigenesis. However, intestine-specific deletion of FAK alone was sufficient to cause complete abrogation of adenoma formation in APC-mutant mice, suggesting that FAK and PYK2 may have distinct roles in intestinal tumorigenesis.
The neural control of transitions between posture and movement involves the regulation of reflex-stabilizing mechanisms to enable motion. To investigate this process, researchers examined the continuity of vestibular control of balance during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion and between two standing postures. Healthy subjects initiated and terminated locomotion or shifted the distribution of their weight between their feet while exposed to electrical vestibular stimuli ( EVS ). The relationship between EVS and ground reaction forces was quantified using time-frequency analyses. Results showed discontinuities corresponding to null coherence periods preceding the onset of movement initiation and during the step preceding locomotion termination. These findings indicate that humans interrupt the vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms to transition between motor states, suggesting a discrete change between motor control policies as predicted by optimal feedback theory. The vestibular system encodes motion of the head in space and is vital to our ability to maintain stability during both quiet standing and locomotion. Vestibular signals influence the activation of muscles engaged in the control of posture. To transition between two motor states, balance correcting responses are disrupted, and the vestibular influence on whole-body balance responses is suspended. This suspension occurs while subjects are still maintaining quiet upright stance, well before the transition onset. The suppression of vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms is not specific to one type of transition, suggesting it represents a general control process that occurs during transitions between standing balance and other motor states. The period of suspension of vestibular-evoked responses appears related to the different relative sensory feedback gains needed to control whole-body movements by the quiet standing and locomotor control policies. The vestibular control of balance during locomotor or posture-to-posture transitions can be monitored by continuously quantifying the magnitude of vestibular-evoked balance responses over the transition. This approach has been used previously to monitor the time course of engagement and disengagement of the vestibular control of balance between self- and externally-driven states of balance control.
In cyanobacteria, the circadian output pathway plays a crucial role in regulating metabolic programs in response to light availability. The transcription factor RpaA is the master regulator of circadian transcription, driving genome-wide oscillations in mRNA abundance. Deletion of rpaA has no effect on viability in constant light conditions, but renders cells inviable in cycling conditions when light and dark periods alternate. This study investigated the mechanisms underlying this viability defect and found that the rpaA- strain cannot maintain appropriate energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes crucial for utilization of carbohydrate stores at night.

The rpaA- strain exhibits defects in cell growth and viability in cyclic, but not in constant light environments, suggesting an important role for circadian regulation of gene expression in alternating light/dark cycles. The phosphorylated form of RpaA, regulated by the circadian clock, is critical for the coordination of carbon anabolism and catabolism and thus cell viability in light/dark cycles. Deletion of rpaA prevents glycogen accumulation in light and renders cells unable to utilize existing glycogen at dusk, leading to alterations in cellular energy charge and a severe reduction in fitness.

Reconstruction of carbon utilization pathways combined with provision of an external carbon source restores energy charge and viability of the rpaA- strain in light/dark cycling conditions. The study highlights how a circadian output pathway controls and temporally coordinates essential pathways in carbon metabolism to maximize fitness of cells facing periodic energy limitations. The results provide insight into the role of the circadian output pathway in enhancing fitness of cyanobacteria through coordination of central carbon metabolism with the metabolic demands imposed by periodic changes in the external environment. The study suggests that functional circadian output mediated by the activity of RpaA is required for the anticipatory accumulation of carbon reserves during the day, as well as metabolic switching to carbon catabolism at dusk. These metabolic adaptations allow cells to stimulate energy production and sustain cell viability during periods of darkness that challenge and constrain resources for cyanobacterial growth.
P-type ATPases, specifically the PIB-4 subclass, play a crucial role in maintaining cellular homeostasis by transporting transition metals across cellular membranes. These proteins are essential for detoxification and redistribution of ions such as zinc, copper, and cobalt. However, the structure and ion-release mechanism of PIB-4-ATPases have remained elusive due to the lack of available structural information. To address this knowledge gap, researchers have determined the structure of an archetypal PIB-4-ATPase, sCoaT from Sulfitobacter sp. NAS14-1, and conducted complementary functional analyses. The results reveal that PIB-4-ATPases lack classical heavy-metal-binding domains ( HMBDs ) and display an eight-helix M-domain configuration. This topology is distinct from other P-type ATPases and provides new insights into the mechanism and diversity of heavy-metal transporters. Furthermore, the study demonstrates that PIB-4-ATPases have a dual role in heavy-metal release and as an internal counter ion of an invariant histidine. Additionally, the researchers found that the turnover of PIB-ATPases is potassium-independent, contrasting with many other P-type ATPases. These findings have significant implications for drug discovery and the development of tools to manipulate heavy-metal accumulation in plants. The study also highlights the potential of PIB-4-ATPases as virulence factors in many pathogens. The metal specificity of PIB-4-ATPases remains unclear, with some members exhibiting cobalt specificity while others transport a broader range of ions, including zinc, nickel, copper, and calcium. The results of this study provide a foundation for further research into the structure and function of PIB-4-ATPases and their role in heavy-metal homeostasis.
In mammals, numerous autosomal genes exhibit mitotically stable monoallelic expression ( MAE ), a phenomenon where one allele of a gene is expressed while the other is silenced. Despite its significance in various human diseases, the regulation of MAE has been challenging to study due to its clonal nature. Researchers have now identified a chromatin signature that distinguishes MAE genes in human cells with high accuracy. This signature is characterized by the simultaneous presence of chromatin marks associated with active transcription ( H3K36me3 ) and silencing ( H3K27me3 ) in the gene body. The MAE signature is found in approximately 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and over 30% of tissue-specific genes across different cell types. Notably, it is enriched among key developmental genes that have bivalent chromatin structure in pluripotent cells. This discovery opens up a new approach to studying MAE that is independent of polymorphisms and suggests a link between MAE and cell differentiation. The MAE signature was detected in a variety of cell types, with up to 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and more than one-third of tissue-specific genes showing the chromatin signature of MAE. This approach does not rely on single nucleotide polymorphisms ( SNPs ) or sample clonality, making it feasible to analyze MAE in primary samples, including comparisons of normal and diseased tissue. The genes with the MAE chromatin signature share some pronounced features as a group. Importantly, the MAE identified in clones is reflected by the chromatin signature in a polyclonal sample. A large fraction of known bivalent genes acquire the MAE chromatin signature in at least one of the differentiated cell types, much higher than other genes that are also silent in embryonic stem cells. These master regulator genes preferentially resolve into a state with the characteristic MAE chromatin signature upon activation.
The study reveals a novel mechanism of activity control for the AAA+ chaperone ClpC in Gram-positive bacteria. ClpC forms large two-helical assemblies through head-to-head contacts between coiled-coil middle domains ( MDs ), which sequester ClpC molecules in an inactive resting state. This resting state restricts substrate binding and ATP hydrolysis.
