The adaptor protein MecA activates ClpC by binding to MD interaction sites, converting the resting state to an active planar ring structure. ClpC is essential for bacterial virulence and stress resistance as part of the ClpCP protease complex. The study shows that loss of ClpC repression in MD mutants causes constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity. The findings suggest that coiled-coil MDs are regulatory devices controlling AAA+ protein activity. This mechanism is distinct from other AAA+ chaperones, such as ClpB/Hsp104, where coiled-coil MDs form a repressing belt around the AAA ring to reduce ATPase activity. Instead, ClpC MDs form intermolecular head-to-head contacts allowing docking of two layers of ClpC molecules in a helical conformation. The formation of this structure involves ATP binding to AAA domains and restricts substrate binding and ATP hydrolysis. Mutating a key regulatory MD residue of ClpE also causes cellular toxicity, suggesting that the repressing mode of MDs is a general mechanism for controlling bacterial AAA+ chaperone systems. Severe toxicity of ClpC and ClpE MD mutants qualifies these AAA+ chaperones as targets for antimicrobials. The study provides new insights into the regulation of AAA+ chaperones and highlights the importance of coiled-coil MDs in controlling their activity.
In Drosophila, adult muscle precursors (AMPs) are a type of muscle stem cell that emerges during mid-embryogenesis and remains dormant throughout embryonic and most of larval life. AMPs express muscle progenitor-specific markers, such as the b-HLH transcription factor Twist, and can proliferate to provide a source of myoblasts for adult muscle growth and the regeneration of a subset of thoracic flight muscles. However, the mechanisms that keep AMPs in a non-differentiated quiescent state and how they are reactivated remain poorly understood. To address this, researchers used a Drosophila model to investigate the behavior of AMPs in vivo. They found that emerging AMPs send out thin filopodia that make contact with neighboring muscles, suggesting that muscles could play a role in AMP reactivation. Genetic analyses revealed that muscles send inductive dIlp6 signals that switch the Insulin pathway ON in closely associated AMPs, leading to the activation of Notch, which regulates AMP proliferation via dMyc. The data suggest that Drosophila AMPs display homing behavior to muscle niche and that the niche-driven Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade plays a key role in setting the activated state of AMPs. Furthermore, the researchers found that dMyc acts as a positive regulator of AMP reactivation and that the proliferation of muscle stem cells is regulated by the same set of genes in both Drosophila and vertebrates. The study provides evidence that muscle behaves as an AMP niche and plays a driving role in AMP reactivation in later larval life. The Insulin-Notch-dMyc cascade was found to be essential for AMP reactivation and proliferation, and genetic rescue experiments showed that Notch acts downstream of the Insulin pathway and dMyc functions downstream of Notch. Overall, the study reveals new features of Drosophila AMPs, including their direct contact with muscles and the role of the muscle niche in AMP reactivation.
Substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr) neurons are a type of GABAergic neuron that fire spontaneously at high rates, providing tonic inhibition to downstream brain areas. A depolarizing 'leak' current supports this firing pattern, but the molecular basis of this current has remained poorly understood. To identify the molecular basis of this current, researchers used single-cell RNA sequencing to determine the transcriptome of individual mouse SNr neurons. The study found that SNr neurons express the sodium leak channel, NALCN, and that SNr neurons lacking NALCN have impaired spontaneous firing. NALCN is also involved in the modulation of excitability by changes in glycolysis and by activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors. The findings suggest that disruption of NALCN could impair the basal ganglia circuit, which may underlie the severe motor deficits in humans carrying mutations in NALCN. The researchers used whole transcriptome sequencing of SNr neurons and detected expression of several nonselective cation channels (NSCCs) that could potentially generate the tonic depolarizing current. NALCN stood out as a prime candidate for the channel carrying the depolarizing leak current in these cells, as it had the highest expression of all NSCCs and its expression was in the top 20% of all ion channels. The study also found that SNr neurons express Unc80, Unc79, and Fam155a, three genes whose protein products are known to interact with NALCN. These findings suggest that NALCN plays a crucial role in the maintenance of tonic activity in SNr neurons and that disruption of NALCN could contribute to basal ganglia-related motor disorders. The study highlights the importance of NALCN in the regulation of SNr neuron excitability and its potential role in the pathophysiology of motor disorders.
Scientists have long relied on antibiotics derived from a limited number of culturable microorganisms to combat bacterial infections. However, the emergence of antibiotic resistance has rendered these compounds increasingly ineffective, while the vast majority of microbial diversity remains inaccessible for cultivation and exploration. To address this challenge, researchers have developed a novel droplet-based microscale cultivation system that combines high-throughput screening for antimicrobial activity with in-droplet cultivation of single cells. This innovative approach enables the cultivation of previously uncultured species and significantly increases bacterial diversity compared to traditional agar plate cultivation. The system consists of a modular droplet-microfluidic platform that mimics environmental conditions in situ, allowing for the cultivation of 2 million compartmentalized single cells in a total volume of 1. 5 mL. Each droplet has a miniaturized volume of 200 pL, facilitating high-density long-term cultivation. The system is directly coupled to a high-throughput screening for antimicrobial activity, which involves the addition of reporter strains to the cultivation compartments. This allows for the detection and selection of droplets containing environmental cells producing compounds with antibiotic activity. The researchers demonstrated the effectiveness of this approach by culturing a complex bacterial community derived from a soil sample and comparing the results to classical agar plate cultivation. One isolate that showed antimicrobial activity in the droplet screening was selected for further analysis, and the secreted metabolites were identified and characterized. The combination of bacterial strain discovery during the in-droplet cultivation phase with subsequent selection of the most auspicious candidates for natural product biosynthesis through a highly robust reporter assay streamlines efforts towards new scaffold structures for the development of antibiotics. Even though axenic cultures of putative new species could not be maintained with standard microbiological cultivation after isolation from droplets, the cells could be used for obtaining high-quality genome information.
A Bayesian analysis was conducted to investigate the association between the leukotriene A4 hydrolase (LTA4H) genotype and survival in patients with tuberculous meningitis (TBM). Previous studies have shown that adjunctive anti-inflammatory corticosteroids reduce mortality from TBM by approximately 30%, suggesting that inflammatory pathophysiology contributes to only a subset of deaths. The LTA4H gene encodes an enzyme that regulates inflammatory eicosanoids, and a C/T promoter variant has been linked to increased survival in patients with TBM. However, a study in Indonesia did not find a survival benefit associated with the LTA4H TT genotype. Using Bayesian methods to analyze both studies, the authors found that the LTA4H TT genotype confers a survival benefit that begins early and continues long-term in both populations. This benefit is nullified in the most severe cases with high early mortality. The results suggest that LTA4H genotyping, combined with disease severity assessment, may be used to target glucocorticoid therapy to patients most likely to benefit from it. The authors propose that personalized genotype-directed adjunctive glucocorticoid treatment may be warranted, with the drug given only to TT patients. They also suggest that the lack of an LTA4H genotype association with survival in the Indonesia cohort may be due to the underlying association being overridden by severe disease, or that the beneficial effects of dexamethasone for the LTA4H TT genotype are nullified in more severe disease.
Biomedical research has long sought to understand how growth cones navigate axonal projection in response to guidance cues. A recent study has shed light on this process by investigating the role of shootin1 in mediating netrin-1–induced axon guidance. The study found that knockout mice of shootin1 display abnormal projection of forebrain commissural axons, a phenotype similar to that of netrin-1 knockout mice. This suggests that shootin1 plays a crucial role in axon guidance, particularly in response to netrin-1 gradients.

    The study demonstrated that shallow gradients of netrin-1 elicit highly polarized Pak1-mediated phosphorylation of shootin1 within growth cones. This phosphorylation increases shootin1 interaction with the cell adhesion molecule L1-CAM, promoting F-actin–adhesion coupling and concomitant generation of forces for growth cone migration. Furthermore, the spatially regulated shootin1 phosphorylation within growth cones is required for axon turning induced by netrin-1 gradients.

    The study also showed that shootin1a is expressed at high levels in developing forebrain commissural axons and that Shootin1 knockout mice display abnormal guidance of these axons. Notably, very small spatial differences in netrin-1 concentration elicited highly polarized directional phosphorylation of shootin1a within growth cones. Netrin-1–elicited shootin1a phosphorylation promoted direct interaction between shootin1a and L1-CAM, thereby generating traction force for growth cone motility.

    The study further demonstrated that disturbance of the spatially regulated shootin1a phosphorylation within growth cones inhibited axon turning, but not axon outgrowth, induced by netrin-1 gradients. Additionally, uncoupling of F-actin-adhesion coupling by shootin1a ( 1-125 ) inhibited netrin-1–induced axon outgrowth and turning on laminin. These findings indicate that netrin-1–induced axon guidance, mediated by the shootin1a–L1-CAM interaction, is not limited to growth cones on L1-CAM.

    Overall, the study has identified a mechano-effector for netrin-1 signaling and demonstrated that shootin1 phosphorylation is a critical readout for netrin-1 gradients that results in a directional mechanoresponse for axon guidance. The findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying netrin-1–regulated axon guidance and highlight the importance of shootin1 in this process.
The study of hummingbirds’ unique ability to produce a distinct humming sound while in flight has long been a topic of interest in the scientific community. To elucidate the biophysical origin of this phenomenon, researchers employed a novel approach combining acoustic nearfield holography and direct measurement of 3D aerodynamic forces using an aerodynamic force platform. The study involved six freely hovering Anna’s hummingbirds, which were equipped with a 2176-microphone array to record their acoustic nearfield and a force platform to measure the aerodynamic forces generated by their flapping wings.

    The researchers developed an idealized acoustic model that integrated the aerodynamic forces with wing kinematics, demonstrating how the timbre of the hummingbird’s hum arises from the oscillating lift and drag forces on each wing. The study found that the characteristic humming timbre and radiated power of flapping wings in both birds and insects originate from the higher harmonics in the aerodynamic forces that support their body weight. Furthermore, the model analysis revealed that allometric deviation makes larger birds quieter and elongated flies louder.

    The researchers employed a new aerodynamic force platform to directly measure the net 3D aerodynamic force generated by freely hovering hummingbirds, which was then integrated into a new aeroacoustics model to predict the sound radiated due to the oscillating forces from flapping wings. The measurements and model showed that hovering hummingbirds generate a highly directional hum, with the broadband acoustic pressure primarily oriented downward opposing gravity. The acoustic directivity and orientation of the harmonic components depend on harmonic parity.

    The study also compared the predicted acoustic field with novel acoustic nearfield recordings for six freely hovering hummingbirds, which corroborated the predictive power of the minimal model. Numerical simulation of the Ffowcs Williams and Hawkings aeroacoustic equation showed that the farfield hum of flapping mosquito wings is primarily driven by aerodynamic force fluctuation. The findings suggest that oscillating aerodynamic forces play a crucial role in generating the wing hum of both birds and insects, providing new insights into the complex bioacoustic behavior of these animals.
Title: Contingency and Chance Erase Necessity in the Experimental Evolution of Ancestral Proteins

    A recent study investigated the roles of chance, contingency, and necessity in the evolution of the B-cell lymphoma-2 (BCL-2) family of proteins. By combining ancestral protein reconstruction and a novel continuous evolution technology, the researchers were able to mutate and select proteins to acquire specific protein-protein interaction (PPI) specificities that occurred during animal evolution. The study found that over long historical timescales, contingency generated by sequence change steadily erased necessity and overwhelmed chance as the primary cause of acquired sequence variation.

    The researchers replicated evolutionary trajectories from multiple ancestral proteins and found that trajectories launched from phylogenetically distant proteins yielded virtually no common mutations, even under strong and identical selection pressures. This suggests that chance arose because many sets of mutations could alter specificity at any timepoint, while contingency arose because historical substitutions changed these sets. The results indicate that patterns of variation in BCL-2 sequences, and likely other proteins as well, are idiosyncratic products of a particular and unpredictable course of historical events.

    The study's findings suggest that present-day BCL-2 family proteins are largely physical anecdotes of their particular unpredictable histories, with their sequences reflecting the interaction of accumulated chance events during descent from common ancestors with necessity imposed by physics, chemistry, and natural selection. The results also indicate that contingency generated by sequence change over phylogenetic timescales plays a profound role in BCL-2 family protein sequence evolution under laboratory selection for new functions.

    The study used human BID and NOXA as fixed binding partners, but in real evolution, these proteins would have varied in sequence as well, introducing opportunities for chance and contingency to further affect the sequence outcomes of BCL-2 evolution. However, some differences between the study's design and the biological setting of historical BCL-2 family evolution could have overestimated chance's historical role.

    The results indicate that the paths through sequence space leading to historical PPI specificities changed repeatedly during the BCL-2 family's history, even during intervals when the proteins' PPI binding profiles did not evolve. This suggests that for chance to strongly influence the outcomes of adaptive evolution, multiple paths to a selected phenotype must be accessible with similar probabilities of being taken.
A genome-wide haplotype-based analysis was conducted on a collection of 48 sub-Saharan African groups to characterize the structure of genetic diversity and gene-flow in the region. The study revealed that coastal populations experienced an influx of Eurasian haplotypes over the last 7000 years, and that Eastern and Southern Niger-Congo speaking groups share ancestry with Central West Africans as a result of recent population expansions. Furthermore, most sub-Saharan populations were found to share ancestry with groups from outside of their current geographic region as a result of gene-flow within the last 4000 years. These findings provide insight into haplotype sharing across different ethno-linguistic groups and the recent movement of alleles into new environments, both of which are relevant to studies of genetic epidemiology. The study also identified clear evidence for the movement of Eurasian ancestry back into the continent as a result of admixture over various timescales. This is supported by previous studies such as the African Genome Variation Project, which described the influence of both Eurasian and hunter-gatherer ancestry on the population genetic history of sub-Saharan Africa. However, questions remain regarding the extent and timing of key events and their impact on contemporary populations. At finer scales, the analysis revealed smaller groups and often differentiated closely related populations consistent with self-reported ancestry. Gene-flow between populations was measured and related to potential historical movements of people into and within sub-Saharan Africa. Admixture is increasingly regarded as a common feature of human populations across the globe, and genome-wide analyses of African populations are refining previous models of the continent’s history and its impact on genetic diversity. The study provides a quantitative measure of the coancestry between groups and identifies the dominant events that have shaped current genetic diversity in sub-Saharan Africa.
During Xenopus laevis embryogenesis, the mitotic spindle adapts to changing cell dimensions to ensure proper cell division. To investigate this process, an in vitro system was established using cytoplasmic extracts from embryos that recapitulates in vivo spindle size differences between stage 3 ( 4 cells, 37 µm ) and stage 8 ( ∼4000 cells, 18 µm ). The kinesin-13 kif2a was identified as a driver of developmental spindle scaling, whose microtubule-destabilizing activity is inhibited in stage 3 spindles by the transport receptor importin α and activated in stage 8 when importin α partitions to a membrane pool. This ratiometric mechanism controls microtubule destabilization and couples spindle size to cell size during Xenopus development. Altering spindle size in developing embryos impaired spindle orientation during metaphase, but chromosome segregation remained robust. Reconstituting spindle formation in embryo extracts enabled the elucidation of a mechanism that coordinates spindle size with cell size through differential activation of the kinesin-13 kif2a. This mechanism is consistent with the gradual change from meiotic to mitotic spindle morphology observed during early mouse development and suggests that physical changes in embryonic cell size can impact biochemical reactions that alter spindle architecture. Importin α phosphorylation is thought to liberate it from membrane stores into the cytoplasm, thereby promoting its transport activity. Kif2a was found to retrieve ∼10 times more importin α from stage 3 extracts than from stage 8 extracts, suggesting that the amount of importin α partitioning to membrane is sufficient to alter its interaction with kif2a. The identification of a mechanism contributing to spindle size control in embryos enabled the testing of its functional significance during development, revealing an important role for spindle size in maintaining spindle orientation in the large cells of early Xenopus development. This work suggests that the changing physical features of the cell can drive intracellular scaling through biochemical partitioning and steady-state redistribution of activities.
The discovery of tandem riboswitch aptamers in bacteria has revealed a natural Boolean logic gate that controls purine metabolism. Riboswitch aptamers are RNA molecules that bind to specific ligands to regulate gene expression. In this study, researchers identified riboswitch aptamers for phosphoribosyl pyrophosphate ( PRPP ) that exist in either singlet or tandem arrangements with guanine aptamers. The tandem guanine-PRPP aptamers can bind their target ligands independently or in combination to approximate the function of an IMPLY Boolean logic gate. This gate regulates the transcription of messenger RNAs for de novo purine biosynthesis in bacteria. The existence of these sophisticated all-RNA regulatory systems supports the hypothesis that RNA World organisms could have managed complex metabolic states without protein regulatory factors.

    The researchers used in-line probing experiments to examine the aptamer regions of the RNA and found that the logic gate function is accomplished through interactions between the two aptamers and the adjoining expression platform. The terminator and antiterminator stems are stabilized or destabilized depending on the relative concentrations of both guanine and PRPP. This is the first example of two tandem aptamers with different ligand specificities that share the same expression platform, allowing for a new type of logic function to be implemented.

    Tandem riboswitch systems have been previously described, but the guanine-PRPP system is unique in its ability to implement a two-input Boolean logic gate. The discovery of this system demonstrates the complexity of chemical sensing and regulatory functions in ancient RNA World organisms. It is likely that additional variations of two-input Boolean logic gates will be represented by tandem riboswitches that have yet to be discovered in modern cells.

    The researchers suggest that RNA aptamers that selectively bound PRPP in ancient times might have persisted to serve as riboswitch aptamers in modern cells. The discovery of tandem guanine-PRPP riboswitches serves as an additional demonstration of how complex molecular devices can be formed purely from RNA. The in vitro transcription data for the logic gate molecules represent the combined output of a population of RNA transcripts.
The number of neurotransmitter-filled vesicles released into the synaptic cleft with each action potential plays a crucial role in determining the reliability of synaptic transmission. The variability in this fundamental property contributes to the diversity of synaptic function across different brain regions. However, the source of this variability remains unclear. The prevailing view suggests that the release of a single vesicle ( univesicular release, UVR ) or multiple vesicles ( multivesicular release, MVR ) is determined by the probability of vesicle release, which is influenced by calcium levels. However, a recent study using mouse brain slices has demonstrated that the number of vesicles released is regulated by the size of the readily-releasable pool ( RRP ), which is upstream of vesicle release probability. The RRP is controlled by protein kinase A ( PKA ) and its vesicle-associated target, synapsin, which dynamically regulate release site occupancy to dictate the number of vesicles released without altering release probability. These findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms that control MVR and contribute to the functional diversity of synaptic signaling. The study suggests that the size of the RRP is a key determinant of the number of vesicles released, and that PKA and synapsin play a crucial role in regulating this process. The results have important implications for our understanding of synaptic function and the development of unifying models of presynaptic function that account for multiple presynaptic parameters. The study also highlights the need to consider the RRP as a fundamental synaptic parameter that contributes to the variability in vesicle release mode. The findings are consistent with recent release models that propose that vesicles transition between sequential states, from loose to tighter tethering with release machinery followed by calcium-triggered fusion. The study provides new evidence that the RRP is a functional measure of the number of release-competent vesicles per active zone, which is under the control of synapsins and constrains the number of vesicles released. The results suggest that the size of the RRP is a key determinant of the number of vesicles released, and that PKA and synapsin play a crucial role in regulating this process. The study highlights the importance of considering the RRP as a fundamental synaptic parameter that contributes to the variability in vesicle release mode. HIV-1 Gag is responsible for the selective packaging of the viral RNA genome in the context of a large excess of cytosolic human RNAs. To investigate this process, researchers used a minimal system consisting of purified RNAs, recombinant HIV-1 Gag, and giant unilamellar vesicles (GUVs) to recapitulate the selective packaging of the 5' untranslated region (UTR) of the HIV-1 genome in the presence of excess competitor RNA. The results showed that Gag selectively packages the HIV-1 5' UTR at subnanomolar concentrations in the presence of excess competitor RNA.

The study found that mutations in the CA-CTD domain of Gag, which subtly affect its self-assembly on membranes, abrogate RNA selectivity. Additionally, it was observed that tRNA suppresses Gag membrane binding less when Gag has bound viral RNA. These findings suggest that the ability of HIV-1 Gag to selectively package its RNA genome and its self-assembly on membranes are interdependent on one another.

The researchers propose a model for HIV-1 genome selection where the NC domain of Gag is necessary but not sufficient for the selective packaging of the HIV-1 genomic RNA. According to this model, a viral RNA with several high-affinity binding sites for Gag can recruit several Gag molecules to the membrane if only one of them dissociates from its membrane-occluding tRNA. This model is supported by the observation that the large majority of plasma membrane-proximal genomic HIV-1 RNAs are monomeric in the absence of HIV-1 Gag, but coalesce to dimers on the membrane in the presence of Gag.

Furthermore, the study highlights the importance of membrane-localized Gag assembly events in HIV-1 genome selection and packaging. The results suggest that lattice contacts conferring Gag ability to multimerize on a membrane are crucial for the selective packaging of viral RNA. The 5' UTR is recruited to membranes by multimerized Gag without any gross changes to its secondary structure, and the association of Gag with tRNA serves to inhibit genome-less virus assembly.
